THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: THIRD PERIOD)
Air Navigation (Charges) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 123 of 1981)An Act to amend the Air Navigation (Charges) Act 1952, and for related purposes.
Apple and Pear Export Charge Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 147 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Export Ch~rge Act 1976.
Apple and Pear Levy Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 1460f 1981)- An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Levy Act 1976.
Appropriation Act (No.1) 1981-82 (Act No. 1640f 1981)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1982.
Appropriation Act (No.2) 1981-82 (Act No. 165 of 1981)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for certain expenditure in respect of the year endingon 30June 1981.
Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 145 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Act 1973.
Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 124 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Cheques) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 126 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Cheques) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 125 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 130 of 1981)- An Act to amend section 6 of the Australian Capital Territory Tax (lnsurance Bus;ness) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Life Insurance Business) Act 1981 (Act No. 128 of 1981)An Act relating to the imposition of tax in respect of certain life insurance business having a connection with the Australian Capital Territory.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Act 1981 (Act No. 129 of 1981)An Act relating to the imposition of tax in respect of the registration of certain vehicles in the Australian Capital Territory.
Australian CapitalTerritory Taxation (Administration) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 127 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Act 1969.
Australian Meat and Live-stock Corporation Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 150 of 1981)- An Act to amend section 50 of the Austrillian Meat and Live-stock Corporation Act 1977.
Australian National University Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 1660f 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Natiollal University Act 1946.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1981 (Act No. 168 of 1981)An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964, and for related purposes.
Census and Statistics Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 177 of 1981)An Act to amend the Census and Statistics Act 1905, and for related purposes.
Commerce (Trade Descriptions) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 151 of 1981)An Act to amend the Commerce (Trade Descriptions) Act 1905 in relation to the exportation of food.
Commonwealth Grants Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 173 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Grants Commission Act 1973.
Companies and Securities Legislation (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1981 (Act No. 153 of 1981)- An Act to amend laws relating to companies and securities and for related purposes.
Continental Shelf (Living Natural Resources) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 182 of 1981)An Act to amend the Continental Shelf(Living Natural Resources) Act 1968 to facilitate the collection of levy imposed by the Foreign Fishing Boats Levy Act 1981 and of amounts payable under certain agreements, and for certain other purposes.
Crimes (Currency) Aat 1981 (Act No. 1220f 1981)An Act to make provision with respect to offences connected with the counterfeiting of money or certain kinds of securities and other activities injurious to Australian currency, and for related purposes.
Customs (Unlawful Exportation of Food) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 152 of 1981)- An Act ~to amend the Cllstoms Act 1901 in relation to the exportation of food.

PETITIONS The Clerk-Petitions have been lodged for presentation as follows and copies will be referred to the appropriate Ministers:
Diabetics: Insulin
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled: The petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That in view, of the recent standardisation of insulin and insulin syringes, having the effect of increasing the cost of both items to the Diabetic in general, and as every insulindependent Diabetic must use the same, daily or twice daily according to medication.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that syringes be made available on the NHS and that insulin prescriptions revert to 10 bOllles as previously supplied.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Charles, Mrs Child, Mr Duffy, Mr Mildren, Mr Milton, Dr Theophanous and Mr Willis.
Petitions received.
Sales Tax on Publications
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That the Government's decision to impose a tax on books, magazines and newspapers particularly educational publications and books of a religious nature, is abhorrent to the notion of the right to in,formation, a clear infringement of our democratic freedom, aild'that no such tax is levied on any other country with which Australia is usually compared.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Government will withdraw its announced intention to impose a sales tax on books, magazines and newspapers.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Adermann.

Rights of Disabled
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
I. The failure of the Federal Government to seek information from the censlis re disabled people-i.e. partial or total disability-to enable for adequate planning of, and fo'r, types of services needed by disabled people to be undertaken.
2. The failure of the Federal Government to lead the way in employment of disabled people by employing a greater number of such people within the Commonwealth Public

2566 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Petitions

Service - note the small number currently employed within the Service.
3. The wastage of the Federal Government funds perpetuating large, bureaucratic institutions and organisations where there are often few disabled people on their boards and little emphasis is placed on consumer participation in decision making.
4. The failure of the Federal Government to encourage independence of disabled people, or to develop 'normalised' support systems-attendant care.
5. The failure of the Federal Government to provide 'noninstitutionalised' specialised accommodation for those people who would not be able to benefit from, or cope with attendant care.
6. Failure by the Federal Government to alter, or create a 'disability' allowance means test free for all disabled peoplethis would assist with necessary equipment and mobility costs.
7. Failure to provide a national compensation scheme thus saving time and enabling people to be automatically covered in the event oFa disability.
8. Failure by the Federal Government to abolish tariffs on all equipment available to the disabled-e.g. Optacon.
9. Mismanagement of Federal LY.D.P. Funds-e.g. nonreceipt by some States of Funds through unresolved negotiations.
Your Petitioners therefore humbly pray that immediate acton be taken to remedy the above stated and that the Federal Government recogni~e that disabled people are citizens who should have equal rights.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Free and Mr Mountford.

That the plan by the Australian Postal Commission to downgrade postal and mail services throughout-Australia will place in jeopardy the existing services and the security of the mails by:

Aboriginal Land Rights
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:

2568 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Petitions

That the projected repeal by the Queensland State Government of the Aborigines Act 1971-79 and the Torres Strait Islander Act 1971-79 will almost certainly lead to the degazettal of all Aboriginal reserves in Queensland. as has already been the case with Aurukun and Mornington Island.
That if. as is likely. the Aboriginal communities from these reserves are placed under similar shire council legislation on firty year leases. they will be exposed to serious encroachment on their land by mining and tourist interests.
That it is also quite clear. on past evidence. that these interests will have the full backing of the Queensland Government and the rights of Aboriginal people to land rights and self-management will not be granted by that Government.
That the Commonwealth Government has the power to legislate and acquire land for Aboriginal people under its constitutional powers since the amendments to the Constitution in 1967. but it is your petitioners' sincere belief that it failed to act in 1978 and thus. failed in its duty before the people of Aurukun and Mornington Island.
That the Aboriginal people of Queensland have stated that they want land rights and self-management.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Commonwealth Government make use of its constitutional and legislative powers to grant full land rights and self-management to the Aboriginal people of Queensland.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Dawkins.

To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
I. All children in Tasmania are not treated equally.
2. Persons with intellectual. physical and mUltiple handicaps in particular will be denied access to Aspect House, 6 Warragul Avenue. New Town in Tasmania if the house is closed.
3. Aspect House is faced with imminent closure because of lack of Government policy and on-going funding.
4. Aspect House is the only community-based facility in an ordinary house in an ordinary suburban setting which provides relief to parents and families engaged in the onerous and demanding task of rearing their handicapped children in the community.

Staging of American B52 Bombers
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled:
The petition of the undersigned in New South Wales respectfully showeth: that the proposed use of Darwin Airport as a refuelling and Rest and Recreation base of American B52 Bombers:

2570 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Petitions

Jeopardises the welfare of the citizens of Darwin and of all Australians, in that it establishes a stronger potential of Darwin being a nuclear target in any conflict between the USA. and another power.
Impedes the sovereignty of the Australian' nation in that Australian citizens would have neither control nor knowledge of, \".hether B52's:
(a) Carried nuclear weapons. (b) Were engaged in bombing missions against another
State.
Violates the Australian people's independence and links this nation irreconcilably with United States foreign policies thereby destroying the potential for independent Australian defence policy.
Violates democratic procedures in committing the Australian nation to participation in future wars without due consultation and informed assessment by Australian citizens and further increases the militarisation in the Indian Ocean thereby increasing the likelihood of super power conflict in this region.
Jeopardises the welfare of the Citizens of Darwin, in the noise pollution and the threat of accidental radioactive fallout will increase substantially to the residents.
Negates the potential of the Australian nation to establish itself as a nation of Peace in the Pacific region and violates Australia's commitment to the Nuclear Non-Proliferation Treaty.
Your Petitioners therefore pray that the Members of the House of Representatives will ensure that the Prime Minister withdraw the offer of Darwin, or any other location in Australia, as a base or depot for American B52 bombers. And that furthermore will ensure that any request of such usage by the US. Government be rejected.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Lionel Bowen.

Telephone Exchange at Mount Mee
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled.
The humble petition of undersigned citizens of Mount Mee respectfully showeth that the Telephone communication service of Mount Mee is in need of immediate attention. Problems associated with the telephones in the area include poor reception, continual ringing of unused telephones, no dial tones available when dialling, engaged signal when dialling into an open line, and crossed lines making private calls impossible. Due to this unsatisfactory service businesses are also suffering from the lack of communication.
Perhaps the most serious problem associated with the telephone service in the area is when emergency services (Doctors, Hospitals etc.) are required the telephone is often the only form of communication available to the residents of Mount Mee and our Petitioners therefore humbly pray that the persons responsible may consider immediate attention be given to the installation of a new Telephone Exchange which could accommodate the needs of the community and your petitioners, as in duty bound, will ever pray.
by Mr Adermann.

IRAN: RELIGIOUS PERSECUTION
Mr CARLTON-Is the Minister for Foreign Affairs aware of reports that innocent citizens exercising their right to protest against the Islamic regime in Iran are being executed without benefit of trial? Is the Minister aware of continuing reports of persecution of members of the Baha'i faith in Iran? If so, what is the Government's position on these matters?
Mr STREET-Since the dismissal of the former President of Iran, Bani Sadr, on 22 June this year, reports available to the Government indicate that almost 2,000 people have been executed in Iran, on the orders of the revolutionary court, for political offences. It has been reported that large numbers of people, including school children, have been summarily shot during or immediately after street demonstrations. These dead are reported to include not only political activists but also innocent bystanders and, as the honourable gentleman has said, members of the already much-persecuted religious minorities. Although the Government cannot confirm all individual reported cases it does seem that the reports that we have had are basically accurate.
It has become increasingly evident also that there has been an intensification at the highest level of the persecution of Baha'is by the Iranian authorities even though those authorities have stated that the charges against Baha'is are not related to their religion. The Supreme Court of Iran has confirmed sentences in which the faith of the Baha'is has been expressly cited. In the Yezd region of Iran in August, 150 heads of families of the Baha'i sect were arrested and their fate is still not decided. The Government is very gravely concerned at these violations not only of internationally accepted standards of justice but also, it appears, of basic Islamic principles. The Government calls on the Iranian Government to extend to all of its citizens the right to fair and open trial for all offences and to cease forthwith executions for political or religious reasons.

SALES TAX
Mr CONNOLLY-I direct my question to the Treasurer. Does he have any evidence of business sector hostility towards the increases in sales tax?
Mr HOWARD-There can be no doubt that those businesses which for the first time have been made subject to sales tax in respect of their products do not particularly welcome the Government's decision. Nobody likes to be brought within the tax net for the first time; that is a self evident proposition. But I think much of the so called hostility of the business community to these changes has been exaggerated. Part of that has been due to a particularly virulent campaign waged by some groups which appear to believe that their products are deserving of specialised treatment so far as the application of sales tax is concerned.
It is instructive, therefore, to examine the latest Australian Chamber of Commerce-National Bank of Australasia Ltd survey, which contains a very high proportion of retailers and wholesalers in its coverage. Their responses to a question on sales tax certainly would not be regarded as being disinterested. It is significant that of those people surveyed in this particular survey-it is a fairly authoritative one so far as attitudes of the Australian business community are concerned-no less than 88 per cent, including a very high proportion of retailers and wholesalers, responded to the effect that they saw little or no impact or only a minor impact on their products so far as the new sales tax measures are concerned. I think that sort

2576 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Questions Without Notice

of survey is a more accurate reflection of what the Australian business community thinks of these changes, because on maturer reflection it believes that there is very great value in the modest broadening of the tax base contained in the Budget measures.
SALES TAX
Mrs CHILD-My question, which is addressed to the Treasurer, really follows on from what he has just been saying. Small businesses around the Henty area do not agree with what he has had to say. My question to him comes in three parts and it comes in a letter from one of the businessmen in my area. Has the Treasurer considered the cost of administering this tax in a small company? Does he realise that retailers do not differentiate between the cost of any article and sales tax and that they apply their margin to both? How can this legislation be other than inflationary as the additional cost over the counter could be more than double the tax collected? This is not a question, but a comment--
Mr SPEAKER-Order! The honourable member is not entitled to do that. She has asked her question. I call the Treasurer.
Mr HOWARD-I thank the honourable member for Henty for the question. I say in reply, as I said in reply to an earlier question from her on the same subject a couple of weeks ago, that of course there is a cost to business in administering sales tax. I have never denied that. I have never run away from that fact, but I believe that the cost to the business community and to the community generally of a sales tax approach is infinitely lower than the cost of a value added tax or a broad based retail turnover tax which are the traditional alternatives so far as indirect taxation is concerned. Certainly, retail margins are applied on top of the increase represented by the sales tax.
That. has always been the case. So far as the inflationary effects are concerned, of course there is a price effect of indirect tax increases but it has to be remembered that because of the more modest increases which have ocurred in the price of oil over the last year the rate of increase in indirect tax collections this year will be slower than last year, despite the increases in the sales tax. It is also worth recalling that in this Budget we have increased indirect tax at about half the rate that it was increased in the Hayden Budget of 1975.
EXPERIMENTAL BUILDING STATION
Mr BRADFIELD-Is the Minister for Housing and Construction aware of industry concern that the important functions of the Government's Experimental Building Station in Sydney will

cease if plans for the station's disposal are pursued? Can the Minister advise, in a brief form, what stage has been reached in .that disposal process?
Mr McVEIGH-I have been informed of the views expressed by certain people about the Experimental Building Station, but they are ill informed. We are quite confident from the nine inquiries we have received that these requirements of the Government can be met.

LIVE SHEEP TRADE
Mr STEELE HALL-I direct my question to the Minister for Primary Industry. In view of the continued interest of Middle East buyers in purchasing live sheep from Australia and the underlying threat from some unions that they may take industrial action to disrupt that trade, can the Minister assure the House that his Department and the Government will co-operate with any State government which may be involved and with representatives of participating industries who may need direct support to prevent the disruption which could arise to this most important export industry?
Mr NIXON-The short answer to the honourable member's question is yes. We will co-operate with any State government and organisation that seeks to keep open trade with the Middle East. I answered a question on this matter in the House in the last couple of weeks. I pointed out then that the fact is-this seems to be misunderstood by the trade union movement-that the growth in carcass meat sales to the Middle East has been as fast as the growth in the live sheep trade. There has been no diminution of the carcass meat trade despite the growth of the live sheep trade. It is totally false for the Australian Council of Trade Unions and the unions involved in the meat industry to go round the countryside claiming that their jobs have been put in jeopardy because of the live sheep trade. That is not the fact at all.
Mr Young-It is.
Mr NIXON-There is no diminution of the carcass meat trade. The honourable member for Port Adelaide could not tell B from a bull's foot.
Mr Young-Lock some of your mates up.
Mr NIXON-Just be patient.
Mr SPEAKER-The honourable member for Port Adelaide will remain silent.
Mr NIXON-The honourable member has been overseas for too long; that is his trouble. It is about time he came back into the House and said something.
Mr SPEAKER-The Minister will cease taking notice of interjections, and the honourable member for Port Adelaide will cease interjecting.
Mr NIXON-I repeat: The fact is that the carcass meat trade has not diminished because of the development of the live sheep trade. That is a factual statement. I gave that information to the members of the ACTU delegation that came to Canberra to meet me on this issue.
Mr Scott-They do not believe you any more.

Mr NIXON-That was prior to their taking that decision. I had an understanding with them that they would confer with me again on the issue after looking at the information. Regrettably that aspect of the arrangement has not been honoured. They have now taken a policy decision to try to stop live sheep exports. I know from first hand experience and knowledge that the pastoral industry and the farmers of this country will not stand for any interference with the live sheep export trade. That is the fundamental strength in the sheep industry at the moment. It will be foolhardy indeed for any trade union to believe that it can interfere with this trade in any way at all.

It is true that there is an international turn" down in the alumina-aluminium industry. There is a slackness. These cycles occur in all industries. reducing its exports by about 50 per cent. The Australian partners in Nabalco, basically CSR Ltd, will be continuing their production and are able to dispose of it. AUSTRALIAN WHEAT BOARD
Mr ROCHER-My question is directed to the Treasurer. Is it intended to allow the Australian Wheat Board to borrow extensively in the domestic money market? Will the difference between interest on government borrowings and rural credits or any other differentials be subsidised? If so, what control will the Government exercise over interest rates offered by the Wheat Board?
Mr HOWARD-The answer to the first two parts of the question is yes. The arrangement that has now been operating for two or three years whereby instead of the first advance to wheat growers coming entirely out of the rural credits department of the Reserve Bank of Australia, it comes rather from commercial borrowings, was instituted for monetary policy reasons. It is part of the arrangements the Government agreed to in order to preserve in an explicit way the implicit subsidy that had existed by reason of a subsidised interest rate prevailing in respect of rural credits advances. The Government has paid a subsidy, being the difference between the rate determined by the market and the rate that would have been payable if the advance had come from the rural credits department. I make it quite plain that it was a deliberate Government decision to keep faith with the industry to continue that subsidy, and it will continue.
On the question of control over the interest rate, I can assure the honourable gentleman that the Wheat Board, in co-operation with the Treasury and other advice available to the Government, ensures that very appropriate and attractive rates in terms of market conditions are obtained. I think most people who have observed the operation of the Wheat Board in this area over the past couple of years would agree that the operation has been managed ,in a very efficient

fashion and that the Wheat Board has not paid anything other than proper market rates. Therefore the subsidy paid by the Australian taxpayer has not been any greater than absolutely necessary.

Mr STREETI can assure the Deputy Leader of the Opposition that no deliveries will be made to Japan under new contracts until a safeguards agreement has been negotiated. That willnot be on terms less stringent than those negotiated with other countries.
EUROPEAN ECONOMIC COMMUNITY: AGRICULTURAL POLICY
Mr LLOYD-Is the Minister for Trade and Resources aware of the increasing trend in the European Economic Community to use an additional consumer price, or co-responsibility levy, to subsidise agricultural exports beyond the limit imposed by the one per cent value added tax ceiling? In the Minister's commendable criticism of EEC agricultural policy, now in concert with several other countries, is the point being clearly made that this new device is no more acceptable to the rest of the world than the more entrenched variable levy, export restitutions and common agricultural policy budget schemes?
Mr ANTHONY-I am familiar with the increasing use of the so-called co-responsibility levy by the European Economic Community. It is just another device in its armoury of extravagant farm support arrangements. It has used the coresponsibility levy for a number of years in the dairying industry. The EEC argues that it is a levy to suppress increased production. Of course, the net result over the period in which it has been used has been a huge increase in dairying production. This levy, added to the other. forms of support, is .used to subsidise the export of the EEC's production to other markets arouno the world.
Recently the EEC has used the coresponsibility levy in the sugar industry. This, in itself, is an additional means to support the export of subsidised sugar from the EEC. To talk in

Questions Without Notice

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2579

terms of the levy being a penalty on the industry is ridiculous when it is really being paid for by the consumer. When a 2per cent levy is added to a product and consumer prices are increasing at the rate of at least 8 per cent to 10 per cent a year, the consumer pays the levy and the producer is not penalised. That the EEC should use this device and continue its irresponsible behaviour is causing enormous resentment and challenges around the world. I fail to see how it can defend the increasing use of this practice along with its other practices.

I further ask whether Publishing and Broadcasting and/or British Telecom was directly or indirectly informed of the Cabinet decision before the public announcement by the Minister or any other government source?
Mr SINCLAIR-The answer to the last part of the honourable gentleman's question is no. The answer to his remark about praying is that I hope somebody will start praying for him pretty soon.
SINAI PEACEKEEPING FORCE
Mr WHITE~I refer the Prime Minister to reports today that foreshadow agreement by France, Britain, the Netherlands and Italy to take part in the Sinai force. What will be Australia's position if these countries take part but Canada does not?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-As a result of discussions that took place during the Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting the Government believes that the most likely way of achieving a balanced force-something which it wanted from the outset and which it made plain from the outset-would be for Canada and Britain to participate in the force along with Australia. That is why the decision was made in those terms. It is also why the United States was informed of our decision in the terms of which the honourable gentleman and the House are well aware. If, as a result of published reports that

have become evident over the last day or two, it appears that the countries the honourable gentleman mentioned-France, Britain, the Netherlands and Italy-will take part in the force, obviously that also will lead to a balanced force. The Commonwealth's concern all along is that there be a balanced force. We would obviously look at the matter on the merits to see which countries are actually going to perform and to contribute. But I am making the point that the participation of Canada is not the only way of achieving that particular objective. If these other countries--
Mr Scott-Mr Speaker, I take a point of order. Could I ask the Prime Minister to speak into the microphone? We cannot hear him on this side of the chamber.
Mr SPEAKER-I call the Prime Minister.
Mrs Child-Start again.
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-Mr Speaker, I was invited by the Opposition to start again. I think this is the first time. I have been shown that courtesy so I thank the honourable member for Henty. But I will spare the House going through it again. I very much hope that the countries that have been mentioned in reports over the last day or two do make firm decisions and firm commitments. I also hope that Canada is still prepared to participate and I welcome the reports concerning New Zealand. But what we are concerned about is achieving a balanced force and it looks at long last as though that will be achieved.

. . . I hope that Qantas will be able to develop a service across the Indian Ocean to Mauritius in the not too distant future.

I think it is fairly unlikely we will go to Mauritius.
I ask the Minister: Firstly, who is determining the Government's international aviation policy; secondly, which of the two statements is correct; and thirdly, does the Minister endorse the continued monopolisation by South African Airways of the lucrative Indian Ocean market?
Mr HUNT-The Government has taken a firm policy decision that Qantas services to South

2580 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Questions Without Notice

Africa will not be restored. However, Qantas has been investigating the possibility of opening services between Perth and/or other ports in Mauritius and Southern Africa. I know that the Chairman of Qantas, with whom I will be having discussions tonight, has taken those negotiations further. I am hopeful that we will be able to initiate a service across the Indian Ocean in the not too distant future. But whether Qantas will inaugurate services between Perth and Mauritius or other ports in Southern Africa will be a commercial decision taken by Qantas.
CLOTHING ITEMS: SALES TAX
Mr SHACK~I have a further question for the Treasurer on sales tax. Has the Treasurer's attention been drawn to the fact that some clothing retailers have recently substantially marked up the price of their items, blaming the price hike on sales tax increases? Is clothing presently subject to sales tax? If not, what action can consumers take in the face of such blatant misrepresentation and profiteering?
Mr HOWARD~I have been made aware of examples of some traders falsely representing to the public that price increases that they themselves have imposed are the result of sales tax increases. Specifically, any claim made by any trader anywhere in Australia that any price increase in recent times is due to a sales tax rise is blatantly and totally false. At the very minimum I would urge consumers in the face of that to take their complaints to the various consumer affairs bodies of the various States.
While I am answering that question, perhaps I could inform the House of a significant decision that the Government has taken regarding the sales tax legislation. The House will be aware that last week when I indicated that the Government would, at the expiry of the three-month period which commenced on 20 October, present Bills to the same effect to the Parliament in the new year, I indicated that I would inform the Parliament of the Government's decision regarding the commencement date of those new measures. I am now able to say that, for practical reasons only, the commencement date cannot be I January but must be a later date. The reason for this is in no way related to a view on the part of the Government that a commencement date on I January, under Bills passed after that date, would have been retrospective, but rather a view the Government has formed that for practical administrative reasons sufficient time must be given to the taxation authorities to send out detailed instruction kits and guides to some 40,000 traders who will be subject to the sales tax for the first time.

It would clearly be seen by many, and properly so, as a contempt of this Parliament by the Executive if action to do that were taken in advance of the legislation being passed. So I make it plain, in again confirming that the Government will present Bills to the same effect in the new year, that the commencement date of the legislation, instead of being 1 January, will be Monday 29 March. In all other respects the legislation and the intentions of the Government will be precisely the same. We will not be acceding to any of the requests on this matter conveyed by another place. In anticipation of the question that inevitably win be asked, the net effect to revenue in the current financial year is $79m.
URANIUM ADVISORY COUNCIL ANNUAL REPORT
Mr ANTHONY (Richmond-Minister for Trade and Resources)-Mr Speaker, I seek your indulgence to answer a question asked of me yesterday by the honourable member for Hindmarsh (Mr Scott) concerning a matter in the annual report of the Uranium Advisory Council which I tabled on 22 October. I am able to advise the honourable member that the report of the Uranium Advisory Council on its review of the Atomic Energy Act and related matters, together with submissions which the Council received on this matter in response to its public call for submissions, are available to interested parties.
As is the usual practice with reports and advisings to the Government froin the Uranium Advisory Council, the report of the Council in relation to this matter appears in its annual report in Appendix H. I refer the honourable member to page 2 of the annual report of the Council, under the heading 'Submissions by the Public', which indicates that 46 ,public submissions on the review of the Atomic Energy Act were received by the Council,. some of them quite extensive. The report states that bound volumes containing copies of submissions are held by the Council library. They may be perused there by interested parties or may be borrowed for a limited period. Additionally, I have arranged for copies of bound volumes containing the public submissions to be placed in the Parliamentary Library.

NUCLEAR SAFEGUARDS AGREEMENT WITH EUROPEAN ATOMIC ENERGY
COMMUNITY
Agreement and Ministerial Statement
Mr STREET (Corangamite-Minister for Foreign Affairs)-I hereby present the agreement between the Government of Australia and the European Atomic Energy Community concerning transfers of nuclear material from Australia to the European Atomic Energy Community, signed in Brussels on 21 September 1981. I seek leave to make a statement.
Leave granted.
Mr STREET-In presenting this agreement I am fulfilling a commitme,nt by the Government to ensure that the texts of all Australia's nuclear safeguards agreements will be presented in the Parliament as soon as practicable after signature with a full explanation of their provisions.
The conclusion of a nuclear safeguards agreement with the European Atomic Energy Community-Euratom-has been a major objective of the Government in the nuclear safeguards area. Upon entry into force it will open the way to exports of Australian uranium to all member states of the European Community under new contracts. The agreement is a significant step in the establishment of a network of bilateral nuclear safeguards agreements between Australia and our potential uranium customers, in accordance with Australia's nuclear safeguards policy announced to Parliament by the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) on 24 May 1977.
The agreement has been negotiated with Euratom as a single entity in recognition of the competence and responsibilities vested in Euratom by the member states under the Euratom Treaty. Australia had already accorded this recognition in the safeguards agreements negotiated with the United Kingdom and France. The present agreement gives full effect to our recognition of Euratom as an entity for transfers of nuclear material from Australia to all member states of the Community. Assertions have been made from time to time that this is not in accordance with Australian policy. These assertions are not correct. From the time of the announcement of Australia's nuclear safeguards policy, the Government made clear that it had recognised Euratom as a single entity and would deal with this reality. The Government is gratified that we have been successful in doing so.
The primary purpose of this agreement is to provide that when Australia supplies uranium to any of the 10 member countries of Euratom for

2584 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Nuclear Safeguards Agreement

peaceful purposes, this uranium will not be diverted to non-peaceful or explosive purposes. To this end, and in accordance with our nuclear safeguards policy, all of Australia's bilateral nuclear safeguards agreements incorporate stringent safeguards and controls on the use of the uranium we may supply to other countries for peaceful purposes. This agreement, as with the other bilateral nuclear safeguards agreements concluded by Australia since the introduction of our comprehensive nuclear safeguards policy, incorporates all the requirements of that policy. It provides for the prohibition of explosive or military use, the application of International Atomic Energy Agency safeguards, 'fall-back' safeguards in the event that IAEA safeguards cease to apply, the application of internationally recognised levels of physical protection, and prior consent rights over retransfers to third countries outside the Community. The agreement provides for administrative arrangements to implement the provisions of the agreement and to track Australian supplied material throughout the fuel cycle of customer countries members of the EUropean Community. These detailed procedures enable us to establish that there is no diversion of Australian-supplied material to non-peaceful or explosive purposes.
I would now like to comment on some particular features of the agreement I have tabled. Like, the French and Swedish agreements which I tabled in Parliament in March of this year, this agreement includes provisions covering reprocessing of spent fuel containing material supplied by Australia. These provisions accord with the Government's policy of maintaining Australia's prior consent rights over reprocessing of Australian origin nuclear material, while establishing conditions in our bilateral agreements under which this consent may be exercised. I announced the Government's policy on these conditions in Parliament on 27 November 1980. The detailed procedures for implementation of the conditions in the agreement relating to reprocessing are contained in a separate document. That document, being of less than treaty status; is not being tabled but will be made available to members or senators on request through my office.
Unlike our agreements with France and the United Kingdom, the scope of the agreement with Euratom is limited to nuclear material only as defined in the agreement and is not reciprocal in that it covers only transfers of nuclear material from Australia to Euratom. This reflects the specific competence of Euratom in the nuclear field. Written assurances have been provided by the member states of Euratom which do not have

bilateral agreements with Australia of their willingness to negotiate additional arrangements with Australia to cover forms of nuclear transfers other than those covered in this agreement if and when appropriate, as well as an assurance from the Community that it will not impede such arrangements between a member state and Australia provided these are consistent with the Euratom Treaty.
Consistent with the division of competence between Euratom and the member states, the latter
will apply the necessary measures of physical protection. In this regard, member states not having bilateral agreements with Australia have provided written undertakings that they will fulfil within their own areas of competence the physical protection obligations of the agreement. All these assurances are incorporated in the agreement. The Euratom agreement, like our agreements with two Euratom member states, the United Kingdom and France, provides that the provisions in the agreement will be complementary to, and where appropriate supersede, the provisions of the bilateral agreements with the United King-
dom and France.
A side letter to the agreement sets out arrangements for the exercise of prior consent in relation to transfers to countries outside the Community of Australian origin nuclear material for the purposes of conversion, low enrichment and fuel fabrication. These arrangements accord with Australia's nuclear safeguards policy. The letter notes that in circumstances where member states of the Community use for these fuel cycle services
the facilities of countries with which Australia does not have a nuclear safeguards agreement, they may do so on an 'all-in, all-out' basis. This principle is already being applied pursuant to our agreements with Finland and Sweden. The side letter also provides that transfers outside the Community for reprocessing of Australian origin spent fuel may be made to countries with which Australia has a nuclear safeguards agreement in
force.
Of the ten member states of Euratom, France and the United Kingdom are nuclear weapon states which, under the Treaty of the NonProliferation of Nuclear Weapons, are not obliged to renounce nuclear weapons or accept international safeguards. Under the Government's stringent nuclear safeguards policy, nuclear weapon states which wish to import Australian uranium must provide assurances that nuclear material supplied for peaceful purposes is not diverted to military or explosive purposes and must agree that the uranium we supply be covered by IAEA safeguards. The French and British

Nuclear Safeguards Agreement

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2585

governments have made these commitments in their separate bilateral nuclear safeguards agreements with Australia as well as through the Australia-Euratom agreement. They have themselves concluded separate agreements with the IAEA and Euratom to make their civil facilities subject to IAEA safeguards. Under Australia's agreement with Euratom, the non-nuclear weapon states member countries of the Community commit themselves to placing nuclear material subject to the agreement under IAEA safeguards applied pursuant to their NPT-based safeguards agreements with the IAEA, which provide for all nuclear material in their nuclear industries to be subject to such safeguards.
The tabling of this agreement makes a total of nine nuclear safeguards agreements covering sixteen countries concluded by the Government since the announcement of its nuclear safeguards policy by the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) on 24 May 1977. The Government is proud of its achievements in negotiating these agreements. We believe they provide a convincing demonstration of the international acceptability of Australian policy. They show that, while applying stringent non-proliferation safeguards to our uranium exports, we are prepared to make Australian uranium available to customer countries for legitimate peaceful energy purposes. The agreements show that Australia, by facilitating peaceful nuclear trade under stringent predictable conditions, is doing so in a manner which supports international efforts aimed ,at preventing the spread of nuclear weapons. The Government welcomes the conclusion of this agreement with Euratom (Quorum formed). It represents an important step forward in Australia's relations with the European Community and its member states.
Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford-Smith) -by leave-The most significant part of the statement of the Minister for Foreign Affairs (Mr Street) is that which states:
The agreement provides for administrative arrangements to implement the provisions of the agreement and to track Australian supplied material throughout the fuel cycle of customer countries members of the European Community. These detailed procedures enable us to establish that there is no diversion of Australian supplied material to non-peaceful or explosive purposes.
Whilst we would all like to see that happen, it has not happened and is not likely to happen in the future. For example, although it was inexcusable for Israel to have destroyed the Iraqi reactor, that reactor was using French enriched uranium. It could have been using Australian uranium which was enriched to such an extent that it was deemed to be weapons grade material. So much for the concept of preventing proliferation and diversion!

The Israeli attack was inexcusable but the uranium in the Iraqi reactor was so enriched that it was deemed to be of weapons grade. It could well have been Australian uranium. It if were not Australian uranium there is no doubt that France would. have been equally pleased to know that Iraq had adequate supplies from Australia and was able to divert its other supplies to the process of enrichment.
Imake it very clear that the Opposition's policy is not to mine uranium until such time as the world can be satisfied that there will be no diversion and we have the technology to ensure satisfactory disposal of high level waste. Those two problems have not yet been solved. It does not help Australia one iota to be told that the situation is magnificent, that we are able to sell our uranium to the European Atomic Energy Community and that it is profitable. The point has been made time and again that when we sell our natural resources all we have left in Australia is the hole in the ground. Our uranium may well be diverted to other sources. Once the uranium goes into the enrichment process it is usually amalgamated with somebody else's uranium. Once it has been through the reactor and gone through the first cycle it is reprocessed and divided 'into plutonium and high level waste. At that -stage we certainly could not trace our uranium. It would be impossible to do so. No country in the world could do so. The International Atomic Energy Agency is presently bedevilled in trying to work out how to get an international standard for the safeguarding of plutonium, which comes from the second part of the process. It is nonsensical to talk about having an ability to track our uranium. The only satisfactory way in which Australia could ever monitor a fuel cycle would be for Australia to own and control the whole cycle. It would then know by audit what uranium it had, how it was used and what it got back. It could literally count it. That is the only satisfactory method. It is about time this Government woke up to that.
Let me deal with some of the other specifics in the Minister's statement. He claimed that we have an agreement with Euratom as a single entity. I suggest that is not the situation. Euratom at present includes Greece but what about Spain? Spain has not yet signed the Treaty on the NonProliferation of Nuclear Weapons. According to the Government's much vaunted policy, nonnuclear weapons states must be parties to the Non-Proliferation Treaty before they receive our uranium. Assuming that Spain becomes a member of Euratom where does it leave us in relation to that leg of our policy?

2586 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Nuclear Safeguards Agreement

Another matter which is worthwhile noting, and which is somewhat disturbing, is what the Minister said about that part of the agreement which states that European countries which are members of Euratom now have all the necessary conditions settled for the supply of Australian uranium; I make the point again: Why are we giving away this very precious, very volatile, very dangerous fuel, when we are getting nothing back, nothing except a large hole in the ground and all sorts of other difficulties associated with our ertvironmnent?
When these countries that are getting the uranium also have the ,technology, would it not be appropriate to suggest that, from the point of view of our own interests in the future, we have a look at their technology, look at how they are making advances, if any, and see where their problems are? In other words, why should our scientists not be made aware of the technology that these people are using? They are so anxious to get the fuel that surely, on a quid pro quo basis, the Australian scientists, for the benefit of the Australian public, ought to have an opportunity to get up to date with the technology that these people are at least allegedly developing. After all, they get access to our uranium but we seem to be left with nothing at all except, as I said, for the hole in the ground. It is important that we develop national policies that protect our national interests and give us access to technological advances. This would give us an opportunity to assess the value of these so-called safeguard agreements.
Today in Question Time I raised'with the Minister the question of Japan. I am pleased with his answer. nobody can be trusted in this area. There are 20,000 nuclear weapons now, many of them as a result of illegal diversion from what is deemed to

the matter. We still have not received that reply. We want a guarantee that we do not have any pipeline leading to France which leads directly to nuclear weapons. France should not be allowed to get the uranium, let us make that very clear. Irrespective of the nature of their government, we are here to criticise things that can endanger us and the rest of the world, and we want to make that point very obvious again today.
I turn to one other matter. I notice that the Minister is very coy about mentioning this matter but I think we can tease it out of him in this way. The Minister said:
A side-Ietler to the Agreement sets out arrangements for the exercise of prior consent in relation to transfers, to countries outside the Community,-
In other words, transfers of our uranium go outside the Community. The Minister went on to say:
. . . for the purposes of conversion, low enrichment and fuel fabrication. These arrangements accord with Australia's nuclear safeguards policy. The letler notes that in circumstances where member states of the Community use, for these fuel cycle services, the facilities of countries with which Australia does not have a nuclear safeguards agreement, they may do so on an 'all-in, all-out' basis.
Let us look at this 'all-in, all-out' basis. We are aware also of agreements for supplying uranium to Finland and Sweden. It is pretty ch!ar that the uranium supplied to Finland and Sweden is enriched, and, I would say, it will be reprocessed, in the Soviet Union. One cannot find anywhere in this agreement that it is in the Soviet Union that this will happen. One is not going to raise this issue from the point of view of the Red bogy but at Question Time we had the Prime Minister castigating us at every level about the Russians or the Soviet Union. Here today in an agreement, we have agreed that the countries in Europe which want to go outside the Community can do so. In other words, the letter provides that:
. . . transfers outside the Community for reprocessing of Australian origin spent fuel may be made to countries with which Australia has a nuclear safeguards agreement in force.
I want to make it clear that this paragraph refers to the Soviet Union. The fact is that there is no real guarantee that supplies of Australian uranium to Europe, under present agreements, will not at some time be processed in the Soviet Union. I think that is a very coy way of approaching the matter because it obviously will happen. As I said, the Soviet Union does not get a mention

in this statement but it will get the uranium for reprocessing and enrichment. Why can the Government not be honest and straightforward with the Parliament instead of dressing up these crucial issues in language which amounts to nothing but a cover-up, particularly when we have to listen to all the nonsense at Question Time about how dangerous it is to be encouraging the Russians in one way or another when at the same time we are tabling statements that relate to the fact that we are going to trust them with the uranium. They have the facilities; they belong to the international safeguards agreements. In fact, they were one of th(( prime movers at Vienna along with the United States, sitting at the same table, working out the destiny as to who shares what in technology and who shares what from the point of view of international energy fuel. It is about time we as Australians got very definite as to what we want to do about these matters in Australia's interests.
I make the point again that at present there are no adequate safeguards. We cannot control this fuel unless we get to a position, which we have not yet got to, where we have satisfactory technology to prevent any diversion and satisfactory technology to enable disposal of high level wa!ite. The only way in which to do those two things is to own and control the fuel through the whole cycle. If anybody is able to use it, subject to those safeguards I mentioned, it should be on a leased basis, whereby Australia will lease the fuel only and take it back and solemnly audit that there has been no diversion. Then we could have some real debate in this Parliament about satisfactory arrangements.

JOINT COMMITTEE OF PUBLIC ACCOUNTS
Reports
Mr CONNOLLY (Bradfield)-I present the one hundred and ninety-first and one hundred and ninety-second reports from the Joint Commit.tee of Public Accounts.
Ordered that the reports be printed.
Mr CONNOLLY-by leave-The one hundred and ninety-first report is a handbook on Commonwealth Government Financial Administration, and the One hundred and ninety-second report is a discussion paper on the Collection and Dissemination of Statistics. These reports have

2588 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Joint Committee ofPublic Accounts

not resulted from any specific inquiry or examination, but rather from the Committee's long standing interest in efficient and economical public administration.
The one hundred and ninety-first report is a handbook which covers matters administered by the Treasurer, (Mr Howard) the Minister for Finance (Senator Dame Margaret Guilfoyle), and their respective Departments. It explains, in concise and readable form, the procedures by which the Commonwealth accounts for the public moneys and other assets which are under its control. The handbook explains the financial documents presented to Parliament, the classification of Budget information, the monthly statement of Commonwealth Government financial transactions, the various Commonwealth accounts and funds, and the management of Commonwealth cash accounts within Australia and overseas.
The handbook follows essentially the pattern of a previous publication which was issued by the Public Accounts Committee and prepared by the Department of Finance in 1977, but has been updated and revised to take into account, in particular, the change brought about by the 1979 amendments to the Audit Act 1901. The Committee wishes to take this opportunity to thank the Department of Finance for its considerable assistance in the preparation of this publication.
In tabling this handbook in the form of a report to both Houses of Parliament, the Committee is convinced that the wider circulation it will receive should substantially contribute to an expanded awareness of the processes by which Commonwealth funds are receipted and expended. The Committee further believes that this report will prove to be a valuable and convenient reference source for senators and members and the community in general.
The Committee's one hundred and ninetysecond report on statistics takes, as its starting point, the 1974 report on the Integration of Data Systems known as the Crisp Report. That inquiry identified three major deficiencies in Data systems: Poor co-ordination of statistical collections; inadequate communication between producers and users of statistics; and poor accessibility to data that was collected. Unfortunately many of these problems remain in 1981.
As a result of the Crisp report the Australian Bureau of Statistics Act 1975 was passed to establish the Australian Bureau of Statistics as a statutory authority charged with co-ordinating statistical systems in Australia. However, the PAC believes that the central purpose has been missed because the Bureau's powers are still not

regarded as being sufficient. Accordingly, the Committee believes that the Bureau's powers should be clarified and strengthened.
There is a widely held view abroad in the business community that collections of basic economic data are, in some areas, wholly inadequate. In this regard I refer honourable members to an article in the Australian of 19 October 1981 where a Sydney stockbroking firm, Dominguez and Barry, had to research such sources as Reserve Bank bulletins, Budget papers, as well as the Australian Bureau of Statistics figures to determine the average per capita public debt, as the Government publishes no such figures. The article quotes these brokers as calling upon the Government for better statistical information on State and Federal borrowings and says that currently information in this area is very poor. However, the Committee believes that the Bureau must, of course, decide whether the cost of such additional collations are, in fact, justified.
This paper addresses itself in part to these problems. It also discusses the advantages of allowing the Bureau access to identified taxation data on businesses as a way of reducing the respondent burden for statistical collections. Access to taxation data, particularly businesses, was recommended in the 1974 Crisp report. Both the Bureau and the Taxation Office sent representatives to assess the systems of tax access for economic statistics operating in Canada and the United States of America in 1979. Surprisingly, no results of the joint investigations by the Bureau and Taxation Office have yet been presented to the Treasurer, to the best of our knowledge.
The proposed access to tax data is supported by the Australian Law Reform Commission, the Australian Taxpayers Association and the Confederation of Australian Industry. It is to be hoped that this discussion paper will stimulate debate on this sensitive issue.
Also, on the question of accessibility to data, it is pleasing to note the Bill introduced on 15 October 1981 by the Treasurer to amend the Census and Statistics Act to allow census data, with personal identifiers removed, to be made more widely available.
The Crisp inquiry had recommended even wider changes to the Act. These recommendations were endorsed by the Crawford report on structural adjustment. The Law Reform Commission also recommended certain liberalisations in 1979 and some of these have been accepted by the Government.
The Committee is still concerned that accessibility to data collection is not optimal, and that

Joint Committee ofPublic Accounts

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2589

savings can be made by reducing duplication of collections and making more useful data more widely available. Of course, increased accessibility carries with it the danger of infringement of privacy. The Committee is very conscious of this danger but feels guidelines put forward in a recent Law Reform Commission discussion paper, entitled 'Privacy and Personal Information', will provide adequate protection in this regard.
I will refer to one other issue raised in the report; that of the paper burden imposed on business by government. This was the subject of a recent interdepartmental inquiry but the outcome of that study disappointed the Committee. We feel that more can be done in this area and suggest that initiatives recently taken overseas, particularly in Canada and the United States of America, deserve serious consideration in Australia. The Committee believes that one initiative that would have a positive effect would be a central register of all statistical collections and for the Bureau to be given suitable powers to authorise and advise on the form of statistical collections by other Government instrumentalities.
The Committee hopes that the issues raised in this report, and the suggestions made, will encourage a wide-ranging discussion on matters that impact very heavily on Australia's overall economic performance in the future.
I wish to conclude by saying that these reports continue the Committee's philosophy of bringing wider notice to financial and management issues in the public sector. In so doing, it would welcome a dialogue with any person or organisation that believes it has a contribution to make. I commend the reports to honourable members.
Dr THEOPHANOUS (Burke)-by leaveWe were told by the Joint Committee of Public Accounts that these reports were to be presented to the House tomorrow. Several members of the Committee-members on both sides of the House-wanted to comment on these reports as they raise substantial issues. Suddenly we find that the reports are presented today. I ask the Government to give consideration to bringing on the discussion of these reports at a later time. They raise very important issues, as I have already mentioned, especially the report on the Collection and Dissemination of Statistics: That report raises questions which are of great concern in relation to a number of decisions that have to be made by the Government and the Parliament about statistics collection, in particular the roles of the Government Statistician and the Taxation Office. There needs to be some clarification of the overlapping of these two departments. I will not take up the

to speak to the reports at a later time. I ask the Government to bring on the matter at some stage in the future.
Mr SHACK (Tangney)-by leave-I thank the House for giving me the opportunity to say a few words on the occasion of the tabling of these reports by the Chairman of the J<;>int Committee of Public Accounts. Before dping so, however, I address a comment to my Public Accounts Committee colleague the honourable member for Burke (Dr Theophanous). It was my understanding that the reports we are discussing this afternoon were to be tabled in this chamber today and in the Senate chamber tomorrow.
The two reports, the one hundred and ninety-
first report on Commonwealth Government Financial Administration and the one hundred and ninety-second report on the Collection and Dissemination of Statistics-a Discussion paper-are very important documents, as both the Chairman and the honourable member for Burke have said. Those honourable members who have had the time today to look at the one hundred and eighty-eighth report, the annual report, which was tabled by the Chairman yesterClay, will see in that document that the Committee was extremely busy in the previous year, 1980-81, in producing reports and in addressing itself to the terms of reference given to the Public Accounts Committee; that is, examining instances of public expenditure.
I draw attention to these two reports because they are slightly different and important in that they do not address themselves to a particular investigation but, rather, they address themselves to the broader aspects of government administration. In the first place, the one hundred and ninety-first report attempts to create a better understanding and appreciation of the system of government administration. I was a tertiary student of politics. Bar for the quirk of events I might still have been at a tertiary institution teaching other people politics. One of the things lacking then-I think it would probably still be lacking today but for a report of this nature-was a comprehensive document relating to government financial administration. It is an extremely complex topic. The table of contents in this report runs through all the aspects of government financial administration-financial documents presented to the Parliament, how the Budget information is classified, monthly statements of Commonwealth Government financial transactions and so on. I do not think it is overstating it

2590 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Public Works Committee

to say that this is an extremely important and fundamental document. I refer. all honourable members to it. It is well worth reading. I know that it will be extremely welcome in tertiary institutions by people who are interested in how we run our affairs. It will be welcomed also by high school students. I believe that the Public Accounts Committee has done the nation a great service in producing in easy readable form this sort of booklet.
The other report, that on the collection and dissemination of statistics, is also an extremely important document. We all recognise the importance of government to collect statistics to form a data base on which to frame references and make decisions. On the one hand, there is a government need for information and, on .the other hand, we have to balance the cost of collection and the demands placed on those who have to respond by supplying the information. I have spoken in this Parliament before about the paper burden. I will not refer back to that speech. As the Chairman said, it is certainly mentioned in this report.
Again, the table of contents shows that the report is comprehensive in that it addresses itself to all the particular issues. I hope that through it being read in this place and outside there will be a greater discussion on the various issues related to the collection and dissemination of statistics. The Canadian system is important. The Canadians have streamlined their process by using, in a confidential and private way, statistics and information collected through the taxation system. So, without imposing on the House any further, I simply refer all honourable members to these reports. I believe that they will further advance an understanding and appreciation of how we run our financial affairs and how we can perhaps improve them in the future.

Reports
Mr BUNGEY (Canning)-In accordance with the provisions of the Public Works Committee Act 1969, I present the reports relating to the following proposed works:
Construction or working, living and area racilities ror Army and Navy personnel at Watsonia, Victoria; and upgrading or airport racilities ror the introduction or domestic wide-body aircrart.

Mr BUNGEY-by leave-The Public Works Committee's report on the upgrading of airport facilities at a number of airports for the introduction of wide-bodied aircraft is the first report on this reference. It makes recommendations on works at Coolangatta, Sydney, Melbourne and

Perth. A second report will be presented early next year in respect of work at Adelaide and Hobart. In the limited time available to the Committee since the reference of these works on 27 August 1981, the Committee has not been able to schedule a hearing in Adelaide, from where a substantial number of submissions have been received from local organisations and the public. A public hearing will be set down for December. In order to avoid any substantial delay in complet-
ing the work at Adelaide, the Department of Housing and Construction will be proceeding with detailed planning of the Adelaide work. The Committee has been unable also to complete its examination of the proposed works at Hobart. These works also will be included in the second
report.
The Committee is concerned at the number of works being referred to it which are deemed urgent. For instance, the works at Watsonia for the Department of Defence which are reported on today are required to be completed by the end of 1982. This arises from the transfer, in late 1982, of the Army Apprentice School, to which the Army School of Music is administratively attached, from Balcombe to Bonegilla. This move has been planned for some years so that the consequential requirement for accommodation and facilities for the Army School of Music has been known for quite some time. Other recent references to the Committee have also had a similar degree of urgency-for instance, the references on facilities for wide-bodied aircraft, for further dredging work at Brisbane Airport and for the upgrading of the international terminal building
at Brisbane Airport.
The Committee is required by the Public Works Committee Act to report to the Parliament as expeditiously as possible. This it does. An average time between reference of a work to the Committee and presentation of the Committee's report is some three months. Three of the reports presented today and yesterday were, in fact, completed in two months from the date of referral by the House. When it is taken into account that the Committee allows at least one month on each reference for the receipt of public submissions, the House will readily understand the pressures put on the Committee when a number of works deemed urgent are referred to it. The Committee attempts to fit in with the Government's work program. It does all that it can to avoid delays to works. The Committee is not, however, prepared to compromise its scrutiny of referred works. It is felt that if the Government can reduce the number of references involving urgent works by better planning or by earlier reference to the

Public Works Committee

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2591

Committee the scrutiny of public works expenditure entrusted by the Parliament to the Public Works Committee will be facilitated.
In the report on the upgrading of airport facilities for the introduction of wide-bodied aircraft, the Committee is critical that a number of the works at airports had been commenced in 1980-81 without reference to the Committee. These works totalled some $6.08m although no single work at any airport was in excess of $2m, the cost requiring reference to the Public Works Committee. However, certain of these works have pre-empted effective Committee examination. The situation at Perth Airport, for instance, clearly demonstrates this point. During 1980-81, works totalling $1.9m were commenced involving both a departure lounge and an arrivals lounge and baggage handling area. Part of the proposed works for Perth Airport now being reported on involve extensions or adaptions to these earlier works commenced last year. In effect, the domestic terminal development has been decided and commenced without scrutiny by the Public Works Committee or without the opportunity for comment by the pubic, by local government or by other interested groups. In fact, I feel that had the Public Works Committee considered the works the current situation in the new arrivals area, where there are no toilet facilities, may well have been avoided.
The Committee understands that the works were required urgently to facilitate the introduction of wide-body aircraft by Trans Australia Airlines. But it cannot accept that proper investigation by the Committee would have unduly delayed the works. The Committee is heartened by the following comments of the Minister for Administrative Services (Mr Newman) in his second reading speech on the Public Works Committee Amendment Bill 1981:
II is understood that the Committee was particularly concerned with cases where major works costing over $2m have been phased in over several years with small projects, that is below $2m. These projects have either escaped Committee scrutiny, of if referred to the Committee, have been referred at a stage where proper Committee investigation has been preempted by the work already done. The Government expects work totaling over $2m to be subject to parliamentary scrutiny, and will do all it can to ensure that all works required to be examined by the Committee in accordance with the Public Works Committee Act will be so examined. I have assured the Committee that should it wish to examine any work below $2m the Government would treat any request sympathetically, and would facilitate Committee investigation of any such work.
