NUCLEAR INDUSTRY
Discussion of Matter of Public Importance
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr MiIlar)-Mr Speaker has received a letter from the honourable member for Cunningham (Mr West) proposing that a definite matter of public importance be submitted to the House for discussion, namely:
The hazards involved in the uranium and nuclear industry resulting from Government moves to hand control of nuclear regulation to the States and the Northern Territory.
I call upon those members who approve of the proposed discussion to rise in their places.
More than the number ofmembers required by the Standing Orders having risen in their places-
Mr WEST (Cunningham) (3.57)-ln the last autumn session of Parliament, in the report of the Review of Commonwealth Functions brought down by this Government, one of the most disturbing future policy decisions was this:
Responsibility for the regulation and control of nuclear activities to be implemented by the States and responsibility for the financing of Northern Territory environmental regulatory services be transferred to the Territory Government (subject to the Territory establishing an independent environmental regulatory agency and continuing to have the Alligator Region Co-ordinating Committee and a Supervising Scientist consider the annual programs of the Northern Territory).
What this means is that this Government is seriously considering handing over to the Territory and the States regulatory and legislative matters concerning uranium mining and the nuclear industry. The Government does not deny this. If it implements this decision we will become the only nation in the world to decentralise control of the nuclear industry. In the United States of America, for instance, the Department of Energy, the Environmental Protection Agency and the Nuclear Regulatory Commission all have a central aspect of controlling the nuclear industry. If the Government proceeds with this decision, Australia will be in the unique position of placing its obsession with small government before its responsibility for the control of this industry. It is well known that the Opposition, for reasons that are well understood, opposes uranium mining and the extension of the

2592 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Nuclear Industry

nuclear industry in Australia. Those reasons relate to the impossibility of effecting acceptable waste disposal, nuclear proliferation and the danger of environmental contamination, particularly in the great Kakadu National Park which has now been placed on the World Heritage List. We can now add to this list of reasons substantial economic reasons and concern at the safety of nuclear plants.
As a prelude to what I want to say about nuclear regulation, I will run through some of the emerging economic arguments. World-wide concern is emerging with regard to the safety of nuclear plants and the cost of capital construction. This has led to a revision of the need for uranium throughout the world up to the year 2018. The Australian Atomic Energy Commission in 1975 assessed that world nuclear capacity would be 2;400 gigawatts. In 1980 it was forced to downgrade that assessment to 710 gigawatts. It says also that world demand for uranium by 1986 will be approximately 40,000 tonnes. We are faced with a falling world price. By 1986 we will be lucky if Australia's share of this 40,000 tonnes is 8,000 tonnes, but if the Government pushes ahead with the multiplicity of new mines that it is considering we will be producing some 14,000 to 15,000 tonnes bf uranium. That is almost double the amount that we can reasonably expect to sell on such a world market. That is the reason we have just heard the Minister for Foreign Affairs (Mr Street) put through yet another so-called safeguards agreement, this time with the European Atomic Energy Community, treating it as a single agency and allowing it the right to reprocess waste fuel rods to plutoniumsomething which two or three years ago it pledged it would never be a party to. It is forced to do this because the world market is declining as I have just explained. This is well understood by the Minister in the Northern Territory, Mr Tuxworth. In a speech on 22 October he stated:
If I was an investor . . . I would probably think twice about pUlling my funds into a uranium project at this stage.
If we don't go by March it is likely that we won't go at all.
I put it on record that the uranium mining and nuclear industry represents the most dangerous, difficult and complicated means of boiling water ever conceived by mortal man. If this Government implements its decision, it will be proceeding against the interests of future generations of mankind. Mr Speaker, the industry is proceeding only in those nations which are deficient in hydrocarbon fuels, such as the European Economic Community countries and Japan, and in those countries whose governments aspire to manufacture and control nuclear weapons. But there is no

reason for us in Australia even to consider the expansion of the nuclear industry, Yet the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) and Government members go around the country actually boasting that Australia will be a nuclear power within the next 20 years. When that is said, the questions that I and the Opposition put to them are: Why? What do they mean? How far does the Government intend to go? Who will control the industry?
The Prime Minister must understand that, when he talks about Australia being a nuclear power in 20 years, he is talking about not only uranium mining but also gasification plants, enrichment plants, nuclear power plants and waste disposal and perhaps reprocessing, For the ultimate logical result of the nuclear industry is fast neutron reactors and disposal of high level waste from such reactors. The construction of gasification and enrichment plants alone involves billions of dollars in capital expenditure and the consumption of vast amounts of Australian resources, particularly water and electricity. In case the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment (Mr Wilson) and those honourable members on the other side of the House who are present in the chamber think this is a ridiculous scenario, I refer them to statements by people such as the Premiers of Queensland and Western Australia. The other day Mr Bjelke-Petersen said: 'We are just waiting to get into the act.' He was talking about uranium enrichment. Sir Charles Court in Western Australia stresses at every opportunity the need for a nuclear power station in Western Australia. I would like to refer him to the capital cost.
When I was in Washington in June this year the highest officials of the Nuclear Regulatory Commission said to me: 'The industry is moribund in the United States because of accident fear at the plants and because of the capital cost of construction'. The State of Washington public power utility has dropped plans to construct five nuclear reactors because each reactor of 1,200 megawatts was going to cost $4,000m. What is more, no State in the United States is willing to be the first State to be permanently put aside as a repository for nuclear waste disposal.
Against these facts the Government is talking about Australia becoming a nuclear power in 20 years. It is considering handing over the regulation of not only the mining industry but also ostensibly a future nuclear industry to the States and to the Northern Territory. This is against the advice from the supervising scientists. It is against the advice of the Uranium Advisory Council. The Supervising Scientist, in a recent report of that body, stated:

Nuclear Industry

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2593

The Supervising Scientist must perrorm his runctions within a complex interlocking network or legislation and agreements.
I stress that the Opposition gives no support to uranium mining. I draw attention to the shocking, complex legislative and regulatory mess that has emerged from this Government's inability to control the situation. No less than seven or eight Commonwealth and State Acts are operative. We have the Atomic Energy Act, the Environment Protection (Alligator Rivers Region) Act, the Supervising Scientist legislation, the Environment Protection (Nuclear Codes) Act, and the Northern Territory Uranium mining environment controllegislation.
These facts emerge from this complex maze of legislation: The Nuclear Codes Act is supposed to specify standards and monitoring procedures which should be followed. The Northern Territory Uranium Mining Act is supposed to be the major instrument in controlling uranium mining under Northern Territory law which permits the Minister to issue authorisation to carry out uranium mining work subject to a long list of conditions. In the case of the Queensland Mines Ltd project at Nabarlek 44 such conditions were laid down which included numerous controls regarding management of contaminated water within an enclosed system. The Environment Protection (Alligator Rivers Region) Act appointed a Supervising Scientist and staff. It allows the Supervising Scientist to make recommendations regarding the implementation of mining procedures and safeguards. Although the Supervising Scientist is in a position to monitor standards he is really a toothless tiger because neither he nor the Northern Territory Department of Mines and Energy is responsible for daily monitoring. That is left to the company concerned under this inadequate conglomeration of Federal-State legislation. Further, there are no punishable offences created in this mishmash. Under it one small mine at Nabarlek with a uranium body of 12,000 tonnes has now been mined, as compared with a predicted 204,000 tonne mine at Jabiluka. But the conditions broke down at Nabarlek at its first major test, because the situation is so disparate and disjointed. Under these legislative controls-the system broke down, as evidenced by the Nabarlek spill.
Let us just have a quick look at the Nabarlek spill. There was an unauthorised release of water from Queensland Mines Nabarlek operation. This fact was covered up for four months. The Northern Territory and Federal authorities and the monitoring and supervising system were not able to discover this spill. They could not decide

whether water stored in the plant run-off pond overflowed or what effects the release had on the environment. Some of the Northern Territories authorities may even have known of the incident and assisted in the company's cover-up. The plant run-off pond was designed so that spills of noxious materials from the treatment plant were to be contained and not reach the environment. The level of radium in the plant run-off pond water was to average 58 pico-curies a litre.
On 8 May and 12 May 1980 raffinate, the acid waste liquid from solvent extraction plants, was diverted into the plant run-off pond. So the June water sample from the plant run~off pond contained 2,500 pico-curies a litre, 40 times the expected level.
On 6 and 7 March as a result of a tropical depression the whole system broke down and water was allowed by Queensland Mines to escape to the environment at Nabarlek. The company neglected to take samples of the water leaving the mine site. This incident was a clear infringement of the authorisation procedures. According to an agreement with the Supervising Scientist the company was obliged to notify him of any major infringement immediately and of any minor anfringement within a month. Over the next four months there was a heavy cover-up of this incident.
On 13 March a Northern Territory Inspector of Mines inspected the plant run-off pond and found no evidence of overflow. The assistant mine manager told him that there was no overflow. The company lied and told the Co-ordinating Committee for the Alligator Rivers Region that the plant operations during the March rainy period were good. Twice in May the Department of Mines and Energy inspectors inspected the site and again dismissed reports of the spill as rumours. Action finally took place on 8 July because the incident was reported by the Northern Land Council. Finally, the Supervising Scientist moved in to examine the situation. Then Queensland Mines admitted its transgression. The Northern Territory officials were either incredibly incompetent or were assisting in the cover-up.
The Nabarlek incident shows that the Northern Territory authorities are not competent to regulate uranium mining and that the Supervising Scientist is a toothless tiger relying on the Northern Territory authorities and the companies for information. If the Northern Territory authorities are unable to control the small Nabarlek operation how can they stop a disaster occurring at the much larger mines of Jabiluka and Ranger?

2594 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Nuclear Industry

We support the inclusion of that great national asset, the Kakadu National Park, in the World Heritage List. But the park will be violated by uranium mining. The United Nations Educational, Scientific and Cultural Organisation committee called for a buffer zone to surround parks as they were declared. Koongarra is an enclave inside the Kakadu National Park and Ranger and labiluka are uranium mines on its perimeter. Those two mines-labiluka and Koongarra-must not be allowed to proceed. We see that despite all of the proven inadequacy of the present hotchpotch of arrangements this Government is considering handing over control of uranium legislation and regulations to the Northern Territory and to the States to administer. Mr Speaker, there is great government confusion as to the adequacy of the current arrangements and what will transpire to devolute control to the States. The Government is determined to press ahead despite the worsening market for uranium. It is determined to decentralise regulations to control this dirty and dangerous industry to the detriment of future generations.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Drummond)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mr WILSON (Sturt-Minister for Home Affaris and Environment) (4.12)-The debate this afternoon is on a matter of public importance that reads in the following terms:
The hazards involved in the uranium and nuclear industry resulting from Government moves to hand control of nuclear regulation to the States and the Northern Territory.
The first point I make is that the leading speaker for the Opposition, the honourable member for Cunningham (Mr West), spent most of his time debating the merits or otherwise of the uranium industry, of uranium mining. He has not focussed very much attention on the mechanism whereby, given that we have a uranium industry in Australia, steps will be taken to ensure that there are adequate safeguards to protect the environment generally from the risks that are involved in such an industry.
Ever since the announcement of the decisions relating to the uranium and nuclear industries following the Government's Review of Commonwealth Functions there have been a series of misunderstandings and mistaken assumptions about the implications of those decisions. This has been particularly so in relation to the potential risks to the environment and to individual health and safety. Two things must be clearly understood at the outset. Firstly, the Commonwealth is not in

any way reducing its level of commitment to ensuring the most comprehensive and effective regulations and controls with respect to all matters regarding the uranium and nuclear industries. Secondly, the decisions of the Government to adopt these particular recommendations of the RCF committee in many instances confirm existing Commonwealth policy which has been consistently followed for quite some time.
I turn to deal in a little detail with some of the issues which relate to the matter of public importance before the House today. My responsibilities in relation to the assessment of environmental impact statements and the development of uniform nuclear codes of practice will not be reduced as a result of the RCF decisions. The decision, in fact, confirms the existing Commonwealth policy that implementation of the nuclear codes of practice should in general be the responsibility of the State and the Northern Territory governments. I shall provide the House with a little of the background to this issue. Under the Environment Protection (Nuclear Codes) Act 1978 codes of practice on nuclear safety and environmental matters are being developed in consultation with the States and the Northern Territory, with the intention that the States and the Northern Territory will legislate as appropriate to give the codes legal effect. Under the Act, the code of practice for radiation protection in mining and milling was tabled in Parliament in September 1980 and became a code formally adopted under the Environment Protection (Nuclear Codes) Act in March of this year.
Collaboration with the States and the Northern Territory on the development and implementation of nuclear codes of practice is achieved through liaison between me and the designated contact Ministers in each State and the Northern Territory. At an official level the Commonwealth State Consultative Committee on Nuclear Codes of Practice provides the mechanism for the development of the code. While the codes will be implemented by the States and the Northern Territory, the RCF decision made it clear that the Commonwealth will maintain a co-ordinating role, confirming the existing policy. Two further codes are currently being developed, through the consultative machinery, relating to the management of radioactive wastes from mining and milling and the transport of radioactive materials. The public comment stage of the Code of Practice on the Safe Transport of Radioactive Materials has been completed and it has been provided to the relevant Ministers in the States and the Northern Territory. I am hopeful that the code

Nuclear Industry

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2595

under the Act will be approved at an earlyopportunity. The proposed Code of Practice on the Management of Radioactive Waste (Mining and Milling) is now in the final stages of formulation and will be released for public comment in the near future.
I turn now to some other specific issues which are often raised when these matters are under debate. Again it is unfortunate that in so many instances that debate is characterised by misunderstanding and mistaken assumptions. In commenting on these issues I draw the attention of honourable members to a reply given in another place by my colleague the Minister for National Development and Energy (Senator Carrick) who sets the record straight on a number of important issues. An interdepartmental committee was established in 1980 to review the Atomic Energy Act and related matters. In announcing the Government's response to that IDC review, it was decided that a legal basis would be developed in consultation with the States and the Northern Territory for the development, regulation and control of nuclear activities in Australia. The IDC was reconvened in June this year to undertake that task. The task has not yet been completed but it should be understood that that in no way diminishes the Commonwealth's role and responsibility in these matters. On 19 August this year Senator Carrick said:
The broad significant mallers concerning nuclear non-proliferation of course are and will remain a Commonwealth maller . . . the movement and monitoring of radioactive materials from Australia to elsewhere in the world will always be a Commonwealth maller.
Senator Carrick also pointed out that the Australian Safeguards Office comes under his ministerial responsibilities. He also made it clear in the same reply that in many instances these matters are, and have long been, the responsibility of the States. For example, medical and industrial radioactive materials are now handled by the States. Those that normally have been treated as coming under health regulations no doubt will continue to be so treated. Senator Carrick also pointed out a salient fact which the Opposition so often seems wilfully to forget-that is, that we live in a federation with divided sovereignty between the spheres of government. In that environment it is essential to formulate co-operative approaches to these issues, to ensure that high and acceptable standards are implemented throughout the country. In that way, by recognising that we live in a federal system where there are three spheres of government, we will ensure that we have a much higher standard of control and regulation than otherwise would be the case. Senator Carrick has made the point that once the co-operative

rules are in place-to a large extent they already are-they will ensure that all kinds of fissile materials and radioactive materials throughout Australia will be subject to the highest possible standards of control and supervision that can be devised.
Some further points need to be clarified. Sales of uranium will be permitted only to countries that are subscribers to the Treaty on the Nonproliferation of Nuclear Weapons and will undertake to effect all the necessary safeguards for its handling and movement. That quite clearly is a Commonwealth responsibility. It also needs to be clearly understood that where there are mining proposals, including uranium mining proposals, arrangements have been made for the Commonwealth and the States to be co-operatively involved in the environmental assessment of those proposals. Similar arrangements are being discussed with the Northern Territory. These arrangements build on the already significant degree of co-operation and consultation which exists between the States and the Territory in the formulation of nuclear codes of practice under the Environment Protection (Nuclear Codes) Act to which I have already referred.
I wish now to comment specifically on the situation as it affects the Northern Territory.-To begin with, I stress that the decisions flowing from the RCF committee's recommendations confirm policies that have been in effect since as far back as 1978. Previous reports from the Office of the Supervising Scientist have made it clear that it was agreed in July 1978 between the Commonwealth and the Northern Territory that uranium mining operations in the Northern Territory should be regulated to the maximum effect possible through the laws of the Northern Territory and that there should be close consultation between the Northern Territory supervising authorities and the Supervising Scientist, who maintains a co-ordinating role. The Northern Territory law is used to regulate mining operations in the Alligator Rivers region. The Supervising Scientist exercises supervisory, co-ordinating, research, standards development and reporting roles under Commonwealth law. In other words, the decision which seems to have upset the Opposition so much is consistent with the principles which the Government has been following for almost three years.
The recent incident at Nabarlek concerning an unauthorised release of water demonstrates how, in a co-operative way, steps can be taken to investigate and upgrade procedures. I want to make it quite clear that I do not condone the incident at

2596 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Nuclear Industry

Nabarlek; nor do I condone in any way the tardiness of the company in informing the relevant authorities of what had occurred. The Supervising Scientist in his report on that matter said firstly that no unequivocal conclusions can be made about the effect on the environment of the water which escaped the restricted release zone. He went on to say that from information gathered to date there is, however, no evidence or expectation of any significant environmental effects. But he also reported that the company was negligent in not reporting the incident immediately and in not operating the prop in accordance with the authorisation. As he also pointed out in his report, the inquiry sprang from information coming to my attention concerning that release and I immediately set in train the appropriate investigations. As a result of those investigations the procedures have been improved and are being improved. Details of the procedures now being adopted are set out in the report that I tabled recently.
Furthermore, the Supervising Scientist has pointed out that, as a result of an experience from which, fortunately, there was no serious environmental effect-nor is there likely to be arising from that incident-as from July 1981 he has established two senior engineers in the Northern Territory. One, based in Darwin, will liaise with the Northern Territory supervising authorities as
well as visit the mine sites; the other, in time to be stationed at Jabiru, will maintain continual supervision. Through these officers, with co-operative support and the working arrangements between the Supervising Scientist and the Northern Territory supervising authorities, as well as a recognition by the companies concerned that it is important that these incidents be reported and that proper measures be taken to ensure that all the authorisations given to them and the undertakings that they have given as a result of the impact assessments are fulfilled, if these incidents occur-it is to be hoped that they will not-there will be close monitoring to ensure that the highest standards are maintained. I have just outlined the general circumstances of the incident and they have been outlined on previous occasions. The report on the incident pointed out that where necessary the Commonwealth, through the Supervising Scientist, can work co-operatively to achieve agreed standards in the activities in the Northern Territory. In this case, with the full cooperation of the Northern Territory supervising authorities, the Supervising Scientist was able to investigate the incident fully and to report to me on the issues involved.
I have made it quite clear that I want everything possible done to ensure that such an incident

will not occur again. I am confident that the necessary measures wi\l be taken to ensure that it does not but that any such incident will be promptly reported and exhaustively investigated at the very earliest opportunity. I believe the ability to meet these objectives has been considerably enhanced by the incident and lam confident that the mechanisms will become even more efficient and effective. Honourable members will be able to read in the Supervising Scientist's report that measures have already been recommended to strengthen the regulatory, monitoring and reporting procedures applying to uranium
mining in the region.
I also point out to the House that environmental requirements which attach to the granting of mining leases require that all operations shall be carried out in accordance with the best practical technology. The requirement of what is known as BPT applies to all facilities and operations taken as whole and in their component parts. It is one of the objectives of the Office of the Supervising Scientist to make sure that up to date knowledge is maintained of the relevant practices and new developments in Australia and overseas to ensure that all assessments and recommendations are consistent wi th best practical technology. Honourable members should be aware that currently discussions are under way between the Commonwealth and the Northern Territory as to the form of an independent environmental regulation agency as referred to in the Review of Commonwealth Functions decision. The Supervising Scientist will continue to carry out his role as laid down in the Environment Protection (Alligator
Rivers Region) Act.

Mr HOWE (Batman) (4.27)-It is perhaps coincidental but not without significance that this afternoon we should be discussing, on the one hand, international safeguard agreements with the European Atomic Energy Agency raising all the possibilities of nuclear proliferation and, on the other hand, the devolution by the Commonwealth Government to the States and the Northern Territory of responsibility for the regulation of the nuclear industry in this country. As was suggested by the honourable member for Cunningham (Mr West), who preceded me in this debate, that makes us unique in the world as the one country prepared to devolve nuclear regulation to provincial governments. On the one hand, we have all the issues involved in the potential for nuclear weapons and nuclear war. On the other hand, we have the sensitive question of the interrelationship between the mining of uranium

at one end of the nuclear fuel cycle and the protection of people and the environment in some of the most sensitive areas of this country.

Mr TAMBLING (Northern Territory) (4.37)-The matter of public importance listed before us states:
The hazards involved in the uranium and nuclear industry . . .
I looked up the definition of 'hazards' in the Collins Dictionary and it refers to 'exposure or vulnerability to injury, loss or evil, or something that is at risk or in danger'. Let me assert that the only real hazard we have in Australia to uranium and the nuclear industry is the Australian Labor Party and its friends in the Waterside Workers Federation of Australia on the Darwin wharf. I

question seriously Labor's motives for bringing forward this debate today. In that respect I will quote from a speech given on 9 April by the honourable member for Cunningham (Mr West). He said:
A Federal Labor Government will repudiate any commitments made by a non-Labor Government for mining, processing or export of uranium and we will demand a moratorium on uranium mining.
That policy is obstructionist, antagonistic and destructive. If we place it in historical perspective, the Commonwealth has the legislative base to control the uranium industry. The Atomic Energy Act arose from the atomic programs of other countries. It was designed for the Manhattan project and was specifically directed to the military use of uranium. Emphasis today, and let us come up to date, is on nuclear power for peaceful purposes. The modern use of uranium is very different from what it was 20 or 30 years ago. Australia, with its rich resources, has a paramount social obligation to supply energy-poor nations with a fair share of what it has.
Who controls uranium mining? I will split that into two questions. Firstly: Who controls uranium? Secondly: Who controls mining? The constitutional authority for defence and security, foreign affairs such as the non-proliferation treaties, export powers, physical protection, and classified information certainly rests with the Commonwealth. However, constitutional authority rests with the States for health, transportation, waste management, mining engineering, safety, and the environment. It is economic and practical madness to suggest that officials and bureaucrats, from both Commonwealth and State governments, should be engaged in duplication and in supervising each other. In the United States of America there are some five surveillance organisations from the Federal, state and local government arenas with areas of responsibility over the uranium industry. They are repetitive, wasteful and destructive to industry. Surely we do not want the same in Australia. The Northern Territory Government and its agencies already have nearly 150 people on the ground in the uranium province. Additionally, we have the Office of the Supervising Scientist, the Australian Atomic Energy Commission and, of course, Aboriginal interests are represented with the Northern Land Council. If we do not watch it we will all go blind watching each other. Our greatest weakness is not constitutional authority, it is not administrative procedures, it is not technical performance; it is the possibility of individual human error.

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2599

Mr TAMBLING-I will tell the honourable member about Nabarlek. It is borne out by the Nabarlek incident. There is no better example. The company manager was guilty of a grave omission in that he did not report the incident. The Supervising Scientist and Northern Territory inspectors sought confirmation of an incident and both inspectors were told there was no incident. We do not eliminate that by placing responsibility with the Commonwealth any more than we do by passing authority to the States. The solution to the human problem is ensuring that we have the best calibre people on the ground where the action is. H is a people thing, not who owns the goods or who does the job. Let us be frank. The best calibre personnel to do the monitoring and control are the experienced, professional technicians who are already in the planning and scientific agencies of the States. The system is already in place through the mining and power utilities. The last thing we want, in this new expanding industry in Australia, is to give it the yoke of a second bureaucracy. The Review of Commonwealth Functions decision with respect to uranium matters stated:
Responsibility ror the regulation and control or nuclear activities in Australia to be implemented as rar as possible by the States and Northern Territory. with the Commonwealth maintaining a co-ordinating role.
It also decided with respect to the Northern Territory matters:
Responsibility ror the financing or Northern Territory environmental regulatory services be transrerred to the Territory Government (subject to the Territory establishing an independent environmental regulatory agency and continuing to have the Alligator Region Co-ordinating Committee and the Supervising Scientist consider the annual programs or the Northern Territory.
It also stated:
The essential Commonwealth interests in the Kakadu National Park would be maintained.
Consistent with the RCF decision, the Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony) and the Minister for National Development and Energy (Senator Carrick) in a joint statement on 5 June 1981 announced that the Government would seek to establish a revised legal basis for the development, regulation and control of nuclear activities in Australia in place of the Atomic Energy Act. This revised legal basis will be established as far as possible by State and Territory legislation, taking into account the respective competencies of the State, Territory and Commonwealth governments in legislating such matters. The Governrnent has decided that high priority will be given to the development of nuclear non-proliferation

legislation. Responsibility for international obligations in relation to non-proliferation and safeguard matters lies ultimately with the Commonwealth. Nuclear non-proliferation legislation would cover the implementation of nuclear safeguards arising from international obligations. It would include such matters as physical protection of nuclear material and the protection of classified information and sensitive technology. The Commonwealth will also seek, in cooperation with the States, to develop a legal framework for licensing and regulation of health, safety and environmental aspects of nuclear activities. This legal framework would be developed under State and Territory legislation, using where appropriate complementary Commonwealth legislation.
The Commonwealth has already made arrangements to have initial discussions'in November this year with the States on these matters. This Commonwealth, State and Territory co-operative approach has already been successfully demonstrated in the arrangements to formulate and approve codes of practice under the Environment Protection (Nuclear, Codes) Act. Already, a Commonwealth, State and Northern Territory consultative committee has developed a code of practice on radiation protection in the mining and milling of radioactive ores which was approved under the Act and is available for incorporation into legislation by the States and the Northern Territory. A code of practice for the safe transport of radioactive materials is also approaching completion. The code is based on International Atomic Energy Agency regulations for the safe transport of radioactive materials. The code should shortly be approved and laid before the Parliament as provided for in the Act. The consultative committee is also in the course of finalising a code for the management of radioactive waste from the milling and mining of radioactive ores.
The new co-operative arrangements being developed with the States and the Northern Territory will avoid unnecessary duplication by the Commonwealth in areas which can be effectively handled by the States and, at the same time, establish a sound legal basis for nuclear control and regulation in Australia consistent with the respective competences of the Federal and State governments. It will be evident to honourable members that there can be no suggestion that there are hazards in the Government's cooperative federalist approach to the control and regulation of nuclear activities in Australia. There is no washing of hands of any matter or issue by the Commonwealth Government. Honourable

2600 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

CSIRO Marine Laboratories, Hobart

members should compare that with the state of the Australian Labor Party's platform which declares that the Labor Party will repudiate any commitment on this matter. The Commonwealth, States and the Northern Territory will complement each other in a sensible, co-operative manner and in a determined and well-defined framework for good arrangements.

CONSTRUCTION OF CSIRO MARINE LABORATORIES, HOBART
Approval of Work: Public Works Committee Act
Mr McVEIGH (Darling Downs-Minister for Housing and Construction) (4.47) - I move:
That, in accordance with the provisions of the Public Works Committee Act 1969, it is expedient to carry out the following proposed work which was referred to the Parliamentary Standing Committee on Public Works and on which the Committee has duly reported to Parliament, namely: Construction of CSIRO. Marine Laboratories at Hobart, Tasmania.
This proposal is for a laboratory complex to accommodate the Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation Division of Fisheries and Oceanography at a site adjacent to Castray Esplanade at Battery Point in Hobart. The new complex will replace inadequate existing facilities at Cronulla, New South Wales, that site being unsuitable for redevelopment. When completed, the complex will enable CSIRO to meet commitments in marine science research. The facilities will comprise fisheries and oceanography research laboratories; offices, workshops and stores; conference rooms and a lecture theatre; staff amenities; and servicing facilities for research vessels.
In reporting favourably on the proposal the Committee made several recommendations and comments. The Committee recommended that further discussions be held between Commonwealth, State and local government authorities of the acceptability of a three-storey development because of the possibility of cost savings. Such discussions are proceeding and the Committee will be informed of the outcome. It was also recommended that the Commonwealth examine the future use of the Cronulla laboratories, including recognition of proposals submitted by universities in the Sydney area with interests in marine science teaching and research. This will be taken into account by the Department of Administrative Services when considering future use of the site.
The Committee expressed reservations about the extent of co-ordination of research activities between CSIRO and the Australian Institute of

Marine Science and drew attention to the need to rationalise or define responsibilities ill marine science as recommended by the Senate Standing Committee on Science and the Environment. I am advised by my colleague the Minister for Science and Technology (Mr Thompson) that the Senate Committee's recommendations are currently being considered.
Attention was also drawn to the need for tests to determine the effect on the site of vibration from traffic and shipping and for more detailed master planning. Tests now completed confirm the site is suitable in respect of vibration and even though there are no present plans for further development of the site a future laboratory can be accommodated, if required, together with car parking facilities.
In addition, it was suggested there be further discussion between CSIRO, the Antarctic Division of the Department of Science and Technology and the University of Tasmania to determine the need for a joint large scale experimental tank or aquarium. Such discussions will be arranged.
The Committee also noted that the proposal has the general support of State and local authorities but a formal application for planning approval has not been made to the Hobart City Council. Such an application is not envisaged as it is not the practice for the Commonwealth to seek formal approval for its works from State or local authorities. The Commonwealth, however, makes every endeavour to co-operate with local authorities and to comply with State or local government statutes in a spirit of co-operation.
Finally, I can advise honourable members that acquisition of the site is proceeding on the basis of agreement reached between the Commonwealth and State governments. The estimated cost of the proposed work examined by the Committee was $1O.75m at February 1981 prices. The Committee recommended construction of the work in the reference. If the House agrees to support this motion, detailed planning can proceed in accordance with the recommendations of the Committee.

Second Reading
Mr HOWARD (Bennelong-Treasurer) (4.55)- I move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
This Bill will amend the income tax law in two respects. Firstly, the Bill will give effect to decisions

In deciding to extend this concession to workers in the mining industry and to others similarly
placed in other industries, the Government has
recognised that large numbers of employees are required, in following their employment, to reside
and work in areas remote from large centres of population where they do not have access to

2602 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Income Tax Assessment Bill (No.2)

many of the amenities and conveniences of urban living.
In accepting that the concession should also extend in certain cases to housing provided in non-remote areas, the Government has recognised that in some instances employees have no . realistic alternative other than to occupy accommodation provided by their employers and that the conditions under which such accommodation is occupied are often onerous by reason of factors such as close proximity to the work place.
The concessional basis of valuation for income tax purposes proposed in the Bill will limit the amount to be included in the assessable income of an eligible employee, in respect of benefits granted by way of employer-provided housing, to 10 per cent of the market rental value of the accommodation, less the amount of any rent paid by the employee for use of the accommodation. The Bill also provides that, as a general rule, future changes in the market rental value of accommodation provided to an employee will be geared to annual movements in the rental component of the consumer price index as measured for the capital city of the State in which the accommodation is situated.
I turn now to the criteria set out in the Bill to establish whether employer-provided accommodation is to be regarded as being in an area remote from a major centre of population for these purposes. It is proposed that urban centres that had a population of 12,000 or more at the date of the 1976 census be classed as major population centres and that a location be classed as remote if it is not in, or adjacent to, such a major population centre.
Places of employment and locations of relevant accommodation that are within 100 kilometres by the shortest practicable surface route of the centre of a city shown at the time of the 1976 census to have had a population exceeding 130,000 people or that are within 40 kilometres of a town or city that had at that time a population of 12,000 or more people will be regarded as being adjacent to a major population centre and therefore not qualifying for concessional treatment on the basis of remoteness. Such accommodation may qualify, however, if the alternative tests to which I have referred are satisfied.
The Commissioner of Taxation is also to have power, where all the circumstances warrant it, to treat a person who lives or works in, or in an area adjacent to, a major population centre so defined as living or working outside that area if persons living or working near that person fall to be regarded as being in a remote area under these

rules. This is to obviate different tax results being applied to near neighbours.
The measures proposed by the Bill concerning employee housing benefits will operate from the commencement of the 1977-78 income year and the Commissioner of Taxation will have power to amend assessments made before the provisions come into operation so as to effect any reduction in the value of assessable housing benefits brought about by the new measures. Before I leave this measure, I would like to acknowledge the assistance and representations of a number of my collegues, in particular, the honourable member for Dawson (Mr Braithwaite) and the honourable member for Herbert (Mr Dean), on what has proved to be an extremely difficult matter.
Industrial Dispute Settlement Payments
Honourable members will recall that when the industrial dispute on the Loy Yang power station project was settled late last year, payments made to dismissed employees who returned to work were described and paid, under the settlement agreement, in such a way that no tax was payable on the payments.
The payments which were based on the wages that the employees might have been expected to receive during the period of their dismissal if they had remained at work during that period were declared as being to compensate employees for hardship suffered during the dispute. But in the Government's view the payments were designed principally to induce the employees to return to work, and if they had been so described, or if they had been paid in respect of a period of employment rather than of a period ofdismissal, the payments would have been fully taxable.
At that time. I announced that the Government was examining the question of whether similar payments made in future, should be made subject to tax, and on 11 August 1981 I announced the Government's decision that any such payments made under arrangements entered into after that date would be fully subject to income tax.
In broad terms, the Bill will amend the income tax law to include in the assessable income of a taxpayer any amount paid to him or her under an agreement, arrangement or understanding entered into after 11 August 1981 for the purpose of achieving the result that the taxpayer would, or but for special circumstances would, resume performing work or rendering services. The Bill will also provide that payments made after the end of the month in which this legislation receives the royal assent are to be subject to pay-as-you-earn tax instalment deductions, at a rate to be

Social Services Amendment Bill

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2603

prescribed in the income tax regulations. That nile will be equivalent to the standard rate of perso~al tax-32 per cent for 1981-82. Details of the Bil,l are contained in an explanatory memoran~um that is being circulated to honourable m~1llbers. I commend the Bill to the House.

SOCIAL SERVICES AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 27 October, on motion by MrHunt:
Thallhe Bill be now read a second lime.
Dr BLEWETT (Bonython) (5.4)-First of all, we in the Opposition wish to protest about the introduction of a fairly massive omnibus Bill in the dying days of the session. We were presented with this Bill yesterday, Tuesday, and are required to debate it today, Wednesday. It is not a simple or ashort Bill and, though it is true that most of the proposals in outline have been before us since the Budget, it is extremely difficult under these conditions for the Parliament to give it a proper and considered response. Moreover, it should be pointed out that though this Bill was introduced on 27 October one of the payments in the Billthat is, payments to unemployment beneficiaries 18 years and over without dependants~is to be operative from next Sunday, 1 November, some four to five days later. I want to make it quite clear to the Minister for Transport (Mr Hunt) that the Opposition will not submit to any legislative blackmail in this matter. We are not prepared to deny proper consideration of these measures in both Houses of this Parliament because of that payment which we note, of course, is a retrospective payment. We certainly will be taking action in the other chamber to ensure that we are not compelled to accept what can only be described as a form of legislative blackmail; that is, introducing a measure four or five days before one of its clauses, in relation to payments particularly, becomes operative.
It is also odd that with so belated a Bill-it has taken some time for the Government to prepare the Bill and it is introducing it at the tail end of the parliamentary session-the last nine paragraphs of the Minister's second reading speech is devoted to announcing that the Government still has not got its act together on the effectiveness of the income test for pensioners. Apart from the further evidence of the confusion that this provides in governmental preparation of legislation, I am afraid that those nine paragraphs will provide scant comfort for pensioners over Christmas.

Whatever the Minister's intentions may have been in delivering those paragraphs they still leave very large realms of uncertainty in the minds of many pensioners.
Let me recite the eight major changes and amendments in this Bill. First of all, the Bill changes the supplementary rent assistance system for pensioners and other beneficiaries. Secondly, it increases the related rate of incentive allowance payable to sheltered employment allowees. Thirdly, it provides for the granting of a wife's pension on conditions similar to those applying to the grant of age or invalid pensions. Fourthly, it increases child endowment payable for the third child and subsequent children. Fifthly, it provides for broadening the criteria under which the double orphans' pension is payable in respect to refugee children. Sixthly, it extends the vicious provisions of last year's Budget relating to the non-payment of unemployment benefits to persons involved in industrial action to the spouses of such persons. Seventhly, it prevents the payment of the unemployment benefit to spouses of persons who have failed the work test or are classified as voluntarily unemployed or unemployed due to their own misconduct. Finally, it increases the rate of the unemployment benefit payable to persons aged 18 or over without dependants; that is, those persons who do not receive indexed payments in relation to unemployment.
First of all, the Opposition supports three of these proposals without any immediate qualification. We support the increase in the incentive allowance payable to sheltered employment allowees, though we think the whole system of tying thar allowance to supplementary assistance may have some historical basis but now seems rather odd. Secondly, we support the broadening of the criteria under which double orphans' pensions are payable in respect to refugee children. We support the basic change in the Bill which is to permit parents of a refugee child to be deemed dead if they are living outside Australia or their whereabouts is unknown in order that the double orphans' pension can be paid to the persons responsible for those refugee children. I gather that there is some problem with the definition of a refugee child but I understand that the Government will be correcting it during the Committee stage of this Bill. Finally, we support the granting of wives' pensions under similar conditions to that applying to age or invalid pensions. We think this is an improvement. But there is one problem here to which we draw the Minister's attention. There still seems to be a problem about the status of a wife's pension if, when overseas, she becomes a widow and has the difficulty of securing a widow's

2604 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Social Services Amendment Bill

pension. There still seems to be a gap there. As we understand the legislation, it would require her to return to Australia in order to qualify for that widow's pension. If she is receiving a wife's pension and her husband suddenly dies she is left without support of any kind. That is a point that should be examined. We support those three proposals without any immediate qualification.
On the other hand, the Australian Labor Party is totally opposed to the barbaric principle of denying to spouses otherwise entitled to unemployment benefits such benefits if they are the spouses of persons involved in industrial action or have had unemployment or special benefits refused under the conditions set out in clauses 18 and 21. The Labor Party has always been opposed, and will remain opposed, to the policy of family destitution as a means of industrial discipline. This measure can simply and clearly be described as just that. These penalty provisions are designed to compel destitution on families in order to discipline the industrial work force. We believe it to be a totally improper use of social welfare provisions. As we will be opposing these clauses in Committee, I will leave further argument on that point until that stage.
The Opposition supports the remaining provisions of the Bill but with qualifications, which we have made clear in our second reading amendment. [ will move that amendment in a moment. Before doing so I wish to make clear our position on each of the remaining provisions of the Bill which we support but in regard to which we wish to make qualifications.
First of all I mention what is perhaps the major change in the Bill, the change to the supplementary rent assistance scheme. At the moment some 400,000 persons are receiving supplementary assistance. The Government has estimated that some 300,000 private renters will improve their situation under these provisions. Another 130,000, who are currently receiving supplementary assistance, will continue to receive that assistance under the protective clauses which have been designed in the Bill so that no one at present receiving supplementary assistance will be disadvantaged. That means that something likean additional 30,000 people will be benefiting as a result of this liberalisation. It is true that this estimate was described as simply a guesstimate: I think the phrase used VIas 'broad orders of magnitude', which suggests that there is considerable uncertainty about the actual figure.
The principle of supplementary assistance has been part of our social security system since 1958 when I understand it began with supplementary

rent assistance of $1 a week. It is to be noted th~t this is the first change in supplementary assistance made by the Fraser Government in six years. Nothing has been done in this area in the previol.Js six years although there has been a very strict means test. There was only that $1 free area ana a $1 for $[ withdrawal rate up to $5 a week which meant, of course, that it excluded all but the very poorest pensioners. The number changes in the guesstimates suggest that this will not be expanded very much. But certainly these people have received no help from the first five Fraser Budgets.
One of our worries is that, to our knowledge, there has never been any proper evaluation of the whole supplementary assistance scheme even though major reports have constantly drawn attention to the importance of the whole rent problem as an aspect of poverty in our society. But, as far as we know, no proper evaluation of supplementary assistance has been made. Certainly there is no indication that the changes now being introduced are based on any thorough evaluation of the working of the system.
Of course, we in the Labor Party welcome this liberalisation of the means test. It is a somewhat complicated formula. I know from my constituents that it is causing considerable bewilderment amongst pensioners. I suspect, from some of the comments that have been made around this House over the past 24 hours, that it is also causing some confusion amongst parliamentarians. I stress that it is particularly important that the Department of Social Security devote time, effort, energy and resources to careful and sympathetic explanation of the operations of the means test under the new supplementary assistance scheme.
The objection that the Labor Party has to the new scheme is that we believe it is illiberal to refuse to grant increases in supplementary assistance to tenants in State housing when those tenants are paying the same level of rent as people in private housing. At the moment all tenants in public housing now receiving supplementary assistance will keep that supplementary assistance under the same means test as in the past; that is with a $5 a week limit. But, of course, all new tenants of State housing authorities will receive no assistance at all. It seems to us to be a discrimination where a public tenant paying $20 a week receives no assistance, and a private tenant paying $20 a week under this new scheme receives $5 a week. We recognise that subsidies are built into the public housing system. But when the same amount is taken out of a person's income, whether it be for private or for public housing, we believe that those people should receive equal treatment

Social Services Amendment Bill

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2605

under the schemes designed by the Commonwealth Government. One cannot help suspecting that these provisions are designed to place a squeeze on State housing tenants and, therefore, on the State housing authorities. They are another plITt of the effort to push financial burdens back on to individuals and, of course, often as a concomitant of that, on to the States. We are opposed to that aspect of the scheme which discriminates against and penalises public housing pensioner tenants. We simply evidence cases of people paying exactly the same amounts for public housing as for private housing; yet, in one case-the private one-they receive a supplementary grant and in the other case-the public one-they are deniedthe supplementary grant.
We support, of course, the increase in the unemployment benefit for those 18 years old and over who have no dependants. It is an increase from $53.45 to $58.10 a week. But we note that because of the Government's discrimination against these people-that is, denying them indexation as it is extended to other unemployment beneficiaries-they are now some $11.60 down on what they would receive had they been given fair and proper indexation as it is given to unemployed with dependants. Here I am talking about the base rate for unemployment beneficiaries 18 years of age and over; I am not talking about rates for children and dependants. That rate is now $11.60 a week down on what it would have been had these people been granted indexation. That means that they are something like $603.20 a year down. Given the growing length of unemployment for many of these people that is an important consideration.
Of course, when we talk about this issue we cannot neglect, as the Government neglects, to mention the fact that the unemployed 18 years of age and under still receive $36 a week. That rate has remained unchanged for the six years that this Government has been in office. One can do the basic calculation about how inflation has cut that away. That figure is $37 a week below the poverty line. That amount of$36 a week has remained unchanged ever since this Government came into power. There are something like 53,000 young people receiving that benefit. It is true that the majority of them live at home. But it is increasingly difficult for such people. Often their families themselves are under great financial pressure to make ends meet and the young can contribute little, with the dwindling value of their unemployment benefit.
Finally, I turn to the election promises in regard to the family allowance provisions of the Bill. I remember that, in Launceston, worried about how

the election was going, the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) somewhat effusively promised that oil revenues would be directed to benefit families. It is clear now that the Prime Minister intended that it should benefit only large families. Of course, when he was electioneering he did not inform the people that his promise to transfer oil revenues to families meant they would be transferred to large families only. But we now see in the Budget that these provisions apply only to families with three or more children. No consideration whatsoever has been given to the income levels of families. Low income families get nothing if they have only one or two children. On the statistics, poverty has only a marginal relationship to size of families. I do not deny that there is some marginal relationship between the size of families and living in poverty but, on the whole, it is only a marginal relationship.
These so-called generous proposals-I must admit that the Prime Minister and most of his colleagues have, for years, been telling us that children's allowances are the greatest social benefit bestowed by his Government-will affect only one-quarter of all Australian families. The remaining three-quarters of Australian families with only one or two children have been utterly neglected in these proposals. Of the group which the Government is supposedly most concerned about-that is, the group at the lower end of the scale, the pensioner and unemployed familiesonly 21 per cent will benefit from these extra family allowances. Some 163,000 pensioner families or 79 per cent of pensioner and unemployed families have only one or two children. So this measure is, in fact, not getting to a very significant proportion of the families in society struggling with poverty. It seems that this is a very poor addition to what has been described by this Government constantly as the greatest of its social programs. In this case it has neglected over three-quarters of the families living below or about the poverty line. Therefore, I move:
That all words after 'That' be omitted with a view to substituting the following words:
'whilst not declining to give this Bill a second reading, the House is of the opinion that it should be re-drafted to provide for-
(I) the removal of the discrimination against tenants in government housing;
(2) the removal of the discrimination against the adult unemployed without dependants;
(3) an increase in the unemployment benefit for the under 18 unemployed;
(4) the inclusion of the first and second children in the provision of increased family allowances in line with those for third and later children;

(5) the removal of the provision discriminating against the spouses of union members affected by industrial disputes, and
(6) the removal of discrimination against the spouses of unemployed persons who faU the work test.'
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Armitage)-Is the amendment seconded?
Dr Klugman-I second the amendment.
Mr HODGES (Petrie) (5.22)-1 reject the amendment that has been moved by the honourable member for Bonython (Dr Blewett), and quite predictably so. I intend to take up only the point that he raised in relation to the supplementary rent allowance. He claimed that there had been ~o increase in that allowance in five Budgets. , That IS correct. There has not been an increase in the supplementary allowance over five Budgets. But on the basis of an inflation rate of 10 per cent or thereabouts for the last four or five years, the increase will almost restore, the allowance to its former value. Over the last five years two considerable benefits have been experienced by all people in this country: The rate of inflation has been brought down from the 17 or 18 per cent to which it soared under the Labor Government from 1972 to 1975; and pensioners have received twice yearly indexation, which has been a great boon to them.
Essentially this Bill provides for amendments to the Social Services Act in accordance with announcements in the 19 August Budget. It is not my intention to repeat all of those changes for, apart from being mentioned in the Budget, they ,are outlined in the second reading speech of the Minister for Transport (Mr Hunt). However, there are one or two aspects of the supplementary a,lIowance increase from $5 to $8 a week for rec!pients of age, invalid, wife's and widow's penstOns and supporting parent's and sickness benefits that I'would like to comment upon. The honourable member for Bonython, of course, mentioned some of these issues as well. The supplementary rent allowance will be paid only to beneficiaries on the full rate of benefit and will be tested by the level of rent paid and other income received. A beneficiary will have to be paying more than $10 a week or $520 per annum before some supplementary allowance is payable and will not receive the full $8 weekly or $416 annually until an amount of $26 a week or more is paid in rent. On the other hand, if other income received exceeds $14 weekly, the beneficiary will not be eligible for supplementary rent allowance. In effect, this means that the benefit for supplementary rent allowance has been directed to those most in need by taking into account, on a sliding scale, both the level of rent being paid and

the other income of the beneficiary. Pensioners and beneficiaries who will not be eligible for the supplementary rent allowance are those persons who occupy accommodation owned by the Commonwealth or a State housing authority and who already receive substantial rent subsidies.
Section 18 of the principal Act will be amended by including a definition of government rent. Those persons paying the lower government rent will be excluded under the definition of 'rent' from receiving the rent allowance. I believe this to be a sensible and fair approach. It would seem less than reasonable for pensioners and beneficiaries already paying lower rent in housing authority accommodation to receive a further allowance, while persons on a similar social security pension or benefit and paying rent in private accommodation receive only the supplementary allowance. Sometimes pensioners in private rental accommodation are paying up to $40 or $45 weekly while those in housing authority units pay in the vicinity of $20 weekly. The double benefit of low housing authority rental and a supplementary rent allowance will no longer apply. However, one anomaly will exist for some time. Pensioners who currently receive the supplementary rent allowance and currently occupy low cost pensioner housing will not have the allowance withdrawn. I think that is the point made by the honourable member for Bonython. This could mean that a pensioner who is ineligible for the rental allowance could move into government pensioner housing and find the person next door receiving the rental allowance. Only with the effluxion of time will this anomaly be eliminated.
I point out to honourable members that the age pension pamphlet will become a little more complex with the addition of a further scale of eligi-
bility for the supplementary allowance. At present there is one scale for pensioner entitlement taking into account other income and a further scale for those over the age of 70. Of course, eligibility for the pensioner health benefit card is determined by other income not exceeding $40 for a single pensioner and $68 for a married couple. While the addition of a further scale may prove a little daunting for some, it is the only way to ensure benefits are directed to the needy and, by the introduction of sliding scales, to provide some benefit to the slightly less needy. Of course, failure to have sliding scales will mean abrupt cut-off points and this, I believe, introduces a de-
gree of inequality.
I turn next to the provision of services for the aged. In particular, I refer to domiciliary services which, although not exclusively for aged persons, are nevertheless utilised mainly by them. In this

Social Services Amendment Bill

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2607

current 1981-82 Budget domiciliary services are provided for in the following ways. The sum of $20.6m will be spent on the domiciliary nursing care benefit, $16m on home nursing services and $24.4m on home care services. This makes a total Commonwealth expenditure of $61m. The home care services include home help, senior citizens' centres, paramedical services and delivered meals. State and local government subsidies will amount to approximately $33m. Therefore, the total annual expenditure on these services is about $94m. Extra funds are raised and provided by religious and charitable organisations but, in my estimation, this figure would not exceed $4m or $5m. Home care services, which should be looked upon as a means of keeping elderly people in their home environment and out of more expensive, less attractive and less desirable nursing homes, are funded from all sources to the extent of only approximately $1 OOm annually.
I am not suggesting that hostel and nursing home beds are not required; indeed they are essential. What I am saying is that there is an urgent need to direct more funds to domiciliary services. More emphasis should be placed on providing support services for the elderly in their homes and less emphasis on the provision of institutionalised care. It is my firm view that the great majority of aged persons want to remain independent in their home or home unit environment. Commonwealth

Government subsidies over more than 20 years to religious, charitable and local government bodies have encouraged the establishment of 80,000 beds in the form of self-contained units, hostel and nursing home beds. We have wrongly encouraged too many elderly people to live out their latter days in institutionalised care. We have made dependent many people who would otherwise be independent. At present about 8 per cent of the elderly population are absorbing approximately 90 per cent of expenditure. Whilst it is conceded that many of the remaining 92 per cent do not require assistance, there are large numbers of people in the community who are struggling to support themselves, and with a little in the way of home care services many of them will not need hostel or nursing home beds.
I now turn to institutionalised care of the elderly. Since the inception of the Aged or Disabled Persons Homes Act in 1954 and the Aged Persons Hostels Act in 1972, almost 80,000 beds have been subsidised by the Commonwealth Government. Up to June of this year approximately $538m has been expended on these programs. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard two tables showing beds constructed and grants provided under these Acts.
Leave granted.

Mr HODGES-I thank the House. The number of beds varies slightly from State to State and averages out at almost 26 beds. I turn nowto nursing home beds. In this country nursing homes are categorised as deficit financed which are those operated by religious and charitable organisations, as government, and as private which are those operated by entrepreneurs for profit. Mr HODGES-Although there is considerable variation between the States,in total private enterprise accounts for 55 per cent of nursing homes, and government and deficit financed about 22.5 per cent each. Some years ago an arbitrary figure of 50 nursing home beds per 1,000 persons aged 65 years and over was set as a guideline for Australia. Because certain difficulties were experienced and the granting of ap-
provals was causing a maldistribution of beds, Commonwealth-State co-ordinating committees were established. Regions were determined and the goal to be achieved was to provide in each region 50 beds per 1,000 persons. In my view these committees have been reasonably successful. Their charter to provide 50 beds per 1,000 persons has resulted in the building up of beds in those regions where the number of beds was under the 50 per 1,000 persons. When the number of beds per 1,000 in those regions is added to the bed numbers in regions where the figure is well in excess of the 50, the result is a figure which well exceeds the average of 50. To illustrate my point, I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard a further table.

Leave granted. The table read as follows-
