From: P. M. Last, 'Clinical Rationing' paper delivered at First National Congress of Australian Nursing Homes, Sydney, August 1981.

Mr HODGES-While nursing home beds for some countries are distinctly shown, others are combined with hostel beds. However, a clear picture emerges: Australia is far more generous than similar Western countries. Additionally, it must be remembered that our popUlation, although aging, is younger than most of those countries. In providing nursing home beds, expenditure is not the only consideration. The needs of the aged must be taken into account and those needs may be financial, physical or emotional. However, under our system of recurrent Commonwealth benefit for nursing home beds, expenditure has to be closely watched and value obtained for the taxpayers' dollar spent. Each nursing home bed costs the Commonwealth on average $10,500 annually. For every extra 1,000 beds built the annual bill increases by $10.5m. I believe there ought to be a moratorium on the construction of new nursing home beds and a complete assessment made of the situation. The alternative is to allow only a very limited number to be constructed while the whole position is being assessed. There is a strong case for an increase in domiciliary services to keep aged persons in their home environment. I advocate a substantial increase in services such as home help, home nursing, delivered meals, et cetera. I suggest that a thorough investigation be made of ways to provide more services to the aged at home and also, where possible, to involve iii the delivery of these services the service clubs and the well aged in our community. Careful assessment

for entry to nursing homes and for domiciliary services is absolutely essential. At present the system of assessment is too loose and lacks a degree of uniformity. Some religious and charitable organisations and medicos are careful and apply stringent rules for entry to aged accommodation and for assessment of eligibility for domiciliary services; others are not.
I refer briefly to the State by State variation in nursing home nursing standards. I see no reason why a patient in a nursing home bed in Victoria should be different in any way from one in Queensland or Western Australia or, for that matter, anywhere else in this nation. The Commonwealth benefit, of course, varies from State to State. I believe that this situation should not exist. If States want to impose nursing standards over and above the normal standards set by the Commonwealth I believe they should pay the extra cost. I recognise that operating costs for a given level of nursing will vary from State to State, and indeed within States. Surely major differences in standards is no reason why the Commonwealth should continue to pick up the tab at the whim of a State. There is evidence to suggest that up to 25 per cent of nursing home patients should not be in nursing homes. If this is the case-I believe it isthere is a 25 per cent capacity available right now without providing more beds.
Social security expenditure this financial year will be $11.3 billion. Apart from $4.5 billion of

261 0 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Social Services Amendment Bill

that being provided for pensions for the aged, approximately $600m is being expended on programs for the care of the aged. My point is: There must be careful analysis of these programs. Are they desirable? Are we placing too much emphasis in some directions and insufficient in others? I trust that the Government will take note of some of the suggestions I have made.
Dr KLUGMAN (Prospect) (5.37)-As the honourable member for Bonython (Dr Blewett) indicated, the Australian Labor Party has moved a second reading amendment dealing with a number of the points on which we disagree with the proposed legislation. I shall try to concentrate mainly on family allowances. I was interested to hear the honourable member for Petrie (Mr Hodges) discussing at great length the issue of nursing homes. Whilst in some ways I agree with him I do not think what he said was in any way relevant to the contents of the Social Services Amendment Bill.
I shall deal with family allowances. I make this point: In this country family allowances were preceded by child endowment benefits and by taxation benefits to the income earner who took responsibility for children. This applied before family allowances were introduced in 1976. To put
their values in perspective, it is interesting to note-ignoring the taxation benefit-that in 1941 the child endowment payments for two children represented 6 per cent of the basic wage and 4 per cent of male average weekly earnings. The figures rose to 9 per cent of the basic wage and 5 per cent of male average weekly earnings in 1948 and reached a peak value in 1950 when child endowment for 2 children represented 11 per cent of the basic wage and 7 per cent of male average weekly earnings. The value of child endowment payments was then allowed to erode quite significantly. At some stage there were also tax allowances for dependent children. I think they came in in 1950. They were abolished in 1975 and replaced by rebates. Tax allowances, of course, significantly benefited higher income earners.
I think the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) when Treasurer changed the system from tax allowances to tax rebates. We all know that these benefit, at least relatively, the lower income earner compared with the higher income earner. Let us look at the position as it was in 1950. For a family with two children child endowment represented 7 per cent of male average weekly earnings. The introduction of family allowances by this Government in 1976 and the abolition of tax rebates which we had introduced earlier was heralded as benefiting in particular 800,000 children in 300,000 families who had

been disadvantaged under previous child support schemes. This was a step in the right direction. It was noted by the Government then that in other households men would lose some take home pay but women would gain some of that money through the system of cash transfers; that is, a redistribution of income in the family from men to women would take place. In the accounting system expenditure on the increased value of cash transfers would be offset by the savings on revenue previously foregone through the operation
of tax rebates.
It must be noted that this apparently beneficent family policy was accompanied by substantial cuts in public expenditure in the areas of housing, Aboriginal affairs and health and the decision to make unemployment and sickness benefits and widows and supporting mothers benefits subject to tax. In addition, the introduction of family allowances was used to vindicate a decision in the national wage case in August 1976 not to implement full wage indexation. At the same time the position of pensioners and welfare beneficiaries with children declined in relation to average weekly earnings because of the failure to raise or index children's allowances. Therefore, in a period in which the value of most pensions and benefits rose in relation to average weekly earnings, pensions and benefits for families with children declined in value.
We can look at the actual figures. Since the introduction of family allowances in 1976 their real value has been' eroded by about 50 per cent, as no
decision was made to index or raise the allowance until this partial indexation now. In 1976 the allowance for two children was equal to 5 per cent of male average weekly earnings. For four children the allowance was equal to 11 per cent of male average weekly earnings. At present the value of the allowance for two children is equal to 3.2 per cent or 3.3 per cent of male average weekly earnings. For four children it is equal to between 7.5 per cent and 8 per cent of male average weekly earnings. The value of the allowance has dropped from 11 per cent to below 8 per cent in the case of four children and from 5 per cent to just above 3 per cent in the case of the two-child family.
By not indexing family allowances the Government ensured that the redistributive effects which accompanied their introduction were removed with the passage of time and the rise in the cost of living. If the child tax rebates had remained and had been raised in a similar fashion to the way in which the dependent spouse rebate has been raised in value since 1976 then the Government would have saved about $600m by not indexing

Social Services Amendment Bill

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2611

cash transfers for the support of dependent children. It is not unreasonable to make that sort of calculation since personal tax indexation was introduced at the same time as tax rebates were abolished. I am not suggesting that the tax rebate system represented a more equitable and just means of redistributing income to families with dependent children. On the contrary, for reasons which I mentioned earlier, a cash transfer scheme is manifestly preferable since it does not disadvantage non-taxable low income earners and since some income redistribution can be made to women. What I am suggesting by these calculations is that what appeared initially to be a reasonably generous scheme for child support and a potentially redistributive policy to alleviate some aspects of sex inequality has, in fact, been allowed to wither. It did not become a corner-stone of government policy on priority as it was indicated it would be in 1976.
It must be stressed also that government expenditure for families with dependent children does not end with direct expenditure on family allowances. Certain social services, in particular in the areas of health, housing and education, provide components of what is called the 'social wage' which supplements the direct wage earned in the work place and which constitutes a greater component of the income of low income earners than that of higher income earners. Cuts in expenditure in the areas of health, schooling, housing and community amenities and the accompanying emphasis on the private sector for the provision of services are seen as a significant retreat from the collective funding of social services which previously benefited disadvantaged groups. It has been noted that the measures taken in the thrust towards encouraging health insurance through the private sector-the cutting back of the community health program, the redirection of funding from the public to the private school system and the cutting back of funding for public authority housing-are all erosions of benefits under a variety of programs which served to redistribute benefits to those disadvantaged groups which were named as the main, beneficiaries of the family allowance scheme. The recent devolution to the States of responsibility for funding women's servicesrefuges, health centres, rape crisis centres, et cetera-represents a further erosion of the social wage which explicitly benefited women.
I think we should look at what happens in some other countries, for example, some countries in Europe. Let me give the House some figures showing the value of family allowances in member countries of the European Economic

Community. The figures, which have been calculated by Bradshaw and Piachaud, compare the relative values of child benefits, child tax allowances, et cetera, and the value of family allowances as a proportion of gross average male earnings. The figures show that in 1980 the value of the family allowance as a percentage of gross male average earnings for the two-child family in Australia was 3.7 per cent. In fact it is down to 3.3 per cent now, but it was 3.7 per cent at that time. In Belgium it was 14.1, per cent; in Germany, 6 per cent; in France, 8.4 per cent; in Italy, 3.1 per cent; in Luxembourg, 7.0 per cent; in the Netherlands, 9.1 per cent; and in the United Kingdom, 8.7 per cent. We can see that Australia was lagging behind quite significantly.
The comparative situation of a four-child family in Australia is even worse. The family allowance as a percentage of gross average male earnings in Australia is now below 8 per cent. In Belgium it is 35.5 per cent; in Germany, 22 per cent; in France, 42 per cent; in Italy, 6.2 per cent; in the Netherlands, 21.5 per cent; and in the United Kingdom, 17.3 per cent. The allowance is paid to the mother not only in Australia but also in the United Kingdom, Germany, Denmark and Belgium. I think it is important to note that those countries-I think we normally compare Australia with them as far as social security benefits are concerned-give much greater benefits to families, particularly the low income families, than does (Australia, both in absolute terms and in relative terms. The new French Government, of course, since these figures were compiled, has gone out of its way much further to increase the benefits for families with children.
One of the prominent members of the Social Welfare Research Centre at the University of New South Wales is Mrs Bettina Casso She has produced interesting papers on this question and I have 'used her material. Carol Keens and Gerry Moller also do excellent work at the Centre to try to provide intelligent figures relating to the benefits which are available at present in Australia and the benefits which ought to be available, and for what can be done in Australia and what is done in other countries.
Obviously, I agree with the Government's proposal to increase by approximately 50 per cent the family allowances for the third and subsequent children. This provision is long overdue. Each year when I have spoken in this debate I have asked the Government to bring about such an increase. I am pleased that it has done it in this Budget. It is a great pity that it has not extended this provision further than just to the third and fourth children especially for low income families.

2612 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Social Services Amendment Bill

Many arguments can be used that it is the first child which is the most expensive one for the family because of the way in which it interferes with the ability of the mother to continue earning and the extra cost that is incurred by the family when that first child is born.
I emphasise again that the Opposition feels very strongly that the Bills should be redrafted to provide for the removal of the discrimination against tenants in government housing; the removal of the discrimination against the adult unemployed without dependants; an increase in the unemployment benefit for the unemployed who are under 18 years of age-there has not been such 3n increase since 1975 and prices since then have almost doubled-the inclusion of the first and second children in the provision of increased family allowances in line with those for third and later children, to which I have just referred; the removal of the provision discriminating against the spouses of union members affected by industrial disputes; and the removal of discrimination against the spouses of unemployed persons who fail the work test. I commend to the House the amendment moved by the Opposition.
Dr CASS (Maribyrnong) (5.52)-Before I get around to discussing some of the specific objections that the Opposition has to this legislation I wish to make some comments about the way in which we deal with these problems. I refer firstly to the time at which the legislation has been brought into the House-in literally the dying days of the Parliament. The legislation was presented yesterday. Given its complexity, it is hardly reasonable to claim that we have all had a satisfactory amount of time and opportunity fully and adequately to analyse it. Of course we can make comments on the inadequacies of payments and the failure to keep up with inflation, which I will no doubt do later, as my colleagues who spoke before me have already done.
By approaching the problems of social welfare in this fashion-we tend to do it all the time-we as a parliament never get around to looking at the overall problem which confronts the community in considering social welfare supports. We still look upon them as sorts of privileges, things that we provide for those who are incapable of looking after themselves. It is as if they deserve to be where they are because they are not good enough, but given that it is a little too inhumane to totally ignore them, we will hand them out something by way of charity. I do not think that is appropriate for this day and age. I think society has become far too complex. The pressures are far too great from so many directions. I think the time has come for us to recognise that whether we are rich or poor,

working or not working, healthy or unhealthy, pensioner or handicapped, we are all an integral part of this society. That being the case, I think our approach to social welfare should be quite different. We see it now as a sort of net to catch someone who falls off the tight-rope and cannot save himself. Our approach ought to be to ensure that all, whatever their age, are able to contribute of their maximum to society's needs to benefit society. I put even school children, at their tender age, into the category of those contributing ultimately to society because their education is vital to society's future. I do not see education as a privilege that we give children. It is a need for all of us that they should be educated.
For these reasons my view is that the emphasis of the whole web of the social security system. should be changed from the sort of propping-up approach to the provision of a broad basis for living, whatever our status may be, so that we can provide as best we can for the benefit of society, gain from society and enjoy life. After all, what is the point and purpose of society if it is not to enable people to survive and to live to some purpose as they perceive it? It should not be se.en as some sort of torture one goes through from birth until death. I can see that what I have suggested requires a totally different approach to social welfare from that which we adopt at the moment. At the moment it is seen in terms of party politics. The conservatives have a particular view which tends, to my mind, to be rather inhuman. They see social welfare more as a charity which is provided out of the generosity of those who are working and who, therefore, are able to pay the taxes to provide it, whereas we on this side see it more as a right simply by virtue of a person being a human being and we do not see it as a fault that a person does not contribute as much as others.
How do we overcome the difficulty, given that we have this polarisation and the party political approach? If I recall correctly, a few days ago the honourable member for Prospect (Dr Klugman) was proposing the use of the committee system to overcome some sort of problem. The Parliament ought to try to grapple with the problems of this very critical area by putting, say, the question of a social security system to a committee of the Parliament-not after all of us in this place have determined our attitudes and policies and have campaigned in election campaigns on what we will or will not do, but in recognition of the fact that we need a new approach. Let us put the problem to a committee of the Parliament before we have stated what we think.-Let that committee inquire from the whole community about what the approach of most citizens in the community today

Social Services Amendment Bill

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES2613

might be to this whole complex question. Let the committee then report back on the sorts of problems I am talking about. We never get round to considering those problems because we are too preoccupied with trying to buy votes, whether it be by cutting back on public expenditure because we think that is the in thing at the moment and that somehow public expenditure is the cause of all our problems or whether it be because, when the tragedies loom too large, the pendulum of the conscience of the community, particularly of the middle class who are comfortable enough, swings round so that society says: 'We should do more for the underprivileged'. Of course, then a party such as the Labor Party gains more political support because that is the sort of line that it tends to propose and people come to doubt the words of a Liberal Party when it tries to do the same sort of thing. We have to get out of that avenue of party political bickering and try to analyse what society should be providing for people, whatever their station, to ensure that they do riot become a burden, are best able to live, can enjoy life and can make some contribution to society.
In the closing moment or two before the sitting is suspended for the meal break, I will start to deal with some of these questions. The very first one, of course, is the proposal in our amendment to redraft the Bill to provide for the removal of discrimination against tenants in government housing. I am not quite sure how many people really will benefit from the Government's proposal because I cannot quite understand the figures provided in the second reading speech of the Minister for Transport (Mr Hunt), but the estimate given by the honourable member for Bonython (Dr Blewett), our spokesman in this area, is that a mere 30,000 people may in fact gain from the liberalisation proposed by the Government.
Sitting suspended from 6 to 8 p.m.
Dr CASS-Before the dinner break I had commenced to discuss some of the details of the Opposition's amendment to this legislation and was about to discuss the question of removal of discrimination against tenants in government housing. Of course, this again illustrates the idiocy of this piecemeal approach to the problem. Quite clearly, the extra money that will be provided will go straight into the pockets of the people who provide the rented housing. The Minister says:
Rents in this range are still well below market rates. In addition, because a subsidy rate of $1 for each $ I of rent paid may encourage landlords to increase rents in line with any increase in supplementary assistance, the rate of subsidy has been changed to one-half of rent above $ 10 a week. This is intended to reduce incentives to increase rent while increasing assistance to pensioners paying higher rents.

Who is kidding who? What will happen is that the money will go directly into the pockets of the owners of the accommodation, who will simply raise their rents sufficiently to soak it all up automatically. Pensioners and people dependent on this sort of support will be no better off. Providing these funds for the private sector and not providing any support for people in government housing, just aggravates the problem. The real solution to these problems is to build more public housing so that more and more of these unfortunate individuals can avail themselves of subsidised State and local government housing and not be dependent upon private housing.
I come now to the question of our suggested amendment to have the Bill redrafted to permit an increase in unemployment benefit for unemployed under-18-year-olds. Again we see the cynicism of this Government. The level of unemployment benefit for young people who are leaving school from about 15 to 18 years of age has not changed since 1975. It is at the level at which the last Labor Government pegged it, despite all the increases that have taken place since then in the cost of living. Of course it can be argued that these people are not much more than children and their parents ought to take responsibility for them. In real terms, for most of the young people-who are in this situation that is an unreal prospect. Most of those affected by this problem of being unable to get employment are ill-equipped to find jobs. They have left school early. Perhaps they come from homes where the breadwinner has difficulty in finding employment or is even unemployed. The Government automatically assumes that parents can look after their children. In many socio-economic groups the reality is that young people of this age need to get out and find jobs and work not just to help themselves but in many cases to help their families. We are going to punish them for being born poor. With this legislation we are going to leave such young people below the poverty line and dependent still upon parents who have far too little with which to care adequately for them.
I wish to deal next with the inclusion of the first and second children in the provision of increased family allowances, in line with those for third and subsequent children. Of course, we do not object to the increase in allowances for the children. However, the point is that over 70 per cent of Australian families have fewer than three children, so over 70 per cent of families are excluded. We are excluding them at the time when the family is just beginning, when the young people are just setting up house. Presumably they still are not secure or advanced in their jobs, so they have the

2614 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Social Services Amendment Bill

least resource backing of any time in their lives with which to settle themselves, and establish their homes and families. Yet this so-called generous Liberal Government is excluding them and, for all practical purposes, will forever eyclude them because, as I say, the vast majority of Australian families have fewer than three children.
Mr Sainsbury-They don't need it so much.
Dr CASS-The honourable member opposite said that they do not need to suffer. I presume all they have to do is breed like rabbits. The fact is that, given their situation, they are in a position where they are less secure than at any other time in their lives. Yet we are being niggardly and not giving them any support at all. Finally the provision which discriminates against the spouses of union members affected by industrial disputes is again another measure of the inhumanity of the Liberal Government. It is exerting pressure on union members who do not go on strike irresponsibly. We hear a lot of talk about unionists who go on strike for any reason, but that is not the real situation. We are talking about working people who never have very much in the way of resources. Their wages are not bountiful. They go on strike because they feel they have a very real grievance. We need only to look at the record of industrial disputation in this country and the final resolution of most of those disputes. Often they get to the Australian Conciliation and Arbitration Commission only because the unions have taken industrial action. They have put in their claims and waited weeks and months and sometimes even years. Finally they have gone on strike and only then does the case get to arbitration. Most of the time, after a case has been to arbitration, after the painful exercise of industrial power in the form of strike, after abuse is heaped upon the trade unionists, the court finds in favour of the trade unions. They get most of what they have been seeking.
So the reality is that this is another move by the Government to exert pressure on the trade union movement, to muzzle the trade unions, to try to stop them from seeking their rights. In my view, that is all the trade unions ever try to do. No one in his right mind who has a family will go on strike just for fun, when he has little or no savings in the bank. People go on strike because they feel desperately that they need to, and now we are putting the screws on them. At least the Labor Government acknowledged that the family should not be made to suffer, and so we provided

a hardly bountiful provision in the form of unemployment benefit or special benefit. The legislation is being amended so that the wife of a person on strike can claim only the single benefit, not the married benefit. The Minister has stated:
The present legislation permits a situation in which trade unions can choose to strike knowing that through the operation of the social security system their members will be largely insulated from the effect of their actions.
The insulation, the miserable single benefit, is hardly compensation for the effort put in by the trade unionists who go on strike for what they consider to be their rights. The Minister also stated:
There have been numerous cases where wives of strikers who have appeared to have no genuine intention of joining the work force have registered for work in places such as mining and construction towns where there is no real possibility of employment for them.
For goodness sake, what a cynical comment that is! What are the wives supposed to do? Lie down and die? Of course that is one of the difficulties which face wives who live in construction towns. That is the reason why there are so many social problems. There is nothing for the wives of the workers in those towns to do. That is a real problem.
On top of that, when men in those areas feel that they have to go on strike, to come up with that sort of observation is the height of cynicism, in my view. The fact is that if work were available those wives would go to work. It is not their fault that they live in a situation where there is no prospect of work. 1 deplore the miserable attitude of this Liberal Government, and for those reasons endorse the amendments moved by the Labor Opposition.
(Quorum formed).
Mr HUNT (Gwydir-Minister for Transport) (8.12)-1 will reply briefly to a couple of points that were raised by the honourable member for Bonython (Dr Blewett). Firstly, he felt that there was discrimination against pensioners living in government houses who would not benefit from the supplementary allowance. The issue he raised was that supplementary assistance will be paid if a private landlord imposes a rent of $20 but not in the case of a house that is the property of a public authority. Generally, public housing authorities already provide a subsidy of $16 per week. Thus, a house rented for $20 in the public sector would have a rental of at least $36 in the private sector and, therefore, could be expected to be of a much higher standard.
The other point the honourable member raised related to a wife's pension. He referred to the case of a couple residing oversrqs where the husband is

Social Services Amendment Bill

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2615

an aged or invalid pensioner. The Department of Social Security has legal advice from the AUorney-Genera\'s Department that it is not possible to grant a widow's pension in those circumstances. A widow's pension may be granted if a woman returns to Australia with the intention of residing here and is able to meet the residence qualifications. It is my understanding that the circumstances referred to are relatively rare. The Minister for Social Security (Senator Chaney) is aware of the situation and is currently having the position examined.
The honourable member for Maribyrnong (Dr Cass) referred to the spouses of those who are involved in a strike. The present legislation permits a situation in which trade unions can choose to strike knowing that through the operation of the social security system their members will be largely insulated from the effects of their action. The Australian public, for its part, is attacked on both fronts-the inconvenience of the strike on the one hand and, on the other hand, having to pay through taxes and through the system for spouses who may choose to use a loophole in the system. There is not the slightest doubt that very few countries provide for this situation. I do not think there is another country which does so. I will stand corrected if there is.
Mr Scholes-The United Kingdom does.
Mr HUNT-It does not. There is no good reason why the community should automatically and immediately be expected to assume the normal financial responsibilities of those who are not willing to work and who voluntarily, without good reason, give up their jobs or are dismissed because of their conduct. This is happening at present. The spouses of such persons register for employment in their own right. If they are unable to obtain it they attract unemployment benefit for themselves and their dependent spouses. I think it is important to impose a sense of responsibility on people in the community. Australia has undoubtedly one of the worst industrial records in the whole world. The Opposition is asking the Government to continue to preside over a situation in which social welfare, which comes out of the pockets of the taxpayers, is able to be channelled through to the households of those who are engaged in industrial action. If people are really sincere in trying to achieve their industrial' objectives they should not expect the taxpayers of Australia to bear the cost.
Amendment negatived.
Original question resolved in the affirmative.

Clause 3 (Interpretation).
Dr BLEWETT (Bonython) (8.18)-We oppose clause 3, which imposes a specific definition of government rent, therefore providing a means test in relation to private rent but not in relation to government rent. The arguments against this provision I developed in my speech in the second reading debate. Let me take up the one point made in reply by the Minister for Transport (Mr Hunt). He suggested-this, of course, is open for debate-that a pensioner paying $20 in a public rental area and one paying $20 in a private rental area might not be comparable because of the subsidies underwriting the public area. The significant issue is not whether the pensioners are renting in a public or private area but that they are both paying the same amounts of their limited incomes for rental accommodation. One may have been more fortunate in some ways in securing government accommodation, though that itself is debatable, but they are paying the same amounts of their limited incomes. In that situation both should be entitled to similar treatment in relation to the means test provisions. Therefore, we are opposed to clause 3 which defines a special type of rent, that is rent paid to certain governmental authorities, as not being subject to a means test or to any of the benefits in future under the supplementary assistance provisions.
Another problem has been caused-the honourable member for Petrie (Mr Hodges) alluded to it-because the Government, in an effort to wriggle its way through the problems it is creating by having a division of the means test, by applying it to private rent and not to public rent, has had to make the concession that all those people at present paying public rent will retain the rights they now possess. Of course we agree with that but it means that in public rental, cheek to jowl, there will be quite marked contrasts in the way in which people are treated. We think that those sorts of inconsistencies and anomalies are the product of a most unsatisfactory way of tackling this problem; that is, to try to say: 'Well, we are going to provide support for those who pay private rent but those who pay rent to a public authority, even if it be exactly the same amount of rent or, in some cases, even more rent, will receive no Commonwealth support through the supplementary assistance scheme'. Therefore, we are opposed to this particular clause.

2616 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Social Services Amendment Bill

Mrs CHILD (Henty) (8.22)-The Government pointed out in the second reading speech of the Minister for Transport (Mr Hunt) that it does recognise that low income pensioners paying unsubsidised rents are among the most needy in the community, and I would certainly agree with that. However, along with my colleagues, I am opposed to just one more instance of discrimination by this Government between low income groups. Under clause 3, supplementary assistance will not be paid to those renting government flats and houses. It means that we now set up two types of people, perhaps living in the same block of flats and possibly on the same floor. They could be next door to each other. The one who moves in to government housing before I February 1982 will get the lower rent plus the subsidy and the one who moves in after I February will not receive any subsidy. I think this is blatantly unfair.
This is the Government that has singled out the housing industry to bear the full brunt of its regressive policies. Not content with cutting the grants to the States for the provision of welfare housing, this Government has now introduced a sales tax on all components of the building industry, which is on its knees anyway. Over 80,000 people throughout Australia are waiting for subsidised housing, although we cannot be really sure of the figures because of the Government's failure to provide regular detailed information.
Until such time as we are able to provide adequate low rent housing for the thousands who are desperate for it, this Government should not act to set up two sets of people-two sets of low income people, two sets of pensioners, two sets of people living in the same block of flats. One group gets the subsidy and the other is deprived of it. The principle involved is wrong. Furthermore, every time we raise the subsidy for those renting private flats and houses the landlords put up the rent. At leasLwe have some cOhtrol over government flats and houses and we know that the subsidy goes to the right person. This Government must concentrate on a housing policy, a planned housing policy, to meet the needs of the thousands of desperate families and put aside its ad hoc, band-aid attitude to housing. It is unfair, it is discriminatory and it will not solve any of the problems.
Clause agreed to.
Clauses 4 to 17-by leave-taken together, and agreed to.
Clause 18 (Unemployment benefits).
Mrs CHILD (Henty) (8.25)- We are opposed to clause 18. We are opposed to the provisions

within this Bill which preclude the payment of unemployment benefits to the spouse of a person involved in industrial action or whose union is involved in industrial action. As my colleague the honourable member for Bonython (Dr Blewett) pointed out, to use the Social Services Act to penalise strikers is highly improper. This Government seems dedicated to punitive action and lacks flexibility when assessing the unemployed.
I do not quite know what the Government is trying to do. How does it expect people to live? It is strange to hear the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) prating in this chamber about the need for a free trade union movement in Poland while every action taken, every word spoken in this chamber, attacks the trade union movement and its freedom to operate. We still have the right to strike in this country and the Department of Social Security should not be used as a weapon to counter strike action. The Department of Social Security should not be used to threaten and punish individual strikers. Unionists and their spouses are taxpayers too. The Minister for Transport (Mr Hunt) said in his second readingspeech:
The present legislation permits a situation in which trade unions can choose to strike knowing that through the operation of the social security system their members will be largely insulated from the effect of their actions. The Australian public for its part is attacked from two directions. First, it has to hear the personal disruptions, inconveniences and associated cost brought about by the strike. In addition, it is then called upon to meet the cost of providing income support for the families of those involved in that industrial action.
I would like to point out to the Committee that unionists and their spouses are also members of the Australian public. They are also taxpayers. They subscribe to the unemployment benefits paid to others and they should not be denied the right to apply successfully for unemployment benefits, each in his own right, not with one being precluded because the other is on strike. The children of unionists and the children of strikers have to eat, the same as everybody else. I teU this Government: Fight your industrial battles in the correct arena and get yourself a better Minister to help you fight them. Leave the Department of Social Security to provide the support to which all Australians should have access.
Mr SCHOLES (Corio) (8.27)-This piece of legislation, I suppose, is the ultimate in hypocrisy. Some time ago the Government legislated to deal with the situation of persons who were unemployed as a result of industrial action within their own organisation. It did not, at that stage, deal with the position of spouses. I want to bring to the attention of the Committee a specific case which currently exists whereby, under this legislation,

Social Services Amendment Bill

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2617

unemployment benefits will be denied to spouses and also will not be available to persons who are disadvantaged because of a lack of income through being stood down as a result of an industrial dispute.
The Government keeps saying that if people choose to take industrial action there is then a case whereby the Government should refuse to provide financial support for that action. That is arguable, and we argued against such legislation some time ago. There is currently a dispute in Victoria involving the Geelong and Broadmeadows plants of the Ford Motor Co. In the case of Broadmeadows, the employees continued an industrial dispute contrary to the directions of their union; and I make that point to start with-contrary to the directions of their union. Under legislation passed by this Parliament some time ago, if the union took any action to discipline the employees acting contrary to the directions of their union, it would be subject to punitive action under Commonwealth law in the industrial court. So the union's position is, under laws established by this Government, that whilst it can recommend that members comply with its executive decisions, it cannot compel them to do so. So the dispute continues and the Government prohibits the union from having any authority over those who are currently involved in an industrial dispute within that union.
The employees at the Gee\ong plant of the Ford Motor Co. voted not to strike. They refused to participate in the strike. That was an action they took, of their own volition, in what they considered to be their own best interests. They are, under the legislation passed by this Parliament earlier, denied unemployment benefits because their union is involved in an industrial dispute, despite the union's recommending a return to work-a recommendation for a return to work which the union is unable to enforce because of laws which this Government has passed to prevent the union executive exerting any authority over its members. This Government has put into the laws punitive measures to prevent that action. We now have a situation where not only are those people who voted not to go on strike and, therefore, acted in a way in. which I would have thought that even this Minister and this Government would have approved, to be punished, but also their families are to be denied unemployment benefits under an Act of this Parliament.

Government has passed legislation which prevents unions from directing their members to go on strike. When the union acts in the opposite way the consequence is still the same. The Government is now passing legislation to deny the unemployment benefit not only to members of a union but also to the spouses of members of a union who do not wish to go on strike, members of a union which may suggest that a strike should not continue and who may wish to work. The Government has taken power of control from the unions. I hope it is not suggesting that members should resign from a union for three or four days in order to meet some criteria. Whatever the reasons the Government may be putting forward for this legislation or the reasons it put forward for existing industrial legislation, it is totally inconsistent. If in fact the Government is pursuing any form of policy it is incompetent.
Dr BLEWETT (Bonython) (8.32)-This clause embodies the latest Liberal Party principle-family destitution as a system of industrial discipline. We oppose this clause because it makes use of social welfare provisions to impose penalties on the families of unionists involved in, or affected by, industrial disputes. In addition, of course, this clause provides a penalty on families whose breadwinner fails the work test. The Minister for Transport (Mr Hunt), who is seated at the table, argued during his second reading speech, and again when he spoke tonight, that the operation of the social security system as it now stands largely insulated union members from the effects of their actions when there was a strike. Of course, it is quite understandable that Ministers of this Government would not understand the problems that are caused for the families of strikers despite all of the social security provisions that exist in this country.
It is simply not true that under the provisions at the moment strikers' families are in any sense effectively insulated from the effects of a strike on their family incomes even if they get some social service provision. There is no doubt that for nearly every family in such a situation the living standards drop each day the strike is prolonged. Of course, very few strikers in our society choose that course of industrial action in a light-hearted or thoughtless manner. The Government's social welfare sanctions are already quite severe, even though some social welfare benefits are available to families involved in strike action.

I ask honourable members opposite: Exactly Honourable members opposite should take as what sorts of criteria are being laid down? The an example the present provision about which

2618 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Social Services Amendment Bill

they boast. The present provision is that the spouse of a person who is affected by a strike or the striker himself may take the single unemployment benefit. If honourable members opposite want to get up in this House and say that that support in any way successfully insulates most families against the loss of regular wages then I suggest that they look at the kinds of family budgets that most working people have to face, In fact, the decision to rely on that limited social security sup-
port usually means that there are real problems in paying mortgages and in meeting all of those commitments which were made when there was an assurance of a sustained wage. Moreover, since the 1979 amendments to this legislation this Government has already extended the punitive provisions by including unionists in another industry or even in another State affected by a particular strike. However distant the original dispute, unionists who belong to the union involved and are stood down as a result of that strike, whether it be an entirely different industry or in an entirely different State, are brought within these punitive sanctions. Under the legislation that this Government is trying to pass tonight it intends to bring the spouses of those unionists also within that sanction.
Until this measure was introduced a spouse, generally a wife of a striking or stood down unionist, was able to claim the unemployment or special benefit at a single rate if she cOlild not find work to help the family. In that situation she was able
to get the benefit at the single rate. As I have suggested, even in that situation there were inevitable difficulties with commitments presupposed on regular weekly incomes.
That reliance on the single rate of the unemployment benefit for the spouse was no solution to the financial difficulties of working class families. But even that inadequate solution is now to be ended. Wives will not be able to apply for the unemployment benefit and the family will be left without support. Thus the generosity of this splendid, humane government! For the last six months the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) has been talking about his Government having a human face. I suppose that there is one provision in this measure which is designed to sustain that image. In clause 21 there is a provision for starving unionists: 'In case of extreme hardship and where there are no other means of support something will be done for the family'. When the Department of Social Security is given a provision like that it means a pretty bleak prospect for these families except in cases of extreme hardship where there is no other means of support. I believe that my description of this Government's

policy as being a policy of family destitution in order to achieve industrial discipline is fairly and correctly taken. In many ways, its policy is simply a declaration of war on the families of those who are involved in any way in a strike.
But, of course, the clause does not finish there. In addition, there are similar provisions introduced to punish families of those who are either voluntarily unemployed or are unemployed due to their own misconduct, or who have failed the work test. Basically, all of those are work test provisions. In all of those cases people have failed the work test.
I no longer think it worthwhile appealing to the morality of the Liberal Party. But perhaps it is possible to appeal to the Government's pragmatism. First of all, it is a minuscule problem upon which this Government is using this great hammer. If honourable members look at the number of people who have failed the work test, only 1.6 per cent of all terminations of unemployment benefit were due to failures of the work test; that is, an average of 1,000 postponements a month under the work test. As something like 70 per cent of the unemployed have no dependants, these draconian measures do not affect them. This measure is being introduced in part to deal with 300 families a month. Three hundred families a month have to be punished for some failure in the case of their breadwinner. That first point is simply a pragmatic point and not a moral one. It is a minuscule problem.
Secondly, in something like half of the cases of people who have failed the work test it is found that that is due to error by the Department of Social Security. In 1979-80 halfof all the appeals against social security decisions were finalised iri favour of the appellants. The great majority of these appeals was in the unemployment area. In many cases these quite harsh sanctions will be taken against families although the original cause of these sanctions will frequently be a departmental error. I hope that there are sufficient people on the Government benches, even if not concerned with morality, who will look at the minuscule size of the problem and the way in which this Government is treating it. Secondly, they should look at the degree of error that has been made in these assessments and the kinds of real family costs that are imposed as a result of social security errors.

REPATRIATION ACTS AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 27 October, on motion by Mr Thomson:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Dr BLEWETT (Bonython) (8.50)-The first thing I would like to say about the Repatriation Acts Amendment Bill 1981 is similar to what I said about the Social Services Amendment Bill 1981. It is extremely disappointing tliat, in the last stages of this session of the Parliament, and at very short notice, we are presented with an omnibus Bill which involves considerable changes and that we are given something like a day in which to prepare our response. I believe that the Minister for Science and Technology (Mr Thomson), who is at the table, is one of the few honourable Ministers on the Government side and I am quite sure that he is equally disappointed with the way in which the Government chooses to deal with measures of this nature. This Bill makes changes which, in a sense, have been awaited by many people for over a quarter of a century. I would have thought that changes of that significance would deserve somewhat more than the cursory treatment which the Government is giving them. Indeed, it has taken over a generation for Parliament to take this action in relation to merchant marines and the decisions relating to disability provisions for those who supported Australia in New Guinea. The kind of contempt which the Government has for this quite serious legislation is demonstrated by the fact that the Government hurriedly introduced it last night and expects it to be debated tonight. It is extremely difficult to respond effectively under those circumstances.
The Bill, which is an omnibus Bill, contains two sets of provisions-those which give effect to a

2620 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Repatriation Acts Amendment Bill

number of Budget decisions and those which contain operational and machinery amendments which I think the Minister described as designed to modernise the repatriation legislation in this country. I will give the general response of the Opposition to the changes. There is a total of six major Budget implementation changes. Firstly, there is the decision to provide service pensions to cover Australian mariners who served in the 1939-45 war. Of course, they have had to wait something like 40 years to receive this response. Secondly, repatriation benefits are to be given to those who served in peace observation and monitoring forces under the auspices of the United Nations or a recognised international body. These benefits will apply to those involved in such services from the end of this year.
Thirdly, disability pensions are to be provided to former members of the Royal Papuan Constabulary and the New Guinea Police Force involved in service with our allies in New Guinea during the war. Fourthly, the Bill raises the limits on disability pensions insofar as these are used in determining service pensions and fringe benefit eligibility. Fifthly, the Bill raises pensions for those who have suffered amputations and loss of vision, and it increases many other similar repatriation benefits. Finally, under Budget implementation, the Bill increases the supplementary rent allowance along similar lines to those contained in the Social Services Amendment Bill 1981. The second section of the Bill, the operational machinery part, is designed to correct, clarify and modernise-the phrase used in the Minister's speech-the drafting of the Act.
I want to deal very briefly with the Opposition's response to each of these proposed changes. First of all, we support unequivocally the provision of disability pensions to former members of the Royal Papuan Constabulary and New Guinea Police Force involved in the Second World War. All J can say is that I am not sure that many people are left to receive those benefits after something like 40 years. But at least it is a belated recognition by this Government and this country of the rights of, and the responsibilities and the obligations we have to, those people who served Australian soldiers so very well in the New Guinea campaigns. We on this side of the House thoroughly endorse the decision that has been made, however belatedly. Secondly, we support the raising from 50 per cent to 60 per cent of the limit on the disability pension with regard to the determination of service pension and fringe benefit eligibility. It is a change about which we have no qualifications. Thirdly, we endorse witho'ut qualification the raising of pension provisions

for a whole series of disabilities suffered through war.
However, we do wish to make some comments upon the other three changes. Indeed, our second reading amendment will relate to these other three Budget implementation provisions. The first relates to the increase in the supplementary rent allowance along lines similar to that contained in the Social Services Amendment Bill. We are opposed to these changes for exactly the same reason as we opposed the similar changes in the Social Services Amendment Bill. We believe that it is improper and inappropriate to make distinctions between those pensioners who pay rents in private accommodation and those pensioners who pay rent in government subsidised accommodation when the same amount of rent is involved. Equally we believe it is unfair and discriminatory for ex-servicemen who pay rent in private accommodation to be treated differently from ex-servicemen who pay rent in government accommodation. Our objections to that section of the implementation provisions are the same.
Secondly, we are concerned with the provisions relating to the participation of Australians in peacekeeping and observation monitoring forces of the United Nations and other recognised international bodies. Our concern is with the extremely limited nature of the coverage of that provision in the sense that it operates only from this year. I would like the Minister to give some explanation of why Australians who in the relatively immediate past served in Kashmir, in Cyprus or the Middle East are not entitled to these benefits. Why is there no retrospective element for those who served in similar forces in the immediate past? I realise that there is always a problem of defining exactly when that past period should be. Australians have contributed significantly in the immediate past to a number of peacekeeping forces. I would welcome an explanation from the Minister as to why a date in advance is being used and no effort is being made to make these provisions retrospective.
It is also rather ironic that, according to the Minister's statement, the contemplated Sinai peacekeeping force cannot be encompassed within the generous terminology of the Bill. I presume that even this Government is forced to recognise that the Sinai peacekeeping force is neither a force under the auspices of the UN or of any other recognised international body. It does seem rather ironic that, after this week of posturing by the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) about the international nature of the Sinai peacekeeping force, he cannot even include that force within the ambit of this legislation. That

Repatriation Acts Amendment Bill

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2621

says perhaps as much about the Minister preparing this legislation as it does about the posturing of the Prime Minister.
The final Budget implementation provision which we are concerned about is the limitation relating to the provision of service pensions to Australian merchant mariners from the Second World War. We certainly support, and have supported for some time, the coverage of Australian mariners involved in merchant seamen activities during the Second World War. There is no doubt that Australian merchant seamen have been grossly discriminated against in the 35 years since the end of the war. Many of them joined the merchant marine at the age of 15 or 16 because they were too young for any other service at that time. But, once they were signed on, they were in for the duration of the war because many of them were simply refused permission to join any other service. Because of the essential nature of their jobs,
merchant seamen took the brunt of the unglamourous and hazardous task of ferrying troops and transporting essential fuel, food and ammunition supplies throughout the war. One in eight of Australian merchant seamen serving in Australian ships between 1939 and 1945 was killed or lost at sea while one in three serving in British and allied merchant ships worldwide was torpedoed, bombed or mined. For their pains, the survivors could wear war service medals but were not entitled to any service pension.
I think those merchant seamen who played such a vital role in the Second World War in theatres of war should have the same rights as others who served in similar conditions but who happened to be members of the military forces. I do not think there is any division between the political parties on this. I readily recognise that there have been members on both sides of the House who have actively fought for a number of years to ensure that these men have the same rights, if rather belatedly. I suspect that after the war has been past for over a generation the numbers who will benefit from this will be somewhat limited. We certainly support these provisions. We believe that all Allied servicemen should be included. We believe that all Allied merchant men should be included; that is, all of our allies who also served on
merchant ships on the Allied side during the war and were in theatres of war. Fundamentally we argue this simply on the grounds of the logic already established under the Repatriation Act. Last year it was decided, again after 40 years, that Allied servicemen would be entitled to repatriation benefits similar to those of Australian servicemen. One would have thought that when the Government came to face this particular issue,

which is a relatively small issue compared with provisions for Allied servicemen in general, generosity might have struck even this Government. I know that is a high expectation but one might have thought that, given that there are very few Allied merchant men in this country, it would have been quite possible for the Governrnent to incorporate a provision to cover Allied merchant men in exactly the same way as last year it was able to cover Allied servicemen. I know that I was deeply disillusioned when at an Estimates committee I dared to suggest this provision for the few thousand men involved and it was suggested that they might have to wait 40 years as the Allied servicemen had to wait 40 years for their benefits. If that is so I should imagine there will be none there to secure them.
I am disappointed also because I find that an enormous number of members on the other side have been publicly active and vocal in support of this provision. I wish to mention here a few quotes from Government members enthusing for this support but apparently unable to persuade the Cabinet of the wisdom of this minor generosity. The honourable member for Bradfield (Mr Connolly) is reported to have written:

Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER-Is the amendment seconded?
Mr SCHOLES (Corio) (9.7)-1 second the amendment. There are a number of aspects to the Repatriation Acts Amendment Bill which are of concern. There are some general aspects of the whole area of repatriation which I think it is time this House faced up to. Some years ago we had a review of the Repatriation Acts. In fact we had two. One review was by a Senate committee. It took six months to bring down substantially the same recommendations as a committee of inquiry headed by a judge which took six years. So I am not suggesting that we appoint another judge.
There is some doubt whether the Repatriation Act in its present form covers the likely modern consequences of any armed conflict and the types of injuries which could occur. I briefly mention in passing the debate which has been going on fo~ some time about exposure to agent orange, and what I see as the real deficiency in the Act; that is, that subsequent injuries to second or subsequent generations cannot in any way be covered by compensation where those injuries can be reasonably established as having been caused by the service of one of the parents, or the presence of one of the parents, in an area of war. I do not want to proceed with that but I believe that it is time the Repatriation Act was extended to cover consequences where a person bears a deficiency or a deformity or some other disability because of an injury of a parent or the exposure of a parent to some form of warfare which could have genetic or generation jump effects.
This Bill extends service pensions. I think, unfortunately, it follows a long pattern of legislation which has been coming before the Parliament. On each occasion where a change is made legislation has increased rather than reduced the anomalies which exist in the legislation. There is and has

Repatriation Acts Amendment Bill

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2623

been-I think all members are aware-a considerable campaign by those people who were in the British occupation forces in Japan, and numbers of other members of the Services who served in various areas during the Second World War, who, because of the designations of 'ex-service' or 'service' for service pensions, are currently denied access to such pensions. The definitions of ex-servicemen in the Defence 'Service Homes Act are different from those which apply in this Bill and in other Acts of this Parliament relative to service in war zones and war service. Persons who were in areas where military activities took place are denied access to repatriation or war service home benefits because of certain aspects of the various Acts.
I believe that there is a strong case for a complete examination of the rationale. It is now a long time since the end of the Second World War. The persons who participated would not have realised the type of Acts which existed. I think it is also true to say that there is a motivation towards early retirement in the community, firstly because people are realising that leisure is not evil and, secondly, because pressure on the employment market is bringing about an attitude whereby people are prepared to retire at the age of 60 years. I think there is a good case for extending service pension benefits to those people who were available for service in war zones and who volunteered to take part in such service during the Second World War at least.
I wish to deal briefly with the anomaly as I see it in the peacekeeping operation situation. Firstly, the Bill, as I understand it, is to apply only to future operations and not to the Sinai operation unless an amendment is subsequently made to the Act. This Bill provides for international forces or United Nations forces and the Sinai force would not meet either of those criteria, as I understand the situation. Australia has taken part in a number of these types of operations. I have already mentioned the occupation forces in Japan. At least some members of the forces there would have been at some risk, probably a risk not dissimilar to that which occurred in certain aspects of military activities. There are very vulnerable areas of military activities-even in war zones-and some very safe activities. I do not distinguish between those, but there are some places where the level of injury is more likely to be of a non-military nature than of a military nature.

from 1950 to 1953 but there were Australian combat elements in Korea until 1957. We had observer groups in the India-Pakistan-Kashmir area and at least some injuries occurred to servicemen during that period. We were part of observer teams in the Middle East. We have had Australian observers in the Middle East at various times since 1956. I refer to the United States disengagement observer force at the Golan Heights in 1974 and the United Nations emergency force for disengagement in the Sinai in 1973. We provided helicopter crews, ground crews and other sections of the Australian forces in Ismalia in 1976. Since 1964 the Australian Government has been providing civilian police forces in the Cyprus area. I think it would be unreasonable not to consider extending the Bill to cover the activities of these persons, who are volunteers and who are acting as a peacekeeping force in the same way as military personnel. Even though it is by nature a civilain force its members are still performing the functions of a peacekeeping force. Australians participated in the Malaysia-Borneo operation. Service in Vietnam is not uniformly accepted for service pension eligibility. Australian servicemen have been deployed in Papua New Guinea and Vanuatu in circumstances where some forms of military action took place or could have taken place.
In all of those cases forces were at risk. In some of those cases service pension eligibility may well have been established. Certainly, no additional establishment of service pension eligibility will derive from this Bill to persons in that situation. It again breaks up the designation of service to one's country in military forces in areas where some forms of combat may take place. Other Australian activities are not generally covered by this designation. For instance, Australian forces served in limited numbers with the Americans in Cambodia. The former members of those forces currently have great difficulty in establishing any form of criteria as ex-service personnel because they do not come within the right dates and other criteria. Australian nurses who served in the Second World War have had great difficulty in qualifying for service pensions in a number of circumstances even though they were probably at as great a risk as many of the servicemen and women-mainly servicemen-who did qualify. The anomaly with regard to service in the merchant area is being exaggerated further. Again, that is a matter which could be cleared up.

The first of Australia's post-war peacekeeping The piecemeal approach, to the service pension operations took place in Indonesia from 1948 to especially, is becoming almost a serial. I do not 195 I. Australia participated in the Korean war want to deal with the other aspects of the Bill. I

Repatriation Acts Amendment Bill

think that if this Government believes that Australian servicemen perform a function which is above and beyond the normal requirements of the community and face risks, whether it be in noncombat or combat situations, or are prepared to face risks, it has an obligation to make sure that they gain the maximum benefit from the applications of this legislation. Unfortunately, this piece of legislation adds to the anomalies. Every time we move in a sideways or lateral direction without taking into account the consequences of the new anomalies which we create we add to the problems rather than reduce them. Every member of this House, when the terms of this Bill become known, will be confronted with more requests from people who believe they are being unjustly denied. This creates a situation of dissatisfaction. In this case the legislation does not add at all to the list of people who are eligible or qualified to receive service pensions. It merely says that in future, and subject to the alterations in relation to the Sinai, anyone who serves in the manner described will be qualified but that those who served in exactly the same manner in the past and who would currently be eligible for a pension will be denied that pension.
Mr LEO McLEAY (Grayndler) (9.18)-1 wish only briefly to address myself to the Repatriation Acts Amendment Bill. I think that the thrust of this Bill is too little too late. As previous speakers have said, the Government should have proposed many of these amendments many years ago. The Australian merchant mariners have waited since the Second World War for their rightful due for the very dangerous service that they provided to our country during that War. Most of them now are no longer entitled to many of the benefits that they would have received if this legislation had been provided to them when it should have been. People in the merchant marine, for instance, who were involved in the resupply organisations in the Islands, probably were in even more dangerous circumstances than people in the regular Australian Navy or allied navies. They had more ships sunk from under them than were sunk in the regular navies. However they have not been entitled to this benefit until now. I think that is a most disgraceful set of circumstances.
The Government has perpetrated those disgraceful circumstances in the Bill before the House. The proposal to entitle people who are serving in peacekeeping forces to the provisions of this Bill after 1 November 1981 is disgraceful. I was recently in Cyprus, where a number of Australian Federal policemen are involved in a peacekeeping mission. Australian policemen have

been there for nearly 10 years now and have suffered some casualties and a great deal of discomfort in representing their Government and their country. None of the people serving in Cyprus now are entitled to receive benefits until this Bill is passed, when they will have an entitlement from 1 November 1981. Indeed, Superintendent Beck of the New South Wales Police, who has received some significant publicity in recent weeks for his activities as a policeman in New South Wales, served in that force and will not be entitled to the benefits provided by this Bill -because he will not be serving in it on 1 November 1981. All those people who have served in the Cyprus contingent, the Kashmir contingent, the Golan Heights contingent and the Indonesia contingent will not be entitled to benefits although the government of the day said that they should serve on behalf of their country. Unfortunately this Government has decided that it will conveniently forget those people. I think that is disgraceful.
I think people who represent their country overseas, whether or not the general population of the country agrees with what they are doing, should be entitled to all the benefits that other people who have done that sort of work are entitled to. I think in many ways the Returned Services League was rather tardy in its support of the merchant marine. It was only in recent years, when it became suitable for the RSL, that it took the side of the merchant mariners of this country in their quest for justice. Once again in this Bill the Government has relegated to nothing the effort of a number of people during the war. Indeed, it is questionable whether those people who served in British merchant marine contingents will receive benefits under this Bill. Prior to the Second World War most of the ships plying the Australian coast and the international routes to Australia were British registered ships. People who served on those ships and who tried to enlist in the Australian Imperial Force but were turned back because they were in the merchant marine will not be entitled t6 benefits under this legislation. Although it is very late, they should be entitled to those benefits. I think it is nothing short of scandalous that the Government, even at this late date, has not been willing to accept its responsibility to the people who did their best at the time. I think to do what it has done to the people involved in peacekeeping forces since the war is rather reprehensible.
The Government has provided in this Bill no repatriation benefits for the people who will be involved in the Government's latest foreign policy escapade in the Sinai. The Minister for Transport

(Mr Hunt) said in his second reading speech that supplementary legislation will be introduced into the Parliament. I think this Government's record on repatriation benefits to Australian ex-servicemen and to people who were involved in what one would term the areas of responsibility of the department responsible for repatriation has not been consistent with what all Australians would expect their government to be doing.
It is very interesting to consider the costs of the programs that we are looking at. On 10 September the honourable member for Prospect (Dr Klugman) asked the Minister for Science and Technology (Mr Thomson), who represents the Veterans' Affairs (Senator Messner), a question upon notice about the cost of providing extensions of Service pensions for British and Allied merchant seamen who served in theatres of war. That encompasses the whole range of British and Allied servicemen who served in theatres of war. In this Bill the Government has seen fit to exclude some of those people. It is very interesting to consider the magnificent cost of providing that range of benefits to that population of people! The additional cost is a paltry $1.5m per annum. I think it is terrible that the Australian Government is not willing to expend a measly $1.5m per annum on people who risked their lives to see that Australia had war materiel and other materials provided during the war. I refer in particular to those servicemen and Allied seamen who provided resupply missions in the islands.
In recent amendments to the Repatriation Act we quite properly widened the criteria for Allied servicemen who may receive benefits, but the Government was too miserly to widen the criteria for those people who served in a non-combatant role. I have no doubt that the heart of the Minister for Science and Technology, who is at the table, who served his country well in the Services and who unfortunately has carriage of this Bill in the House tonight, would go out to those people who risked their lives to supply the Australian servicemen whom he commanded in many areas. I must say that I have a small axe to grind this evening in that my father was in the Australian merchant marine and received nothing. Most of the merchant marine did not expect to receive anything, but I think the too little too late that the Government is offering them is not even a

little-it is absolutely nothing. It is an insult to those people who did their best for their country at a time of great crisis.
Mr Milton-They were the forgotten people.
Mr LEO McLEAY-They were; that is right. As my colleague has said, the Government's proposals are iniquitous. We should provide for all Allied ex-merchant mariners the same eligibility for Service pensions as isprovided for Allied ex-servicemen. That is the only proper thing this Parliament should do. The discrimination against ex-servicemen and Allied ex-merchant mariners who now live in government housing should be opposed by all members of this Parliament.
When one considers the rhetoric put forward in this country after the Second World War and the rhetoric that was directed to those people who served in the Australian Army in Vietnam, for instance, one should consider the approach taken by the Government tonight as nothing short of hypocrisy. aged. The delays have been brought about partly by the Nancy Law case which has been before the courts for a long, long while and has now been upheld by the High Court of Australia. The question of the onus of proof has been very decisively determined, and it lies basically on the Government, not on the servicemen. Of course, that case relates to men who first started smoking whilst in the Services and who subsequently contracted lung cancer.
Quite apart from the Nancy Law case there is. another case, the name ofwhich does not come to me at the moment, which is also before the courts..

It also touches on the two issues of 'an occurrence' and onus of proof. I recollect issuing a Press statement well back into last year-unfortunately it was not printed-because I had had word that the Government, if it lost these cases, intended to
amend the Act to overcome any decisions in favour of ex-service men and women, as in the case of Nancy Law and the other case, after they had gone right through the courts-through the Administrative Appeals Tribunal, the Federal Court of Australia, the High Court. Ultimately, it means that ex-servicemen will lose out because the Government will simply amend the Act to get around the decisions of the courts.

women-
