INDEMNITY OF PRINCIPAL PARLIAMENTARY REPORTER AND COMMONWEALTH GOVERNMENT
PRINTER
Mr SPEAKER~The honourable member for Prospect has indicated to me that he wishes my indulgence to raise an issue. I invite the honourable gentleman to do so.
Dr KLUGMAN (Prospect)~Today for the first time, when I received my 'greens'~the transcript of the speech I made in this House~two pieces of paper were attached to them. The first is signed James W. Roberts, Principal Parliamentary Reporter. Amongst other things it states:
IF printed copies of the speech are required, please complete the lower portion of this form and return to the Hansard office.
The lower part of the form which is headed 'Application for Copies of Speeches', inter alia, states:
In consideration of the supply of these copies of the reprint I agree to keep the Principal Parliamentary Reporter and his staff and the Commonwealth Government Printer and his staff indemnified against all actions proceedings claims or demands made against them by reason of any defamatory matter contained in the said reprint, and against all costs damages or expenses in respect of such action proceedings claim or demand.
We are supposed to sign this form. I signed my copy of the form as I had spoken on the Social Services Amendment Bill and my speech was not likely to lead to any actions. Mr Speaker, what is the reason for the change? If there is a good reason for it should the whole question be referred to the Privileges Committee of the Parliament if there is some legal doubt?
Mr SPEAKER~The honourable gentleman is quite right to raise the matter. So far as I am aware today is the first day on which the forms have been distributed. It is done under my instructions. Let me dispose of the last point first. I think it would be a misuse of the Privileges Committee to ask it for a legal opinion about the matter 'so I put that point aside. However, I indicate to the honourable gentleman that for some time the question of the scope of privilege has been a matter of concern to me. It has also been a matter of concern to the Principal Parliamentary Reporter and the Government Printer. The Principal Parliamentary Reporter who reports and arranges for the printing and the Government Printer who actually prints are capable of being sued personally. If a libel action arose out of a libellous statement it may be alleged that the Government Printer and the Principal Parliamentary Reporter were liable for damages.
The honourable gentleman has asked me to answer him on this matter and I must say that I

2628 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Indemnity ofReporter and Printer

am inhibited in giving a full and frank reply. The reason I am inhibited in giving a full and frank reply is that I do not want to instigate a course of action which may be to our disadvantage or to the disadvantage of certain honourable members. It would not be proper for me, representing all members, to encourage that course of action. Therefore, I am being very circumscribed in what I have to say. I have had the opportunity to explain to the honourable member for Prospect the sorts of things that are in my mind. I am rather hesitant, unless pressed, to go further than I have. I am anxious to ensure protection for the Principal Parliamentary Reporter and the Government Printer. The paper the honourable gentleman is asked to sign relates to the provision, without charge to honourable members, of a certain number of copies. I think it is 35.

The opinions that the Attorney has given, in some instances in private, if tested could bring into question the rights of honourable members. NATIONAL HEALTH AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 27 October, on motion by Mr MacKellar:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Dr BLEWETT (Bonython) (IO.O)-Unlike the other two Bills introduced by tlie Government yesterday, which it has compelled this House to debate today because of the chaotic state of its legislative preparation, l:lt least the National Health Amendment Bill is a small Bill and, therefore, has an advantage over those two omnibus

Bills with which we have had to deal at very short notice. It contains two quite distinct amendments in the pharmaceutical field. The first change is what I would call a confessional change; it is to correct an error in the hastily drafted legislation of May 1981. The second change is a further extension of the user pay principle; that is, it raises the patient contribution for items under the Pharmaceutical Benefits Scheme from the present $2.75 per prescription to $3.20 per prescription.
I wish to deal with each of the two parts of the Bill. First of all I deal with what I call the confessional part of the Bill. Although there is very little sign of any confessional element in the second reading speech of the Minister for Health (Mr MacKellar)-1 did not expect it-if we examine clauses 4, 5, 6 and 7 in this Bill they are simply the result of a ministerial boo-boo. That error was the result of the hasty and hurried way in which major health legislation was rushed through the House in May and June of this year. Of CO).lrse, the Government recognised that its rather shabby health legislation would not survive critical scrutiny in a Senate which it did not control. The result was rushed, hasty legislation, full of holes and anomalies which are now beginning to surface.
The anomaly which this Bill seeks to correct is the first of the fruits of that rushed legislation. I can certainly promise the House that it assuredly will not be the last. The anomaly which this Bill seeks to correct arose in the following way: The Government abolished the hospital cost-sharing agreements with all States except South Australia and Tasmania. Having abandoned those cost sharing agreements it paid to the States a grant which included an amount towards the cost to the hospitals in the States of supplying pharmaceuticals to all patients. So the cost-sharing agreements were ended but the States were paid for pharmaceutical provisions in public hospitals. In its hurry the Government overlooked-simply forgot, or made some other error-the fact that the ending of cost sharing reactivated section 99 of the Health Insurance Act under which State public hospitals could claim reimbursement from the Commonwealth for their expenditure on pharmaceutical benefits.
So here we have this idiotic Government, out to save money with its new health scheme, faced with the problem of having to pay money twice over. Having already paid the money to the States for pharmaceuticals under the arrangements it had made with the States in May and June, it was faced with a possibility of duplicated claims for those same moneys from State public hospitals. I do not quite know when the error was discovered.

National Health Amendment Bill

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2631

Presumably it was some time in early September. Certainly an announcement was made by the Acting Minister for Health (Senator Peter Baume) in mid-September a fortnight after the scheme was in operation. As I have said before in this House, the Acting Minister for Health, unlike the existing Minister for Health, has a certain frankness and honesty about his statements. I wish to quote the confession of error because I think it makes it much clearer than the Minister's second reading speech. The Acting Minister for Health said this:
In States where those agreements are no longer in force it is now open to those hospitals which have been approved to again claim reimbursement. . . . if taken advantage of would, in the Government's view, represent a duplication of funding as regards pharmaceutical benefits.
When the Act was originally drafted no provision was made for such an eventuality and approvals of hospitals to dispense pharmaceutical benefits cannot be revoked in such circumstances.
In order to avoid any excess payment by the Commonwealth, the Government intends to amend the National Health Act at the earliest opportunity to empower the Minister to revoke the approvals.
The Bill we have before us tonight is the result of this discovery. The amendments are designed to empower the Minister to revoke approvals to hospital authorities for the purpose of supplying pharmaceutical benefits. The Opposition will not oppose these amendments.
As we will have more of these amendments, I wish to make the Opposition's position perfectly clear. The Opposition remains implacably opposed to the health scheme introduced on 1 September. In the months remaining to this Government we will support the correction of the worst anomalies and absurdities in the scheme for two reasons; firstly, because such anomalies simply compound the present mess and impose even greater hardships on the Australian people and, secondly, as in this case because they would impose unjustified burdens on the Australian taxpayer. As I have said, this is the first fruit of illprepared legislation and there certainly will be more anomalies to follow.
In the time which is available to me tonight I wish to take up one of the next anomalies that I think the Government will have to do something about soon; that is, the predicament of overseas students studying in Australia. Private overseas students require, as a condition of entry, a financiaI guarantee which in most cases is provided by a parent or patron residing overseas. Because of this guarantee the private overseas student is regarded as wholly or substantially dependent on a resident of a country other than Australia. Therefore, he is not eligible for the health care card. Of course, the difficulty for such students is

that, unlike the similarly dependent Australian student, his parents or guarantors do not have the splendid opportunity to participate in our new health scheme and, therefore, provide cover for them in the way in which many parents provide cover for their children undertaking tertiary education. But, of course, most of these people are overseas and, therefore, do not get an opportunity to participate directly in the Australian private health insurance system. Thus such private foreign students are required to take out private health insurance in a way in which their similarly dependent Australian contemporaries do not have to. It is simply part of the family cover that
the parent takes out.
But perhaps more seriously placed, because their financial resources are often less, are foreign students sponsored by the Commonwealth, State or local government authorities in Australia. In this context of local government authorities are included universities and colleges of advanced education. So it refers to students sponsored in terms of scholarships in universities and colleges of advanced education. By the very fact of that sponsorship such students are exluded from the health care card. Whatever their income situation, which is often directly comparable with Australian students possessing the health care card, they will be refused a health care card on the grounds of that legal sponsorship.
I have many examples but I think the classic example is that of a young Malaysian student, married with two children, receiving a payment of $135 a week, partly as a result of scholarship and partly as a result of teaching. This man received through the post four health care cards. There was enormous eagerness to present him with health care cards and he presented all four cards that he received through the post. This was followed by a letter informing him that because of his sponsorship he was not entitled to a health care card. His comment on the system I think is one that we should preserve and one which we will certainly keep. He said: 'I came to Australia to study higher mathematics, not the complexities of your health insurance system'.
Finally, I mention one other problem. Even those foreign students who might evade all of these legal requirements and are entitled to a health care card cannot receive it until they have been resident in Australia for six months. I want to know from the Minister what happens to them in the first six months of their studies. Are they treated as immigrants? Is an adjustment made and are these students treated as immigrants for those first six months? That is a third problem in relation to overseas students. Thus today many

2632 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

National Health Amendment Bill

hundreds of overseas students in this country are fully entitled to a health care card on grounds of low income and residence but they are being denied those cards because of the legal interpretations of sponsorship and guaranteeship. So we have the rather ironic situation that while the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) postured at the Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting many overseas students from those very Commonwealth countries were battling to secure access to health facilities in one of the wealthiest of the Commonwealth countries represented at CHOGM.
I turn now to the second element of the Bill, leaving behind the correction of anomalies. No doubt we will have much more time to deal with those anomalies in this Parliament. The second element of the Bill which we oppose in this House, which I believe will be opposed in the other House also, is the proposal to increase the patient contribution for prescriptions under the Pharmaceutical Benefits Scheme from $2.75 to $3.20. The Opposition opposes this increase and will oppose clause 3 in the Committee stage of the Bill. This is a strange interpretation clause but, nevertheless, that is the way in which this provision has been made effective. I wish briefly to state to the House our reasons for opposing this increase. First of all, in the six years of this Government the patient contribution has more than doubled, rising from $1.50 in 1976 to the proposed $3.20 in 1981. That is a rise of 113 per cent. In that time the consumer price index has risen by 65 per cent, so that, under this Government, the increase in the patient contribution has been almost twice the rate of the CPI rise In that period.
As with every other aspect of health, the Government has shifted the burden of the cost of pharmaceuticals from government to individuals. In 1975 the patient contribution represented 54 per cent of the average cost of items under the Pharmaceutical Benefits Scheme. Today it represents 70 per cent of the average cost of items under the Pharmaceutical Benefits Scheme. The second set of reasons why we oppose this legislation concerns the difficulties which are already flowing from the $2.75 contribution and which will be simply compounded by this increase. Firstly, persons with multiple prescriptions for a single incident-often a single medical incident requires a number of prescriptions for each of which the patient contribution has to be made-or families with a number of prescriptions for the children of the family are in particular difficulties. Every pharmacist in this country can give examples of patients coming to the pharmacy

with a number of scripts and asking the pharmacist which is the most necessary as they can afford only one; Certainly, in the contacts I have had with pharmacists I think practically every pharmacist I have spoken to has given me examples of that problem. Of course, that difficulty will be increased particularly forpoorer families with multiple prescriptions for a single incident or a number of prescriptions because of the numbers in the family.
Secondly, the increase will add to the difficulties and problems of the chronically ill. The Government has often pretended in its rhetoric to be concerned with the problems of the chronically ill. But it does not, in practice, seem to have given any particular attention to this form of social disadvantage. The problems of these people will be simply compounded by this increase. There has been no effort, for instance, by the Government-I thought it would be possible-to moderate the impact of the increase on the chronically ill.
Thirdly, the increase in patient contribution charges highlights another anomaly in the health scheme which I presume we may also get some action on in the next 12 months. That anomaly is that the holders of pensioner health cards are entitled to free pharmaceuticals but the holders of health care cards are not entitled to free pharmaceuticals. What is the reason for this discrimination? I see that the honourable member for Petrie (Mr Hodges) is nodding his head. Perhaps we will get an explanation from him. First of all, the income levels for health care cards are approximately the same as those for pensioner health benefit cards. I am prepared to agree that there are some differences. But the income levels for pensioner health benefit cards closely overlap the income levels for the health care cards. So, roughly similar income levels are the subject of different policies and different values are accorded the cards in the pharmaceutical field. It seems to be very difficult, even for this Minister, to argue that many of the unemployed, many of those on special benefits and many of the low income earners are not as badly off and are not as socially disadvantaged as those with pensioner health benefit cards; yet they do not receive the same entitlements under health care card as the pensioner health benefit card people receive. Health care card holders secure no pharmaceutical benefits. This is an anomaly which needs explanation. We, on this side of the House, look forward to the various efforts to explain this anomaly or this discrepancy.
Of course, the philosophy behind clause 3 is, once again, the user-pays philosophy. Like all

National Health Amendment Bill

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2633

examples of health changes at the point of delivery, it is fairly clear what its impact will be. It will have very little impact on the overall demand for pharmaceuticals. But it will add greater burdens for those least able to bear them, many of whom, in this case, have been specifically designated by the Government in its latest health plan as disadvantaged in the health area. That will almost certainly be the result. There will be very little effect-perhaps a marginal effect-on the demand for pharmaceuticals, but certainly quite severe burdens on those least able to bear them, particularly people identified by this Government as socially disadvantaged.
In conclusion, the whole issue of pharmaceutical provision in this country needs to be fully debated, instead of bits of pharmaceutical legislation being trundled into the Parliament in this manner at the end of the session. This is not untypical of the handling of pharmaceutical issues by this Government. They tend to get put at the tail end of health legislation. That usually means that they get trundled in at the last moment in bits and pieces. For instance, we have yet to hear of the Government's final decision on the Ralph report, although the pharmaceutical industry assumes that the report is dead-consigned to that limbo especially reserved by this Government for troublesome reports. As usual, it is a pretty expensive dead letter, but it shares that in common with many other expensive reports commissioned and then ignored by this Government. It is another example of the gross discourtesy shown to able and dedicated people who agree to work on these inquiries and then have their wo.rk relegated and forgotten. I think the Minister's problems are mounting in the pharmaceutical field but perhaps he will ensure, in the new year, that the whole issue of pharmaceutical provisions iri this society is debated in a general way. We do not oppose the motion for the second reading of this Bill because we recognise the need to correct the anomaly which it is the major task of this Bill to correct. But we will oppose clause 3 in the Committee stage as it imposes higher patient contributions for pharmaceutical benefits.
Mr HODGES (Petrie) (1O.16)-The honourable member for Bonython (Dr Blewett) during the earlier part of his speech made some rather uncharitable comments. I do not wish to make particular reference to those. Indeed, they were quite contrary to his nature. But one matter about which I amin agreement with him is his claim that health care card holders should be entitled to some concession when they take prescriptions to

chemists to have them dispensed and have to pay in full what will now be $3.20 per item. In previous debates in this House I have advocated some form of subsidised or concessional pharmaceutical benefits scheme for disadvantaged persons-low income earners. In this instance, the Government has instituted a system whereby health care card holders-that is, the lower income earners other than the pensioners-can obtain treatment from their local doctor at no cost, provided the doctor bulk bills and is satisfied with 85 per cent of the common fee. Yet, those people may well be faced with a number of charges at the rate of $3.20 a prescription when they leave the doctor and make their way to the pharmacist.
It is not uncommon for families to have two or more members with chronic illnesses. It is not uncommon also for a doctor to prescribe three and four items for a patient. At $3.20 an item it is easy to see how, with two or three chronically ill patients in a family, the health care card holderwho, of course, is the lower income earner-can go to the doctor at no cost at all and yet find himself up for $20, $30 or $40 when he takes the prescriptions to his local chemist. Some form of concessional or subsidised pharmaceutical benefits scheme has been advocated by the Pharmacy Guild of Australia. I support the honourable member for Bonython in his request to have some such scheme implemented.
I do not want to take up much time of the House but I want to make reference, as did the honourable member for Bonython, to the changes in patient contributions over many years and also make comparisons with movements in the consumer price index. I want to go back a little further than did the honourable member for Bonython. I draw the attention of the House to the fact that the patient contribution in March 1960 was 50c and it has risen to $1, $1.50, $2, $2.50, $2.75 and, from December this year, to $3.20. As a percentage of the prescription cost, the patient contribution has risen from 28 per cent in March 1960 to 65 per cent in December this year. If we take the index at 100 in March 1960, there has been a 540 per cent increase in the patient contribution up to December of this year. The consumer price index has risen by 283.1 per cent and the patient contribution as a percentage of the prescription cost has risen by 175.3 per cent. They are considerable increases. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard the table setting out those figures.

Mr HODGES-I thank the House. The other point I wish to raise concerns the new pensioner health benefit card, which indeed is very similar to the previous pensioner health benefit card which had been in existence for a number of years, and also the new health care card. I want to raise some of the difficulties that can arise with both of these cards. About three million Australians are eligible to have the pensioner health benefit card and the health care card. When aperson carrying one of these cards visits the doctor no immediate check is made by the doctor as to when the patient last saw a doctor. There is no charge to the pensioner patient for a visit to the doctor. The doctor bulk bills in most cases; if he does not the pensioner patient may register with a registered health insurance fund. Therefore, it costs the pensioner patient nothing when he visits a doctor. Also, there is no' charge for the pensioner patient when he visits the pharmacist. But because of the present design of the pensioner health benefit card there is absolutely nothing to prevent a pensioner patient who holds such a card from visiting a doctor a dozen times in a matter of days, or indeed a dozen different doctors in one day. Such a person can obtain prescriptions for considerable supplies of drugs and, having obtained those prescriptions, it is a simple matter for the patient to visit a number of different pharmacists to have the prescriptions filled. I suggest that in those circumstances nobody would be suspicious.
If the pensioner health benefit card which existed in 1972 were still in vogue-it went out some time in the early 1970s; I am not sure of the exact date-at least the doctor concerned at the time of presentation of the card would immediately notice the dates of a patient's previous visits to doctors and thus the frequency of visits. The old card recorded the medical attendances of the pensioner patient at a doctor's surgery. There

was space on the card to record the date and the doctor's initials. There was a.certain safeguard. At present the system is wide open to abuse. The current PHB card records optometrical consultations which of course would not occur very frequently-certainly not as frequently as visits to the doctor.
What baffles me is why the card does not have visits to doctors recorded on it. If the date of visit and doctor's initials were recorded it would be a deterrent to the patient to overvisit the doctor and overindulge in the taking of drugs. Additionally, it would serve to alert the doctor if the patient were abusing the system. It would also act as a deterrent to doctors not to see patients too frequently or unnecessarily. Without a date or doctor's initials there is no immediate record of visits for patients who are trekking around to different doctors. Fortunately, of course, the vast majority of pensioner patients would not be involved in excesses but, with more than one million holders of PHB cards, the system cannot afford to be loose or open to abuse.
In the case of health care card holders, the sam:e position exists with regard to free visits to the doctor. There is nothing to prevent a health care card holder from visiting one dozen or more doctors on the one day. If obtaining supplies of drugs is the motive the health care card holder will have to pay for his supplies at the pharmacist at the rate today of $2.75 or after 1 December of $3.20 for each item. If he is successful in obtaining the drugs for which he has a market, it might well be worth his while to pay the $3.20 contribution to the chemist.
The PHB card and health care card have been made large enough to include columns for date and doctor's initials. I see no good reason why this information should not be included on them. As I

National Health Amendment Bill

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2635

pointed out, their inclusion would reduce abuse of the system. I ask the Minister for Health (Mr MacKellar) to examine this matter because I believe that a number of people in our community who are out to abuse the system will be able to do so. It may be questioned whether this sort of thing ought to be highlighted here or indeed anywhere. The fact is that people will find ways to abuse the system. I can see no reason, given the size of the cards and the previous design of the PHB card, why we cannot institute for both PHB cards and health care cards the system which existed in the early 1970s.
Dr CASS (Maribyrnong) (10.27)-The contribution of the honourable member fOI Petrie .(Mr Hodges) highlights some comments I made earlier on the Social Services Amendment Bill which we were discussing before dinner. It is a very good example of how much the Government loses when it presents legislation in this fashion. It loses the wisdom of even its own back bench, let alone the contributions that we might make. I do not happen to agree with the reasons given by the honourable member for Petrie for overutilisation. The point I make is that he has quite clearly indicated an area of concern which should have been considered. I do not expect the Minister for Health (Mr MacKellar) to be a genius or God and therefore to be able to encompass all these possibilities; no one can. That is all the more reason why we ought to take a more rational approach to problems such as this. The honourable member could have said that the Government has a problem because the cost of pharmaceutical benefits is enormous, which it is, or because we as a community are consuming far too many drugs. That is true; we are all drug addicts. We should therefore look into the problem.
The Government approached .it specifically from the financial point of view and has tried to stop the excesses by imposing a higher charge. I
cannot accuse it of that without at the same time conceding that when we were in government we took somewhat the same approach. Our remedy was not as extreme but we also raised the contribution paid by the patient in order to try to cut down the cost to the community of drugs. I happen to think that that is a totally wrong approach. We cannot consider the question properly in the House. My speech in response to the honourable member will not register in the way that my points could be registered if we were in a small committee discussing the issue with the Minister and calling upon people, such as pharmacists, to shed light on the problem. I simply make the observation on a seemingly trifling point, which draws only four people into the House-that

shows how insignificant it is-but which illustrates a very fundamental weakness in the way in which we approach legislation.
I am sorry that my colleague the honourable member for Petrie and I are not able to be-on a parliamentary committee discussing this issue with the Minister and trying to solve the problem, which I see as being excessive consumption of drugs. I take issue with the honourable member in trying to analyse what the causes of this excessive consumption of drugs might be. He suggests that, because we do not have these, I think quite sensible, requirements for entering on to a card when patients visit doctors and so on, some patients will make it a habit of visiting three or four doctors and getting batches of prescriptions. Of course, if they are pensioners they do not have to pay for them. They can get the drugs. I, personally, do not believe that that is how people, except for a very small minority who may be drug addicts, behave. Registering them in that way does not necessarily solve the problem.
The patients who are getting special benefit cards because they are underprivileged but are not pensioners, still have to pay for their drugs. Again, I do not believe that they will bother to go to a doctor for nothing to get a whole range of prescriptions and then trot off to pay for the drugs. I do not think that happens very often at all. However, the statistics certainly show that we are enormous pill poppers in this country. Compared with any other country, barring perhaps America, we are way up in front. I think this relates to the whole way in which medicine is practised. I had better be careful beca~se I do not want to blame doctors. Let me change the words. This all relates to the system under which doctors practise their medicine in this country and the incentives built into the system which in a way encourage doctors, in my view, to-overtreat and overpreScribe. I do not expect the Minister to agree with me. Some of us have been saying this for a while. This includes not just people on this side of the House; academics have been saying it. It is interesting to note that in recent weeks even the Australian Medical Association has been stirred to institute two inquiries into the utilisation of services~one relates, I think, to hysterectomies and the other to tonsillectomies-to try to prove or disprove--
Mr MacKellar-And appendectomies too, I think.
Dr CASS-It does not matter. A couple of surveys have been made of a range of hospitals to try to ascertain whether the claim that there is overutilisation of these sorts of services is true. Of course, the medical profession officially believes

2636 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

National Health Amendment Bill

that this is not happening. There are other members of the medical profession-Professor Lou Opit for one-who agree. Some may say: 'Well, he is a bit of a nut because he says those sorts of things over and over'. He is basing his .findings on statistics that are published. For example, some were provided by Marlene Lugg who was at the time. I do not know whether she still is-a statistician in Western Australia. Some were published, I think, by Dr McEwin-I am not sure; it is somebody to do with the health services in New South Wales who has published data from the Medibank computer showing the rates of operations even in country towns varying enormously for simple things such as tonsillectomy.
The drug problem relates to the same sort of thing; it is no different. For this reason I think this approach is doomed to fail. It will not succeed. True, the Government is going to charge patients more. Sadly, in cases where the drugs are really necessary people unable to afford to pay for them will suffer. My own view is-I give it not as the policy of the Australian Labor Party but one which I think we should try to work towards-we need some system of medical practice whereby drugs are prescribed only when they are really necessary. That involves analysing the system under which medicine is practised. I think there is evidence to show that in certain situations, for instance under salaried medical services, there is less inclination to prescribe drugs so freely. There is no suggestion that therefore many patients die because they do not get drugs. I think the evidence suggests that there is a lower consumption of drugs and services and, on analysing the results, that the quality of the health care provided is better. The Minister may dismiss that as my prejudiced view.
I opened up these remarks with a comment that we ought to have a committee of the House or the Parliament to look into these sorts of problems. The situati.on is getting beyond a joke. It is getting beyond party politics. It is reaching a stage where all advanced countries like ours are finding that the problem of the cost of health care seems to be never-ending in its capacity to expand and to gobble up more and more of our resources. We could work out a better system. In my view, drugs that are necessary should be free. They are as vital a part of the management of illness as is visiting a doctor. Of course, in our view visits to doctors ought to be free also. For these reasons, among the many others that have been mentioned by the honourable member for Bonython (Dr Blewett), I certainly will support our move to oppose this clause when it (;omes up in the Committee stage.

Mr MacKELLAR (Warringah-Minister for Health) (10.34)-in reply-In replying to the honourable members who have spoken in this debate on the National Health Amendment Bill I do not want to say too much about the contribution of the honourable member for Bonython (Dr Blewett). He has been in the chamber for a long time tonight so I can excuse him for substituting some personal remarks for substance in his speech. I take up the point that the honourable member for Bonython, the honourable member for Petrie (Mr Hodges) and the honourable member for Maribyrnong (Dr Cass) have men-
tioned; that is, the cost of pharmaceutical prescriptions to holders of health care cards, the fact that they have been required, if they wish those prescriptions to be dispensed by a pharmacist, to pay the patient contribution.
There are two facts about this which have to be kept in mind. I hate to introduce the question of money but I am encouraged in this by the contribution of the honourable member for Maribyrnong because he frankly acknowledged that modern health care is a very expensive proposition. That is why I enjoy the contributions of the honourable member for Maribyrnong. I do not always agree with what he says; in fact, I seldom agree with what he says. He is a thoughtful member of the House who puts forward his views in a way which I think should be taken notice of by members on both sides of the House. I thank him for the way he presents these thoughts. Modern health care is a very expensive proposition. Of course, the cost of modern pharmaceuticals is a very expensive proposition. The fact remains that if all those who hold health care cards, or their dependants, were to be provided with the same sort of facilities' as pensioner health benefit card holders then the cost to the community would be considerable. Obviously, this is a factor which has to be taken into account.
The other fact of the matter which has not been
mentioned tonight is that holders of health care cards can receive free treatment at public hospitals. That, of course, includes the supply of pharmaceuticals at public hospitals. Rather than there being no opportunity for holders of health care cards to avail themselves of pharmaceuticals at a lower cost, in fact, that source of supply is available to them. I acknowledge the very real concern that has been shown by all members speaking here tonight about the cost of pharmaceuticals, particularly to people with chronic complaints and people with children who may all at the same time come down with some medical problem which requires a number of pharmaceutical prescriptions to be made for them. I acknowledge that. The

National Health Amendment Bill

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2637

Government, as I have mentioned in this House before, is seeking to look into this question in great depth. I hope that we will be able to come up with something in the future. I say that I hope we will be able to come up with something in the future because, as the honourable member for Maribyrnong has suggested, there is no modern country which has solved the questions of how to bring about a proper reward, if you like, for the manufacturers of pharmaceutical products and how to maintain the supply of pharmaceutical products to the general community at a cost that the individual and the community can afford.
I agree with the honourable member for Maribyrnong that people in Australia have a tendency to consume excessive amounts of drugs. In recent times a campaign was mounted in doctors' surgeries and in hospitals which put very clearly the fact that if one were not prescribed some drugs by the doctor this was not necessarily a bad thing but a good thing. Unfortunately some people feel that if they go to a doctor and he does not give them something to take they have somehow been cheated. We have to overcome that problem. I am not convinced, as is the honourable member for Maribyrnong, that salaried doctors are any better or worse prescribers of drugs than are fee-for-service doctors. I do not happen to have the same beliefs in this area as the honourable member for Maribyrnong has. Certainly, all of us who are connected with the application of health care policies-that includes everybody within the medical profession and the allied health professions-have a very real duty to educate the public to the fact that a reduction in the prescribing of drugs, rather than being a bad thing, is a very good thing. I think it is a sad reflection that so many people in hospitals these days are there as a result of misprescribing by the medical fraternity as a whole. That is not confined to Australia by any means; it is a common feature of medical practice in societies around the world.
Two other questions were raised, both of which are really outside the ambit of this Bill. Certainly, the one raised by the honourable member for Bonython in relation to students within Australia is outside the ambit of the Bill. I take on board what he said and will reply to it at the appropriate time. The matter of the design of pensioner health cards, which was raised by the honourable member for Petrie, comes within the responsibility of my colleague the Minister for Social Security (Senator Chaney); I will make sure that the honourable member's remarks are drawn to his attention. I have no doubt that he will be speaking to the honourable member for Petrie and availing

himself of that large reservoir of experience that is represented by the honourable member.
Question resolved in the affirmative.

Clause 3 (Interpretation).
Dr BLEWETT (Bonython) (10.43)-ln dealing with clause 3, I welcome first of all the sympathy of the Minister for Health (Mr MacKellar). He explained the superficialities of my speech as being due to the severe strain under which I have been suffering in the last several hours. In response, I say that I simply hope that in future the Government of which he is a member will get its legislative act together so that members of the Opposition will not be submitted to the kinds of strains and demands that we have had to put up with in the last 24 hours. At this hour of the evening I do not wish to deal in detail with the grounds for our opposition to clause 3. I expressed them quite clearly in my speech during the second reading debate. All I will do is summarise them briefly.
There are four reasons for our opposing this clause. First of all, the increase in the patient contribution has run very well ahead of inflation in the six years of this Government. I gave figures to establish that fact. Secondly, the result of this Government's policy has been that the patient contribution as a proportion of the average cost of pharmaceutical benefit items has again risen quite dramatically as a result of the user-pay policies of this Government in the pharmaceutical benefit field. Thirdly, I point out that the difficulties for families with multiple prescriptions, either as a result of a single medical incident or because there are numerous prescriptions for different members of a family, are compounded by this increase of 45c per prescription. The same compounding occurs for those who are chronically ill and who have to pay for prescriptions regularly. Finally, as I have pointed out, we object to this .clause because it simply underwrites and highlights the anomaly in relation to the health care card in that people with pensioner health benefitst cards are entitled to free pharmaceuticals but those with health care cards, which are based on much the same economic criteria, are not entitled to free pharmaceuticals. This increase of 45c will simply underwrite, exaggerate and stress that anomaly. For these reasons we are opposed to clause 3.
Question put:
That clause 3 be agreed to.

COMMONWEALTH GRANTS COMMISSION AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 27 October, on motion by MrMoore:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Mr WILLIS (Gellibrand) (10.54)-The purpose of the Commonwealth Grants Commission Amendment Bill, which was introduced only last night, is to amend the Commonwealth Grants Commission Act so as to increase the number of members of the Grants Commission. In 1977 the Grants Commission Act was amended so as to reduce the number of commissioners from between four and seven to between two and four. It was amended again in 1978 to allow the appointment of associate members, of whom three were to be appointed on the nomination of fhe State Premiers. The appointment of those associate members lapsed on 30 June this year. Now the Government want, to change the composition of the Commission yet again, this time by removing the upper limit on the number of commissioners so that the Commission can be composed of a chairman plus two or more permanent commissioners.
The background to this matter, which was not in any way explained by the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs (Mr Moore) in the whole of his three paragraph'second reading speech, is important if one is to understand what this Bill is about. As members of the House would be aware, the States have varying capacities to raise revenue, and the cost of providing services also varies widely between the States. Therefore, the Commonwealth allocates its tax sharing payments to the States in a way which takes account of these differences. The Grants Commission in the past has been given the task of determining these State tax sharing relativities. In 1978 the Commission was asked to determine whether any change was desirable in the State factors which are used to adjust State population as the first step in the process of calculating the percentage distribution among the States of the total tax-sharing entitlements. The Commission was asked to carry out the review on the basis of the principle that the respective payments to which the States are entitled should enable each State to provide government services at standards not appreciably different from the standard of the government

Commonwealth Grants Commission Bill

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2639

services provided by the other States without imposing taxes and charges at levels appreciably different from the levels of the taxes and charges imposed by the other States.
The Grants Commission, after careful analysis, submitted a comprehensive report to the Government on 9 June this year, just before the June Premiers Conference. The effect of the changes in the tax sharing relativities proposed by the Commission would have been to increase the proportions received by New South Wales, Victoria
and Queensfand and to reduce the proportions received by South Australia, Western Australia, and Tasmania. These increases and reductions were substantial. For the record I note that for New South Wales there was an increase of $116m, for Victoria an increase of $SSm, for Queensland an increase of $128m, for South Australia there was $77m less, for West Australia $160m less and for Tasmania $64m less. The States which would have lost as a result of the implementation of this recommendation protested strongly against acceptance of this report. The Commonwealth agreed not to implement the report immediately but to pay additional grants to New South Wales, Victoria and Queensland in the current financial year to compensate for the additional grants which they would not receive because of delay in the implementation of the Grants Commission recommendation and to resubmit the issue for further consideration by the Grants Commission; that is, the Commission was being asked to review its own report. It is principally for this purpose that the Bill now before the House has been introduced; that is, to give the Commission the number of members needed to conduct this important review.
However, the Government, in so doing, has adopted a different approach from that which it adopted in extending the membership of the Commission to enable the previous inquiry into relativities to be held. At that time it extended the membership of the Commission by providing for the States to nominate three so-called associate members on the basis of New South Wales and Victoria jointly nominating one member and the other four States jointly nominating two members. This ensured that the States would have on the inquiry people who they could be reasonably sure would represent their interests. However, their appointments expired on 30 June under the existing legislation. In the Bill now before the House, instead of retaining that basis of appointment by which the States nominate their members to the Commission, the legislation that provided for that form of appointment is being repealed and the Government is simply moving to

extend the number of permanent members of the Commission without any legislative guarantee that the States will have their representatives on the Commission for this crucial review.
The Minister, in his second reading speech, said
that it was the wish of the States that the Commission should have the same composition when conducting this review as it had for the initial report. He also claimed that the legislation would enable this to occur. This is so, in that it allows the Commonwealth to appoint to the Commission for this review the same additional personnel as were previously there for the initial report. However, they are no longer associate members. Furthermore, the legislation does not make it mandatory that the same persons be appointed or that persons who are nominated by the States be appointed. All we have is the Minister's word for this in the second reading speech.
There is no guarantee in the legislation that the States will get the same people for this review as they had for the initial report. The Government should have made clear in this debate what it intends to do. I guess it is not going to, given the way it operates in this House. It did not do so in the
second reading speech, but it should have made its intentions absolutely clear. Does it intend to ensure that the States will have the exactly the same personnel on the Commission for the purposes of the review as were on it for the initial report, or is that not the intention? The I\1inister certainly has not made it clear in his second readingspeech.
As I mentioned earlier, the speech the Minister made in introducing this Bill was an absolute contempt of Parliament. A three-paragraph speech does not describe the background to this Bill ina way which is intelligible to members or to the public at large. On 22 October the Minister issued a Press statement announcing the reference to the Grants Commission which was a far more extensive review of the situation than the one he gave to this House in his second reading speech. I think that shows an absolute contempt of this Parliament. By neglecting to describe the background to the Bill the Minister also managed to sloth over the fact that the Bill is long overdue. The Treasurer (Mr Howard) announced on 21 June, that is, over four months ago, that the Grants Commission would be asked to produce a further report on its assessment of State relativities and to present this further report early in 1982. However, the matter has only now been referred to the Grants Commission, four months after the Treasurer's announcement. This totally unexplained delay in making the reference will now make it impossible for the Commission to report

2640 REPRESENTATIVES 28 October 1981

Commonwealth Grants Commission Bill

early in 1982, as agreed between the Commonwealth and the States. The Commission is now being asked to report by 31 May 1982. The Commission will need every day of the seven months until that date adequately to prepare its report.
The States and the Commonwealth will have too little time, after receiving the report, to evaluate it carefully before it has to be discussed at the June 1982 Premiers Conference. As a result, conflict between the States and the Commonwealth is likely to occur. The States which are adversely affected by the report which eventually, will be brought down by the Commission are bound to argue that it has been a rush job and therefore has been inadequately done, and that they have been hardly done by in the report. This conflict will have been caused by the Commonwealth's totally unexplained failure to expedite the reference to the Commission.
Why has there been this delay? We have been told absolutely nothing by the Government. Let me make some assessment as to why I think there has been a delay. I believe the delay has been brought about because of the Government's absolute inability to resolve a number of factors. As I have said, those factors, are, firstly, that some States-that is, those States which had been well treated by the initial report-did not want a review. If there was to be a review they wanted the terms of reference to be restricted to issues that could be determined quickly. For instance, New South Wales argued that the .review should be confined to four elements. The first element was any new evidence or new material provided by any State or the Commonwealth. The second was the calculation errors reported by any State or the Commonwealth. The third referred to comments by any State or the Commonwealth on the methodology adopted by the Commission in its initial report, and the fourth to any revised assessment method not previously proposed by the Commission.
The New South Wales Government considered that such terms of reference would enable the review to be relatively short. That is, it would not require the volume of submissions of the orginall report nor would it take the time of that report. That those submissions have been rejected means that the review will take much longer to conduct than would otherwise have been the case.
The second reason for delay in establishing the terms of reference is possibly that some States did not want reference to be made to the data for 1980-81. The previous inquiry into relativities was based on data for the three-year period from 1977-78 to 1979-80. If the Commission were now

to be required to look at data, so far as it is available, for 1980-81 then clearly the length of the review would be considerably extended because it would have to go over a whole lot of new material which had not been available to it for the previous report. However, the Commonwealth has seen fit to require that the Commission have regard to 1980-81 data. In the reference that has been sent to the Grants Commission it has been required particularly to have regard to such data. That means that the Government has acted against the wishes of at least some of the States in this regard and the Grants Commission will have to prepare a more extensive report than would otherwise have
been the case.
The third reason (or delay, and probably the most important, is the decision of the Commonwealth at the Premiers Conference in May this year to revise the form of health payments to the States. As honourable members are well aware, up until this financial year payments to the States in repect of hospital cost-sharing agreements, the school dental program and the community health program had been by way of specific payment to the States. However, at the Premiers Conference in the middle of this year the Government decided to put all those payments into general revenue payments but have them specially itemised within that category. The question then arose in relation to this review by the Grants Commission of the report on State relativities as to whether relative health needs in the various States should also be assessed by the Commission in conducting the review. I understand that this question has been the subject of intensive discussion within the Government and between the Commonwealth and the States. Finally, it has been decided to include this item in the terms of reference for the Grants Commission in conducting its review.
Thus, because of the Government's failure to deal expeditiously with these three factors-thilt is, the desire of some States for a limited review, the question of whether to include 1980-81 data, and whether to take into account relative health needs-not only has there been an inordinate delay in the Government getting around to establishing the terms of reference and conveying that reference to the Grants Commission, but almost certainly there will be great difficulty in the Grants Commission meeting the timetable established for it by the Government, that is, to conclude the review by 31 May next year.
The Government has wasted four whole months since it decided to have the Grants Commission conduct its review. It has taken that long to get the reference to the Commission and to provide it with the personnel needed to establish the

Commonwealth Grants Commission Bill

28 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2641

review. Of course, the provision of personnel is only now occurring, with the legislation before the House. As I indicated earlier, this is likely to mean that not only will the Grants Commission not have sufficient time to do its job properly, but also that the States adversely affected by its final report will seize on this point as a basis to challenge the results. It also means that with the report not being available until the end of May there will be little time for the States and the Commonwealth to discuss it adequately before decisions have to be made at the Premiers Conference in the middle of the year on the relative payments to the States for 1982-83. The four months delay in making this reference to the Grants Commission is utterly unforgiveable and is indicative of a Government which is quite incapable of efficient management of the affairs of government.
Another purpose of the Bill in enlarging the personnel of the Commission is to enable it to undertake another inquiry into the finances of the Australian Capital Territory. The Opposition welcomes the enlargement of the Commission for this purpose. Such an inquiry is a necessary precondition for the preparation of proposals for self government in the Australian Capital Territory. However, this is another inquiry which has been extraordinarily delayed by the Government. I understand that the Minister for the Capital Territory (Mr Hodgman) has been talking about this reference for a whole year, yet up until now we have had no reference to the Grants Commission for such an inquiry. I understand no reference has yet been made, but at least the personnel of the Grants Commission will be expanded so that such an inquiry can be undertaken. Obviously, this again is something which should have been done a long time ago if we were to have a proper inquiry into the Australian Capital Territory and if the proposals for a move towards self-governmentwhich the Minister for the Capital Territory says he is in favour of-were to be expedited. The Opposition, despite its regret ai the Government's delay in bringing this Bill before the House and taking other decisions in relation to it, does not oppose the legislation.
Question resolved in the affirmative.
Bill read a second time.
Third Reading
Leave granted for third reading to be moved forthwith.
Bill (on motion by Mr Wilson) read a third time.

Overseas Investment in Australia: Intra-corporate Trade
(Question No. 2616)
Mr Lionel Bowen asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 10 June 1981:
(I) Has his allention been drawn to the Federal Treasury economic paper Overseas Investment in Australia which states that imports by Australian branches and subsidiaries
from their related companies overseas accounted for about t
of total Australian imports c.iJ.
(2) If so, is it a fact that the United Nations Economic and Social Council Commission on Transnational Corporations, in their publication Transnational Corporations in World Development-a Re-examination (1978) defines this type of international trade as 'intra-firm' or 'intra-corporate' trade and warns that this type of international trade has considerable and often deleterious effects on national policy options in taxation, industry, exchange rates, resources, trade, technology, research and development and the distribution of national income and wealth.
(3) Using the United Nations definition of intra-corporate trade what was the (a) total value of exports, (b) value of intra-corporate exports and (c) proportion of the former constituted by the latter, in percentage terms for each year from 1970-71 to 1980-81 in 1980-81 values.
(4) What was the (a) total value of imports, (b) value of intra-corporate imports and (c) proportion of the former constituted by the latter in the same terms and for the same years as in part (3).
(5) What are the figures for parts (3) and (4) broken down into agricultural, mineral, energy, manufactured goods, services and finance sectors.
(6) What was the (a) total value of the export of each of the major mineral products, (b) value of intra-corporate

export of each of the major mineral products and (c) proportion of the former constituted by the latter in the same terms and for the same years as in part (3).
(7) What was the (a) total value of the (i) export and (ii) import of each of the major Australian Statistical Industry Code manufacturing classifications, (b) value of intracorporate (i) exports and (ii) imports of each of the classifications and (c) proportion of each of the former constituted by each.of the latter in the same terms and for the same years as in part (3).
(8) Is he able to state what the figures were in parls (3) and (4) for each (a) country of origin of imports and (b) country of destination of exports.
(9) What was the value and proportion of the value of intra-corporate trade in parts (3), (4), (5), (6) and (7) contributed by the 6 largest corporations involv~d expressed in the same terms and for the same years as in part (3).
(10) Is he able to estimate the overall quantity of tax avoidance and evasion involved in transfer pricing in each year from 1970-71 to 1980-81 in 1980-81 dollar values.
(II) How many companies have been investigated by the Australian Taxation Commission under section 136 of the Income Tax Assessment Act in the last 10 years and what were the results of these investigations, including the names of tax haven countries involved in each case.
(12) Taking into account the results of the answers to parts (3), (4), (5), (6) and (7) is he able also to estimate the effect on the measurement of productivity using the Australian Council of Trade Unions and the Confederation of Australian Industry preferred measure of productivity and, accordingly, adjustments to these figures from 1970-71 to 1980-81.
(13) Taking into account the answers to parts (3) and (4), is he able to also estimate the effects of transfer pricing (under-valuing exports and over-valuing imports) on balance of payments, balance of trade, terms of trade and exchange

Tertiary Graduates
(Question No, 2679)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 18 August 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to predictions that Australia will have a national shortage of tertiary graduates this decade.
(2) If so, has his Department determined the areas where shortages are expected to occur; if so, what are the identified areas.
(3) Is the government satisfied that a shortfall would not impede (a) university research, (b) national research and (c) the implementation of high technology.
(4) What steps will be taken to ensure that a shortfall does not occur.
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) I am not aware of the source of the predictions referred to by the honourable member. The Commonwealth Tertiary Education Commission has estimated, in Volume I, Part I, of its 'Report for 1982-84 Triennium', that by 1990 there will be about 1,200,000 graduates from universities and CAEs, or about 7 per cent of the population. This compares with about 660,000, or 4.7 per cent of the population in 1976. The Commission has not predicted that there will be a national shortage of tertiary graduates by the end of the decade.
(2) and (3) The Government looks to the Commonwealth Tertiary Education Commission to provide ongoing advice on the relationship of manpower supply to demand in areas which are critical for education planning. In Volume I of its 'Report for 1982-84 Triennium' the Commission reviewed supply of and demand for technological manpower in a number of key areas. In particular, it proposed that there should be a reallocation of resources within the advanced education sector from teacher education to the technological and business studies. The Government has supported this reallocation, and provided increased support to university research through its initiative for the establishment of Research Centres of Excellence. The Government has IIlso demonstrated its commitment to capital and quality improvement programs in T AFE designed to enhance the capacity of TAFE systems to meet increasing demands for skilled manpower.
(4) The Government will continue to seek advice from the Commission on these issues on a regular basis.

2,4,5-T
(Question No, 2735)
Mr Holding asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 19 August 1981:
(I) What Commonwealth or State statutory requirements exist for controlling the levels of TCDD (dioxin), in such materials as herbicides containing 2,4,5-T and bacteriocidal preparations containing hexachlorophene, for example, Phisohex, during manufacture, use and disposal.
(2) What are the levels of TCDD specified in the legislation.
(3) When was the legislation assented to, in each case, and what formal mechanisms exist for its enforcement.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The Commonwealth has no statutory requirements for controlling the levels of the dioxin TCDD in materials such as 2,4,5-T and bacterial preparations containing hexachlorophene during manufacture, use and disposal, since such matters are the constitutional responsibility of the States.
Hexachlorophene, when imported into Australia for use in the manufacture of pharmaceutical preparations such as Phisohex, is required to meet the standard for hexachlorophene laid down in the British Pha'rmacopoeia 1980. There is no reference in this standard to 11 level of dioxin content. However the British Pharmacopoeia Commission proposes a limit of 15 parts per billion (thousand million) for dioxin in hexachlorophene and it is likely that this will be adopted in Australia.
(2) and (3) The National Health and Medical Research Council (NH & MRC) has undertaken a review of TCDD contamination of both materials. Whilst hexachlorophene is still under consideration the NH & MRC has recommended since 1975 that 2,4,5-T containing more than 0.1 parts per million TCDD should not be permitted for use as a herbicide in Australia. This recommendation subsequently was incorporated into Standards Association of Australia, Australian Standard AS II 75-1976 'Herbicides of the Phenoxyacetic Acid Type'.
With regard to 2,4,5-T products, the following State legislation exists:
Relevant sections of the Australian Standard were incorporated into the Victorian Agricultural Chemicals Act, 1958, on 5 July 1977. In Western Australia, 2,4,5-T is manufactured under licence under the Poisoris Act (proclaimed I July 1965) and its formulations are registered under the Pesticides Regulations (Gazetted 11 April 1956).
In New South Wales, products containing 2,4,5,-T are currently controlled under the Pest Destroyers Act, 1945. This Act is being replaced by the Pesticides Act, 1978, which requires that 2,4,5-T complies with the above mentioned Australian Standard. SAA 1175-1976 is also recognised in Queensland.
In Tasmania, the level of TCDD in herbicides containing 2,4,5-T is controlled under the Poisons Act 1971 to a level ofless than 0.1 ppm.
There are no statutory requirements in South Australia for TCDD in 2,4,5-T but routine monitoring shows levels well below 0.1 ppm.

(a) As average weekly hours worked over the period by part-time workers have been approximately 40 per cent of the aver-

Statutory Authorities under the Minister for Defence
(Question No. 2847)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 26 August 1981:
(I) Further to the Review of Commonwealth Functions which called for Ministers to scrutinise all authorities within their portfolios (Hansard, 30 April 1981, page 1850) which statutory authorities within the Minister's portfolio are to be (a) terminated, (b) continued and (c) reviewed in depth.

Pensioners: Compensation for Medical Insurance Cover

(Question No. 3129)

Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Health,

upon notice, on 16 September 1981:

.

Is it a fact that a pensioner, whose annual income is below
the tax-free threshold and who opts for private hospital and medical insurance cover, is not entitled to receive financial compensation for this expenditure in lieu of the 32 cents in the
dollar tax rebate which normal tax-payers are entitled to . receive.

Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

Yes. Persons whose annual income is below the taxation threshold and are thus not liable to pay income tax, are not entitled to a rebate of tax. The amount of rebate allowable to
a taxpayer cannot exceed the tax otherwise payable.

I must emphasise that the rebate applies only in respect of
basic levels of insurance. There is no reas~m at all for, eligible
pensioners to take basic medical insurance for themselves or their dependants. This is because they are entitled to basic medical insurance cover provided to them by the Common-
wealth on the basis of no payment by them for bulk-billed services or a maximum payment of $5 per item of service if their doctors do not bulk-bill. Eligible pensioners and their depend-
ants are also entitled to free hospitalisation, but if they elect to take private hospital cover only they still may have combined medical and hospital cover at a rate lower than taxpayer who have the benefit of the rebate.

Armed Services: Average Age of Retirement
(Question No. 3138)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 16 September 1981:
What was the average age of retirement for (a) officers and (b) other ranks in the 3 branches of the Armed Services in (i) 1978, (ii) 1979 and (iii) 1980.
(2) What was the percentage of (a) officers and (b) other ranks retiring from the 3 services with periods of service of (i) up to 15 years, (ii) 15-25 years and (iii) more than 25 years.
Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The term 'retirement' applies specifically, in the Defence Force, to separations from the Services at the statutory retiring age. It does not apply to separations from the Services before reaching retiring age of members who are eligible for 'retirement pay' having served 20 or more years. Nor does it include a third category which comprises those members who leave the Services after serving for relatively shorter periods an~ do not receive 'retirement pay'.
It has been assumed that the question is not directed, literally, to 'retirements' as it applies in the Defence Force but to the broader category of separations which includes 'retirements' as well as the categories noted above.
Information readily available in the form requested relates to those members who left the Services with a pension entitlement, ie., who reached statutory retiring age during the three most recent financial years, together with those who separated from the Services after serving at least 20 years. New South Wales Medical Benefits Fund
(Question No. 3183)
Dr Blewett asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 22 September 1981:
(I) Did the New South Wales Medical Benefits Fund (MBF) have an operating deficit of$54m in 1977-78 and was this transformed into a $22m surplus the following year.
(2) Did it register an improvement in its assets position from $76m to $95m during the same period.
(3) Can this improvement be attributed solely to the reintroduction of a Government 40 per cent medical benefits subsidy, or can other reasons be advanced for the change; if so, what are the details.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
('1) According to relevant reports on the Operations of the Registered Medical Benefits and Hospital Benefits Organizations, the Medical Benefits Fund of Australia Limited (New

South Wales) recorded a combined operating deficit of $55m in 1977-78, and a combined operating surplus of $23m in 1978-79.
(2) The report also indicates that this organization increased its assets position from $76m at 30 June 1978 to $96m at 30 June 1979.
(3) No, the improvement would not have been attributed solely to the reintroduction of a Government 40 per cent medical benefits subsidy. The following factors would have been largely instrumental in bringing about the improved performance of MBF in 1978-79:
(a) In the light of continuing deficits in both the medical fund and the hospital fund, approval was given for the organization to increase its contribution rates to both funds from I April 1978. The medical basic rate was increased by 34 per cent and the hospital basic rate by 17 percent.
(b) Revised health insurance arrangements from I November 1978 provided for Commonwealth benefits to be payable at 40 per cent of the Schedule fee, with a maximum payment of $20 for anyone service. This brought about savings in fund benefit for the medical fund. However, the savings on the basic table were partly offset by a reduction of 44 per cent in the contribution rate from I November 1978. There were no changes to the hospital fund rates at that time.
(c) With the introduction of the revised arrangements from 1 November 1978, MBF also restructured its medical fund, introducing two new tables that were designed to operate at a surplus.

Australian Industrial Participation in Offsets
(Question No. 3272)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 13 October 1981:
(I) What level of Australian industrial participation is guaranteed to Australian industry in respect of tenders of (a) British Leyland and (b) Mercedes Benz if granted the Army contract for 4 tonne trucks.
(2) Will all or part of the Australian industrial participation be in offsets rather than being part of the production of the chosen truck.
Mr KilIen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) (a) British Leyland was not a tenderer. A tender from Leyland Motor Corporation of Australia was passed over

when vehicles were selected for evaluation in 1977. Consequently, firm levels of participation are not available.
(b) I recently announced the selection of Mercedes-Benz Australia Ltd as the supplier of Army's 4 tonne trucks subject to the negotiation of a satisfactory contract. The company has offered a firm undertaking to provide work for Australian industry equal to the value of the contract awarded to it.
(2) Australian content valued at some $25m will be incorporated in the vehicles. In addition, the Australian automotive component industry will participate in offset orders worth at least $33m for automotive-related goods and services to be placed by the German company, Daimler-Benz AG.
South Africa: Enriched Uranium
Mr Malcolm Fraser-On 14 October 1981 (Hansard, page 1969) the honourable member for Kingsford-Smith (Mr Lionel Bowen) asked me a question, without notice, concerning reports that the United States Government is about to resume supply of enriched uranium to the South African Government.
The following is in answer to the honourable member's question:
The Government is aware of reports that South Africa delivered uranium oxide feedstock to the United States in August for enrichment to fuel a nuclear power station in South Africa. Reports indicate that the low-enriched'uranium product remains under storage in the United States.
It is my understanding that United States export policy with regard to South Africa has not changed. The United States is attempting to convince South Africa to ratify the Non-Proliferation Treaty (!'IPT) and to accept full-scope IAEA safeguards. In the absence of these steps I further understand that supply of enriched material from the United States to South Africa will not be possible.
Australia's own position on the question of nuclear trade is well-known. The Government requires that as a minimum nuclear trade with non-nuclear weapon states should only be conducted with countries which have adhered to the NPT and which accept IAEA safeguards on all their nuclear activities.
Government policy does not permit nuclear dealings between Australia and South Africa. Furthermore in 1980 Australia slipported UNGA Resolution 35/206B which calls for the cessation of all forms of nuclear collaboration with South Africa.

Temporary Resident Visas
(Question No. 2637)
Mr Young asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 10 June 1981:
(I) How many persons were allowed into Australia on temporary resident visas and in what categories (e.g. teachers, domestic servants, entertainers) for each year since 1978.
(2) For each category how many relatives, dependants or others came to Australia with persons admitted on temporary resident visas for each of the same years.
(3) For how long are these visas valid and what is the maximum-period that they can be renewed.
(4) How many of the persons in each category admitted to Australia on a temporary resident visa have applied for permanent residency for each year since 1978.

(5) How many of the family members or dependants admitted to Australia on a temporary resident visa have applied for
permanent residency for each year since 1978.

'(6) What methods are there to ascertain that the skills of the people admitted on a temporary resident visa are in shortage in Australia.

(7) What were the countries of residence of applicants for temporary resident visas in each category since 1978.

(8) What was the average stay in Australia of persons
admitted with temporary resident visas in each category, from each country, each year since 1978.

(9) How many persons admitted with temporary resident visas in each category left Australia since 1978.

(10) What are the methods ofrecruitment of persons with temporary resident visas in each category and in each country.

(II) What are the methods of selection for persons admit-

ted to Australia on temporary resident visas.

.

Mr Macphee~The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(\) See Table A.

(2) See Table A for total figures. Departmental statistics do not provide a segregation into individual categories.

(3) Figures are not available by category by period of stay.
Usually temporary residents are eligible to remain for periods up to 4 years.

(4) See Table B which provides figures for broad categories of occupation skills.

(5) Statistics previously in the form requested are not available. Table B provides figures on dependants.

(6) The Department of Employment and Youth Affairs is
consulted as necessary on the availability of appropriate personnel on the local labour market.

(7) Statistics are not kept of applicants by country of residence. However details of the number of applications lodged
in each country are kept. See Table C.

(8) and (9) This information is not available from the statistical records maintained by the Department. .

(\ 0) There is no active recruitment by this Department of temporary residents in any category. It is the sponsor who
finds suitable overseas personnel where they are not available locally.

(\ I) Once a person overseas has been sponsored for
temporary entry from within Australia and it has been established that no suitable person is availl\ble locally, then provided the nominee is able. to meet normal requirements and
has not come under adverse notice, avisa is issued.
